The Category of Mixed Benefit-Harm Actions
Definition of the sixth category and the basis for guardian permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the category of actions that oscillate between benefit and harm (al-dāʾir bayn al-ḍarar wa al-nafʿ) and explain why a minor requires guardian permission for such contracts.

Core Idea
Actions with mixed potential benefit and harm require guardian permission because the minor cannot assess consequences.

In this lesson, we explore the sixth category of actions according to the Hanafi school: those that involve both possible benefit and harm, such as sales and leases. The text explains that minors are not entrusted with these contracts due to their inability to foresee outcomes; instead, a guardian's oversight provides the necessary judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category and its examples
Read the definition: 'what oscillates between harm and benefit' includes sale, hire, and other commutative contracts. Understand that these actions have both potential benefit (profit) and potential harm (loss of property or body).
Commutative contracts (muʿāwaḍāt) are contracts of exchange where each party gives something.
2
Understand the minor's incapacity
The text states that the minor is deficient in knowing consequences (ṣabī qāṣir ʿan maʿrifat al-ʿawāqib). Therefore, such contracts are not entrusted to him out of mercy, lest he fall into harm.
This is a legal incapacity (ḥajr) based on lack of discernment.
3
State the role of guardian permission
The guardian, being more compassionate, provides oversight. When the guardian's opinion is added (bi-inḍimām raʾy hādhā al-walī), the possibility of harm is repelled, and the minor may validly execute these contracts.
The term 'with him' (maʿahu) indicates the contract is done with the guardian's involvement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(والسادس) وهو الدائر بين الضرر والنفع كالبيع والإجارة وغيرهما من المعاوضات، ففيها نفع لاحتمالها الاسترباح مشوب باحتمال ضرر لاحتمال خسارة المال أو البدن والصبي قاصر عن معرفة العواقب، فلم تفوض إليه هذه لعقود مرحمة له لئلا يقع في ضرر، بل أولى عليه من هو أشفق به فبانضمام رأي هذا الولي يندفع ذلك الاحتمال) من الضرر (فيملك) هذه العقود (معه ثم عند الإمام أبي حنيفة لما انجبر (القصور الذي كان في الصبي من تصرفاته (بالإذن) الصادر من الولي (كان) كالبالغ في نفاذ التصرفات (فيملك) العقود (بغبن فاحش مع الأجانب باتفاق الروايات كالبالغ (و) مع (الولي في رواية) وفي أخرى لا يملك، لأن الولي حينئذ متهم في الإذن لجواز أن إذنه كان خداعاً منه لأخذ ماله ولا كذلك في أجنبي وعندهما لا يجوز العقود مع الغبن الفاحش (وقولهما أظهر) لأن الإذن إنما اعتبر شرعاً ليأمن عن الضرر، فلما عقد الغبن علم أن إذنه لم يقع في محله والعذر له أن الإذن مع مظنة عدم الضرر وتخلف الحكمة المظنة لا يوجب عدم العلة كسفر الملك المرفه مع عن الحكمة يثبت الترخص فتدبر، والله أعلم بأحكامه
(The sixth) is what oscillates between harm and benefit, like sale, hire, and other commutative contracts. In them there is benefit due to the possibility of profit, mixed with the possibility of harm due to potential loss of property or body. The minor is deficient in knowing consequences, so these contracts were not entrusted to him out of mercy, lest he fall into harm. Rather, someone more compassionate is put in charge, so with the addition of the guardian's opinion, that possibility [of harm] is repelled. Thus, he may perform these contracts with him [the guardian]. Then, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, once the deficiency in the minor's actions is compensated by the guardian's permission, he becomes like an adult in the validity of his transactions, so he may execute contracts with gross fraud (ghabn fāḥish) with strangers by unanimous narrations, like an adult. With the guardian, there is one narration that he may, and another that he may not, because the guardian is then accused in his permission, as it might be a deception to take his property, unlike a stranger. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contracts with gross fraud are not valid. Their statement is more apparent, because permission was legislated to ensure safety from harm. When fraud occurs, it is known that the permission was not in its proper place. The excuse [for Abu Hanifa] is that permission is based on an assumption of no harm, and the non-realization of the assumed wisdom does not negate the cause, like the case of a traveling prosperous king, despite the wisdom [of travel hardship] being absent, concessions still apply. So ponder. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage examines the sixth category: actions that have both potential benefit and harm, such as sales and leases. The minor cannot perform these alone because of his inability to evaluate consequences; a guardian's permission is required. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that once the guardian permits, the minor's transactions are valid even if there is excessive fraud (ghabn fāḥish) when dealing with strangers, and it is unanimous in the narrations. However, when dealing with the guardian himself, there is a difference: in one narration it is valid, in another not, due to potential collusion. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) maintain that contracts with excessive fraud are invalid altogether, because permission aims to prevent harm, and fraud indicates the permission was misplaced. The text notes that the companions' view is 'more apparent,' but offers an analogy to excuse Abu Hanifa's position: just as a king on a comfortable journey still qualifies for travel concessions even though the hardship is absent, the cause (permission) remains effective despite the specific wisdom not being realized. The reader is to ponder this distinction.

Key Terms
الدائر بين الضرر والنفع (al-dāʾir bayn al-ḍarar wa al-nafʿ): Actions that involve both potential harm and benefit, like commercial contracts.
المعاوضات (al-muʿāwaḍāt): Commutative contracts of exchange, e.g., sale, hire.
الولي (al-walī): Guardian, typically the father or someone appointed to manage the minor's affairs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why are contracts like sale and hire considered 'dāʾir bayn al-ḍarar wa al-nafʿ'?
A) They involve both profit and loss possibilities
B) They are always harmful to minors
C) They are purely beneficial for the guardian
D) They require two witnesses

Key Takeaway
The category of mixed benefit-harm actions requires guardian permission for minors, as they cannot evaluate consequences.

The Dispute Over Excessive Fraud in Minor's Contracts
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ghabn fāḥish with reasoning and tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling and that of the two companions regarding a minor's contract involving excessive fraud (ghabn fāḥish), and evaluate the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
Permission does not automatically validate a contract with excessive fraud because the guardian's oversight aims to prevent harm.

The previous lesson established that a minor can contract with guardian permission. But what if the contract involves excessive fraud? Here, the Hanafi school is divided. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that permission makes the minor like an adult, covering even fraudulent deals, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that such contracts are invalid because the purpose of permission is to avoid harm.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Imam Abu Hanifa)
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that when the guardian gives permission, the minor's deficiency is compensated, and he becomes like an adult. Therefore, even if the contract involves excessive fraud (ghabn fāḥish), it is valid. This is unanimous in narrations when the contract is with a stranger, but with the guardian himself, there is a difference in narration.
The text says 'fayamliku bi-ghabn fāḥish maʿa al-ajānib bi-ittifāq al-riwāyāt'.
2
Identify the opposing view (the two companions)
The two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, say that contracts with excessive fraud are not valid at all, whether with stranger or guardian, because permission was legislated to ensure safety from harm. When fraud occurs, it shows that the permission was misplaced.
The text says 'ʿindahumā lā yajūz al-ʿuqūd maʿa al-ghabn al-fāḥish'.
3
Analyze the proofs on each side
Abu Hanifa's proof is an analogy: permission compensates deficiency, so the minor is like an adult, and an adult's contract with ghabn fāḥish is valid under certain conditions. The companions' proof is purpose-based: the purpose of permission is to avoid harm; ghabn fāḥish indicates failure. The text provides an excuse for Abu Hanifa using the analogy of a traveling king who receives concessions even though he feels no hardship.
The analogy: cause (ʿilla) remains effective even if the specific wisdom (ḥikmah) is absent.
4
Understand the text's tarjih (preference)
The text states that the statement of the two companions is 'more apparent' (qawluhumā aẓhar). This indicates that the preferable view for practice is that contracts with ghabn fāḥish are invalid, though Abu Hanifa's view is defended with a rational analogy.
Tarjih does not mean rejection of Abu Hanifa's view; it means the companions' view is stronger in evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(والسادس) وهو الدائر بين الضرر والنفع كالبيع والإجارة وغيرهما من المعاوضات، ففيها نفع لاحتمالها الاسترباح مشوب باحتمال ضرر لاحتمال خسارة المال أو البدن والصبي قاصر عن معرفة العواقب، فلم تفوض إليه هذه لعقود مرحمة له لئلا يقع في ضرر، بل أولى عليه من هو أشفق به فبانضمام رأي هذا الولي يندفع ذلك الاحتمال) من الضرر (فيملك) هذه العقود (معه ثم عند الإمام أبي حنيفة لما انجبر (القصور الذي كان في الصبي من تصرفاته (بالإذن) الصادر من الولي (كان) كالبالغ في نفاذ التصرفات (فيملك) العقود (بغبن فاحش مع الأجانب باتفاق الروايات كالبالغ (و) مع (الولي في رواية) وفي أخرى لا يملك، لأن الولي حينئذ متهم في الإذن لجواز أن إذنه كان خداعاً منه لأخذ ماله ولا كذلك في أجنبي وعندهما لا يجوز العقود مع الغبن الفاحش (وقولهما أظهر) لأن الإذن إنما اعتبر شرعاً ليأمن عن الضرر، فلما عقد الغبن علم أن إذنه لم يقع في محله والعذر له أن الإذن مع مظنة عدم الضرر وتخلف الحكمة المظنة لا يوجب عدم العلة كسفر الملك المرفه مع عن الحكمة يثبت الترخص فتدبر، والله أعلم بأحكامه
(The sixth) is what oscillates between harm and benefit, like sale, hire, and other commutative contracts. In them there is benefit due to the possibility of profit, mixed with the possibility of harm due to potential loss of property or body. The minor is deficient in knowing consequences, so these contracts were not entrusted to him out of mercy, lest he fall into harm. Rather, someone more compassionate is put in charge, so with the addition of the guardian's opinion, that possibility [of harm] is repelled. Thus, he may perform these contracts with him [the guardian]. Then, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, once the deficiency in the minor's actions is compensated by the guardian's permission, he becomes like an adult in the validity of his transactions, so he may execute contracts with gross fraud (ghabn fāḥish) with strangers by unanimous narrations, like an adult. With the guardian, there is one narration that he may, and another that he may not, because the guardian is then accused in his permission, as it might be a deception to take his property, unlike a stranger. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contracts with gross fraud are not valid. Their statement is more apparent, because permission was legislated to ensure safety from harm. When fraud occurs, it is known that the permission was not in its proper place. The excuse [for Abu Hanifa] is that permission is based on an assumption of no harm, and the non-realization of the assumed wisdom does not negate the cause, like the case of a traveling prosperous king, despite the wisdom [of travel hardship] being absent, concessions still apply. So ponder. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage examines the sixth category: actions that have both potential benefit and harm, such as sales and leases. The minor cannot perform these alone because of his inability to evaluate consequences; a guardian's permission is required. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that once the guardian permits, the minor's transactions are valid even if there is excessive fraud (ghabn fāḥish) when dealing with strangers, and it is unanimous in the narrations. However, when dealing with the guardian himself, there is a difference: in one narration it is valid, in another not, due to potential collusion. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) maintain that contracts with excessive fraud are invalid altogether, because permission aims to prevent harm, and fraud indicates the permission was misplaced. The text notes that the companions' view is 'more apparent,' but offers an analogy to excuse Abu Hanifa's position: just as a king on a comfortable journey still qualifies for travel concessions even though the hardship is absent, the cause (permission) remains effective despite the specific wisdom not being realized. The reader is to ponder this distinction.

Key Terms
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghabn al-fāḥish): Excessive fraud or price disparity in a transaction.
السفر المرفه (al-safar al-muraffah): Comfortable travel where hardship is minimal.
مظنة (maẓinnah): Assumption or indication; something that is likely but not certain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Valid with strangers, invalid with guardian
B) Invalid in all cases
C) Valid in all cases
D) Valid only if the minor is orphaned

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a minor's contract with excessive fraud is invalid according to the preferred view (the two companions), because permission aims to prevent harm.

The First Impediment: Ignorance of the Disbeliever
Definition and rulings on impediment of disbelief affecting legal capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define jahl al-kāfir and explain its effect on dafʿ al-khiṭāb, applying the Hanafi position on Zoroastrian mahram marriages.

Core Idea
The disbeliever's willful ignorance blocks the address of revelation, making their actions legally effective until a Muslim judge intervenes.

This passage from Fawātiḥ al-Raḥamūt introduces the first acquired impediment to legal capacity: the ignorance of the disbeliever. Unlike ordinary ignorance, this is a willful rejection of clear truth, and it has unique legal consequences in Hanafi uṣūl al-fiqh. The key dispute is whether this ignorance prevents the address of revelation from applying altogether (Abu Hanīfa) or only excuses punishment while the original ruling remains (the two companions).

Guided Path
1
Identify the impediment type and its classification
Recognize that the impediment is acquired (muktasabah) and is the first type of ignorance mentioned by the author.
The author cites the category 'acquired impediments'.
2
Understand the definition of jahl al-kāfir
It is ignorance arising from intellectual stubbornness (mukābarah) and abandoning a decisive proof as clear as the sun.
This is not ordinary ignorance; it is a deliberate rejection.
3
Grasp the concept of dafʿ al-khiṭāb
According to Abū Ḥanīfa, such ignorance blocks the very address of revelation, so rulings based on that address do not apply.
Contrast with al-Shāfiʿī who holds the address is universal.
4
Contrast the positions of Abū Ḥanīfa and the Ṣāḥibayn
Compare the validity of Zoroastrian mahram marriages and the resulting legal effects.
The Ṣāḥibayn hold the original prohibition remains; Abū Ḥanīfa considers the address blocked.
5
Determine the practical thamarah and preferred view
State the practical consequence of the ikhtilāf and identify which view the author considers stronger.
The text says 'hūwa al-ashbah' indicating the Ṣāḥibayn's view is more correct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ها هنا عوارض على الأهلية ذكرها مشايخنا الكرام، والمصنف أهمل الأكثر منها، وأشار إلى البعض في الجملة، وأنا أذكرها لشدة الحاجة في استخراج الأحكام إلى معرفتها . العوارض المعترضة على الأهلية سماوية ومكتسبة، أما المكتسبة فمنها الجهل، وهو على أنواع: الأول : الجهل الذي يكون من مكابرة العقل وترك البرهان القاطع الظاهر أشد ظهوراً من ظهور الشمس على نصف النهار، وهو جهل الكافر لا يكون عذراً بحال، بل يؤخذ به في الدنيا بالإذلال بالقتل والنهب والاسترقاق أو أخذ الجزية وبعد قبولهم تكون حجتهم دافعة للتعرض بما فعلوا بشرط أن يكون في دينهم الباطل جائزاً لا كالربا فإنه محرم في الأديان كلها بالاتفاق، فلا يحد شاربهم ودافعة للخطاب أيضاً عندنا خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله، كأن الخطاب النازل لم يتوجه فلم يسقط، تقوم الحمر في حقهم فيضمن بالاتلاف وينفذ نكاح المجوس من المحارم فلا يفسخ إلا بترافعهما إلينا، ويثبت نسب الأولاد منها، ويجبر على إعطاء النفقة والمهر ويصير محصناً بالوطء إذا أسلم بعده ، وقالا : لا ينفذ ويفسخ جبراً، ولا يثبت النسب ولا نفقة ولا مهر ولا إحصان لأن ديانتهم وإن منعت من توجه الخطاب لكن لا تثبت حكماً جديداً، بل يبقى الحكم الأصلي، والحكم الأصلي في المحارم الحرمة، فتبقى كما كانت في الربا، وهو الأشبه
Then here there are impediments to capacity that our esteemed scholars have mentioned. The author omitted most of them and only indicated a few in summary. I will mention them due to the great need in deriving rulings to know them. The impediments that oppose capacity are celestial and acquired. As for acquired, among them is ignorance. It is of types: The first is ignorance that arises from intellectual stubbornness and abandoning a decisive proof that is more apparent than the sun at midday. This is the ignorance of the disbeliever. It is never an excuse; rather, he is taken to task in this world by humiliation through killing, plunder, enslavement, or taking jizya. After they accept [Islam], their proof is a defense against punishment for what they did, provided that it was permissible in their false religion—not like usury, for it is prohibited by consensus in all religions. So their wine-drinker is not flogged. And [their ignorance] is also a defense against the address [of revelation] according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. It is as if the revealed address was not directed at them, so it does not fall. They are liable for damages, the marriage of Zoroastrians to mahrams is valid and is not annulled unless both come to us, the lineage of children from such marriages is established, he is compelled to provide maintenance and dower, and he becomes muhṣan (one who can be stoned for adultery) by intercourse if he later becomes Muslim. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: It [the marriage] is not valid and is annulled forcibly; lineage, maintenance, dower, and iḥṣān are not established—because their religion, though preventing the direct address, does not establish a new ruling; rather, the original ruling remains. The original ruling regarding mahrams is prohibition, so it remains as in the case of usury. And this is more similar [to the correct view].
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage introduces impediments (awāriḍ) to legal capacity. The author distinguishes celestial and acquired impediments. The first acquired impediment is ignorance. The first type of ignorance is that of the disbeliever: it is never an excuse in this world. Disbelievers are punished directly. However, if they later accept Islam, they are not punished for past acts that were allowable in their religion, except for things universally prohibited like usury. There is a key ikhtilaf: According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the ignorance of the disbeliever also acts as a deterrent from the legal address (khiṭāb) itself—meaning they are not addressed by Islamic rulings in matters where their religion permits something, because it is as if the address never reached them. Thus, their marriage to mahrams is valid as long as they are Zoroastrian, and they incur no sin. Once they come to a Muslim judge, it can be annulled. The lines of these marriages are established, and they become muhṣan. The two companions disagree: they hold that the marital prohibition remains, so the marriage is void. The text notes that the companions' view is 'more similar' to the correct one, echoing a preference. The contrast between Abu Hanifa and the two companions shows a fundamental principle: does the disbeliever's ignorance block the very application of Islamic law (Abu Hanifa), or merely excuse punishment while the original ruling remains (companions)? The text ends with the companions' view being stronger.

Key Terms
الجهل (al-jahl): Ignorance as an acquired impediment to capacity; here, the first type is the disbeliever's stubborn ignorance.
دفع الخطاب (dafʿ al-khiṭāb): The concept that the legal address of revelation is not directed at disbelievers in certain matters, according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Void automatically
B) Valid until they come to a Muslim judge
C) Valid under all circumstances
D) Invalid only if the wife objects

Key Takeaway
The first impediment of ignorance (jahl al-kāfir) blocks the legal address from applying to disbelievers in certain matters, leading to the validity of actions that would otherwise be prohibited, unless they are universally condemned like ribā.

The Four Types of Ignorance and Their Legal Effects
Defining four categories of ignorance and their legal effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the third through sixth types of ignorance in Hanafi usul and state whether each excuses from sin, legal ruling, or both.

Core Idea
Ignorance's legal effect depends on its source: some excuse from sin, others from the ruling as well.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut categorizes four types of ignorance (jahl) that affect legal responsibility in Islamic law. Understanding these categories is essential for correctly applying rulings in cases where a person claims ignorance. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced framework: some ignorance excuses from sin but not from the legal effect, while other types are complete excuses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of ignorance
List the third, fourth, fifth, and sixth types mentioned in the passage. Note their distinguishing features.
The passage begins with 'الثالث‌: جهل نشأ...' and continues through the sixth type.
2
Determine the source of each type
For each type, identify what causes the ignorance: ijtihad in non-permissible matters, ijtihad in permissible matters, doubt/error, or necessity (dar al-harb).
Pay attention to the phrases 'فيما لا يجوز فيه الاجتهاد', 'فيما فيه مساغ', 'شبهة وخطأ', and 'جهل لزمه ضرورة بعذر'.
3
Analyze the legal effect for each
For each type, decide if it excuses from sin (إثم) and if it excuses from the legal ruling (حكم) or penalty (حد). Distinguish between 'عذر في حق الإثم' and 'عذر في الحكم'.
The passage explicitly states these effects for types 3, 4, 5, and 6.
4
Apply the classification to the examples
Examine the examples: sale of deliberately untasmiyah meat, marriage of thrice-divorced woman, mistaken intercourse, and drinking in dar al-harb. Confirm which type each example belongs to and its consequence.
The examples illustrate the practical outcome of each classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : جهل نشأ عن اجتهاد ودليل شرعي، لكن فيما لا يجوز فيه الاجتهاد بأن يخالف الكتاب أو السنة المشهورة أو الإجماع، وحكمه أنه وإن كان عذراً في حق الإثم لكن لا يكون عذراً في الحكم، حتى لا ينفذ القضاء به، فلا يصح بيع مذبوح متروك التسمية عمداً، ولا القضاء بحل المطلقة ثلاثاً الناكحة زوجاً آخر غير الذائقة عسيلته، كما حكي عن سعيد بن المسيب الرابع : جهل نشأ عن اجتهاد فيما فيه مساغ كالمجتهدات، وهو عذر البتة، وينفذ القضاء على حسبه . الخامس جهل نشأ عن شبهة وخطأ كمن وطىء أجنبية بظن أنها زوجته، أو وطىء جارية ابنه أو زوجته وهذا عذر في حق سقوط الحد. السادس : جهل لزمه ضرورة بعذر وهو أيضاً عذر كجهل المسلم في دار الحرب أحكام الإسلام، فلا يحد بالشرب وللجهل أحكام بحسب الإثم، وسيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى في الخاتمة مفصلاً.
The third: ignorance arising from ijtihad and a legal proof, but in matters where ijtihad is not permissible, such as contradicting the Book, the well-known Sunnah, or consensus. Its ruling is that although it is an excuse with regard to sin, it is not an excuse in the ruling, so judgment is not executed accordingly. Thus, the sale of a slaughtered animal whose name of Allah was intentionally omitted is not valid, nor is the judgment permitting a woman divorced three times who marries another husband without tasting his honey (sexual intercourse), as narrated from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab. The fourth: ignorance arising from ijtihad in matters where ijtihad is permissible, such as matters open to ijtihad. It is an absolute excuse, and judgment is executed accordingly. The fifth: ignorance arising from doubt and error, like one who has intercourse with a strange woman thinking she is his wife, or with his son's or wife's slave-girl. This is an excuse for dropping the hadd penalty. The sixth: ignorance necessitated by necessity and excuse, which is also an excuse, like the ignorance of a Muslim in dar al-harb about the rulings of Islam. Thus he is not flogged for drinking. Ignorance has rulings according to sin, and it will come in detail in the conclusion, Allah willing.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage categorizes four types of ignorance (the third through sixth) and their legal implications in the Hanafi school. The third type is ignorance that results from ijtihad but on a matter where ijtihad is not permitted because the ruling is clearly established by the Quran, well-known hadith, or consensus. Such ignorance excuses the person from sin but does not affect the legal ruling: the act remains invalid, and a judge cannot rule based on it. Examples include a sale of meat where the name of Allah was deliberately omitted (the meat is not halal) or a marriage of a thrice-divorced woman without proper consummation. The fourth type is ignorance on matters open to ijtihad; here it is a full excuse, and the ruling is based on the ijtihad. The fifth type is ignorance arising from a genuine mistake or doubt, like mistaking a stranger for one's wife. This excuses from the hadd punishment but not from civil liability. The sixth type is ignorance due to genuine necessity, such as a Muslim in a non-Muslim land not knowing certain rulings; this excuses from punishment. The passage emphasizes that the excuse varies with the type of ignorance.

Key Terms
جهل (jahl): Ignorance categorized into six types with different legal effects.
عذر (udhr): A legal excuse that mitigates or removes sin or penalty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of ignorance is a complete excuse for both sin and legal ruling according to the passage?
A) Ignorance from ijtihad in non-permissible matters
B) Ignorance from ijtihad in permissible matters
C) Ignorance from doubt and error
D) Ignorance from necessity in dar al-harb

Key Takeaway
The legal effect of ignorance is determined by its source; only ignorance from permissible ijtihad or necessity is a complete excuse.

Legal Effects of Intoxication (Sukr) — Hanafi Rule on Responsible Statements
Distinguishing between lawful and unlawful intoxication and their rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between lawful and unlawful intoxication and apply the Hanafi rule that a voluntarily intoxicated person is held to all statements except apostasy (with the exception of insulting the Prophet) and certain retractable confessions.

Core Idea
Intoxication from a prohibited substance is no excuse: divorce, manumission, oaths are valid; only apostasy and retractable confessions are exceptions.

In this passage from Ibn al-Humam's Fawatih al-Rahmut, we explore the legal status of intoxication (sukr) according to the Hanafi usul. The key distinction is between permissible intoxication (e.g., from necessity or medicine) and prohibited intoxication (voluntary consumption of wine). The latter does not nullify legal responsibility, except for apostasy due to lack of corrupt belief, but with an important exception regarding insults to the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of intoxication
Find in the text the distinction between permissible (mubah) and prohibited (haram) intoxication and their respective rulings.
Permissible includes medical pastes or wine under coercion/hunger; its ruling is like unconsciousness — no legal responsibility.
2
Extract the general principle for prohibited intoxication
The core rule: a person intoxicated by a prohibited substance is held fully responsible for all statements, including divorce, manumission, oaths, and zihar.
This is the default: no excuse.
3
Identify the exceptions to the principle
Apostasy (ridda) is generally not upheld because its rukn (corrupt belief) is absent. However, for apostasy involving insults to the Prophet, the person is still held liable — this is the relied-upon position.
Note the internal ikhtilaf: some books say the exception does not apply to insulting the Prophet.
4
Apply the rule to confessions (iqrar)
Confessions are generally binding, but those that can be retracted (like zina or drinking) are not binding on the intoxicated person. Non-retractable confessions (murder, slander) are binding.
The retractability is based on the nature of the confession; if evidence establishes the act while intoxicated, hadd is applied when sober.
5
Analyze the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
Why does prohibited intoxication not excuse statements? Because the shari'ah holds the person accountable for choosing to drink, and the contracts (divorce, etc.) are built on statements that are effective.
The illa is that the prohibition does not remove capacity; it is a choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها السكر، وهو إما من مباح، كما إذا سكر بالمعاجين التي تؤخذ من أشياء غير الخمر المأكولة لقوة البدن أو بالخمر المشروب وقت الإكراه والمخمصة، وحكمه حكم الإغماء الذي سيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى، وأما من محرم كالخمر المشروب في غير حال الضرورة، وحكمه أنه لا يكون عذراً في حال، فيؤخذ بعباراته حتى يقع طلاقه وعتاقه، ويصح يمينه وظهاره إلا عبارة الردة، إذ ركنها فساد العقيدة ولم يوجد ورأيت في بعض كتب الفقه إلا الردة بسبب الرسول صلوات الله عليه وآله وأصحابه، فإنه يؤخذ به السكران أيضاً، وعليه الاعتماد للفتوى، ويؤخذ بالأقارير إلا الإقرار الذي يصح فيه الرجوع، كالإقرار بالزنا، وشرب الخمر، لا الإقرار بالقتل والقذف فإنه يقتص ويحد، كما إذا قامت البينة على ارتكاب الزنا حال السكر، لكن يحد حال الصحو
And among them is intoxication. It is either from a permissible [substance], such as when one becomes intoxicated from electuaries made from things other than wine for bodily strength, or from wine drunk under coercion or extreme hunger; its ruling is like the ruling of unconsciousness which will come, Allah willing. As for [intoxication] from a prohibited substance, like wine drunk without necessity, its ruling is that it is never an excuse; he is held to his statements such that his divorce and manumission take effect, his oath and zihar are valid, except the statement of apostasy because its pillar is corruption of belief, and that does not exist. I saw in some books of fiqh, except apostasy concerning the Messenger, peace be upon him and his family and companions; then the intoxicated person is also held to it, and that is relied upon for fatwa. He is held to his confessions except a confession that can be retracted, like confession of zina or wine-drinking, not confession of murder or slander, for then retaliation and hadd are applied. Similarly, if evidence establishes zina while intoxicated, he is flogged when sober.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage discusses intoxication (sukr) and its legal effect on utterances and confessions in the Hanafi school. It divides intoxication into two types: (1) from a permissible cause, e.g., medicinal pastes or wine drunk under coercion or extreme hunger—this has the same ruling as unconsciousness, meaning no legal responsibility for statements; (2) from a prohibited cause (voluntary wine-drinking)—this is never an excuse. The intoxicated person is fully responsible for his statements: divorce, manumission, oaths, and zihar are valid. The exception is apostasy, since its essence is corrupt belief, which is absent due to intoxication. However, some authorities hold that if the apostasy involves insulting the Prophet, the intoxicated person is still held liable, and this is the relied-upon position. For confessions, the intoxicated person is held to confessions except those that can be retracted, like zina or drinking, but not for murder or slander, which require evidence. If zina is proven by evidence while intoxicated, the hadd is applied when sober.

Key Terms
سكر (sukr): Intoxication, divided into permissible and prohibited causes with different rulings.
ردة (ridda): Apostasy from Islam; requires corrupt belief which intoxication negates unless involving insults to the Prophet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which statement of an intoxicated person (from prohibited wine) is valid and takes effect?
A) Apostasy regarding belief in Allah
B) Divorce of his wife
C) Retraction of a confession of murder
D) Denial of zina

Key Takeaway
A person intoxicated by a prohibited substance is legally responsible for all statements except apostasy (with exception for insults to the Prophet) and retractable confessions.

Jest and Foolishness as Impediments to Legal Acts
Definition and legal effects of hazl and safah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define hazl and safah, state their legal effects according to the Hanafi school, and identify the ikhtilaf regarding hijr for the safih.

Core Idea
Jest invalidates legal acts; foolishness restricts financial capacity without removing obligation.

In this lesson, we explore two key impediments to legal capacity: jest (hazl) and foolishness (safah). The Hanafi school distinguishes between them: hazl negates the required intent for certain acts, while safah limits financial autonomy without affecting religious obligations. We will examine their definitions, legal effects, and the scholarly differences within the school.

Guided Path
1
Define hazl and safah
Read the passage to identify the definitions: hazl is speech contradicting apparent intent in jest; safah is stubbornness against using the intellect.
Note that hazl is about the mode of expression, while safah is about a persistent character trait.
2
Identify legal effects of hazl
The passage lists three categories: contracts (may be void), statements of information (invalid), and beliefs (invalid, may cause disbelief). Understand the reasoning: jest indicates lack of intent.
Pay attention to the intra-Hanafi difference regarding khiyar al-shart in contracts made in jest.
3
Analyze the definition of safah
The passage defines safah as mukabara ala al-aql (stubbornness against intellect). It does not remove taklif (obligation) because the person can still understand and act upon divine commands.
This distinguishes safah from insanity (junun), which removes taklif entirely.
4
Examine the dispute on hajr (interdiction)
Abu Hanifa denies hajr for the safih, while the two companions require it. Understand their proofs: Abu Hanifa argues wasting intellect does not necessitate hajr; the two companions emphasize preventing waste of wealth. Note the fatwa follows the two companions.
Also note the difference between Abu Yusuf (hajr by judge's decree) and Muhammad (automatic hajr).
5
Synthesize the rulings
Summarize: hazl invalidates legal acts requiring intent unless firmness is presumed (e.g., divorce); safah restricts financial transactions until rushd, but does not affect worship or other obligations.
Apply this understanding to hypothetical scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is not eligible for the option of stipulation according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), while according to him (Abu Hanifa) it depends on their choice if they built (the transaction?), for the option of stipulation is valid according to him regarding them. If they turn away, the jest becomes void and the contract is completed. If they remain silent or differ, the statement is of the one claiming seriousness according to him, and for the one claiming building according to them, but the jest becomes void, the payment is obligatory, and divorce occurs. The second: that is, statements (of information) have no validity at all, because jest is evidence of the absence of the thing informed about, and proof was only based on its consideration, so acknowledgments have no validity at all. The third: that is, beliefs are not valid with jest either, except that one disbelieves due to jest, not because of change of beliefs, but because jest is contempt of religion. This (is one), and among them is foolishness (safah), which is stubbornness against the intellect so that one does not use it. It does not prevent obligation (taklif) because it does not contradict understanding the address and acting on it. However, it prevents (control of) wealth until one reaches the semblance of sound judgment (rushd) according to him, which is the age of the grandfather, twenty-five years. According to them, (it is) the reality of sound judgment by explicit text in the Mighty Book. Then according to them, it is necessary to look after him, so interdiction (hajr) is necessary by a judge's ruling according to Abu Yusuf, and he becomes interdicted by himself according to Imam Muhammad. And the Imam (Abu Hanifa) says: He is not a subject of consideration, for he wastes the intellect that Allah gave him and does not use it, and also it involves wasting his humanity and likening him to animals, so no interdiction. In al-Tahrir, the more similar (view) is their statement, because preventing wealth is a clear indication that the objective is non-waste, and that is more effective by interdiction. I saw in fiqh books the fatwa is based on their statement.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on the effects of jest (hazl) and foolishness (safah) on legal transactions. The author, commenting on the matn, first concludes the discussion on jest: contracts made in jest are void but if conditions like khiyar al-shart are attached, Abu Hanifa differs from the two companions on its validity. The core principle is that jest indicates lack of intent, so information statements (like acknowledgments) are invalid, and beliefs expressed in jest are invalid and may cause disbelief due to contempt. The passage then defines safah as stubbornness against using one's intellect. Although a safih (foolish person) is legally obliged, his financial autonomy is restricted until he reaches an age of presumptive sound judgment—25 years according to Abu Hanifa, while the two companions consider actual rashd (sound judgment) as determined by clear texts. The two companions require a judge's interdiction (hajr) for the safih, while Muhammad holds it automatic; Abu Hanifa opposes interdiction entirely. The author reports that the relied-upon position (fatwa) is that of the two companions, as it better fulfills the objective of preventing waste.

Key Terms
الهزل (al-hazl): Jesting or playful speech that contradicts the apparent intent of a legal act.
السفه (al-safah): Stubbornness against using one's intellect, leading to waste of wealth.
الحجر (al-hajr): Legal interdiction preventing a person from managing his own wealth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Interdiction is not allowed at all.
D) The safih must request interdiction himself.

Key Takeaway
Jest invalidates legal acts requiring intent; foolishness restricts financial autonomy but leaves religious obligations intact, with the relied-upon position requiring judicial interdiction to protect wealth.

The Hanafi Rule for Attribution of Coerced Acts and the Shafi'i Division
Explains the Hanafi rule for coerced acts and compares with Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule (ḍābiṭa) to determine whether a coerced act is attributed to the coercer or the coerced person, and compare the Shafi‘i division of coercion.

Core Idea
The coerced person is an instrument of the coercer unless the act changes the subject — then liability stays with the coerced.

Coercion (ikrāh) affects the validity and attribution of acts in Islamic law. The Hanafi school has a detailed rule (ḍābiṭa) to determine whether the coerced person is merely an instrument (āla) of the coercer or bears responsibility. This lesson unpacks that rule, distinguishing utterances from actions, and compares it with the Shafi‘i division of coercion upon truth and falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of the coerced act
First, determine whether the act is an utterance (qawl) or an action (fi‘l). The rule differs fundamentally for these two categories.
Utterances include reports (ikhbārāt) like testimony, and constructions (inshā'āt) like divorce and sale.
2
Apply the instrument rule to utterances
If the act is an utterance, the coerced person cannot be an instrument because one does not speak with another's tongue. For reports, coercion negates the intent to match reality, so they do not convey rulings. For constructions, non-revocable ones (e.g., divorce) take effect despite coercion; revocable ones (e.g., sale) become invalid (fāsid).
The rationale: jest has no effect, so coercion (where some choice remains) has even less effect on irrevocable acts.
3
Apply the instrument rule to actions
If the act is an action, check if the coerced person can be an instrument (āla). If yes, then assess whether making him an instrument changes the subject (mahall) of the coercion. If it changes, the act is attributed solely to the coerced. If not, the act is attributed to the coercer, who bears liability.
Examples: killing a muhrim's game (subject changes) vs. killing a Muslim (subject unchanged).
4
Compare with the Shafi‘i division
The Shafi‘i school divides coercion into that upon truth and upon falsehood. If upon truth (e.g., forcing a harbi to faith), the coerced act is valid. If upon falsehood and the coerced act is permissible (mubāḥ), liability falls on the coercer; otherwise, the act is void (bāṭil). All transactions become void according to Shafi‘i because coercion cuts off agency.
The Hanafi position is more nuanced, preserving some coerced acts like divorce.
5
Synthesize: practical application
Use the Hanafi rule to analyze a new scenario: identify the act type, apply the instrument possibility, check subject change, and determine attribution and validity.
Example: coerced manumission – from one perspective it is a disposition (inshā') so not instrument; from another it is destruction of ownership so instrument, leading to liability on coercer.

Source Text

العهدة، ويجعل الفاعل آلة كما إذا أكره على قتل إنسان مسلم، فالقصاص على الملجيء دون القاتل، وكما إذا أكره على إتلاف مال المسلم فالضمان عليه دون المتلف وكالإكراه على الإعتاق فإنه من حيث صدر منه إنشاء And the detail regarding coercion: the rule according to us is that the agent can or cannot be an instrument (āla) for the coercer in the act. The first is the sayings: indeed, a person does not speak with the tongue of another, whether they are reports or constructions. Reports do not convey a ruling in both types because the authority in them is based on the reported content, and coercion is a clear indication that he does not intend correspondence. As for constructions, they are either not revocable like divorce, manumission, and similar, where jest has no effect—so their rulings do not occur and coercion does not affect them, because since jest had no effect despite no choice in the ruling, it is more appropriate that coercion has no effect even though there is choice. For he is coerced to effect divorce, not merely utter the word of divorce, and he intends the coerced act to preserve himself and his body. So reflect on it, for it is a subject of reflection. Or they are revocable like sale, lease, and similar, so they become invalid. The second is that which can be an instrument for the coercer. Then it is examined: if making him an instrument changes the subject of coercion or not. If it changes, the act is restricted to the agent, as in coercion to kill a muhrim's game (hunting). Even if he is made an instrument, it changes the subject of coercion because if he made him a killer, this killing would be a transgression against his own ihram, not against the coercer's ihram, and coercion is with transgression against the coercer's ihram. So it is restricted to him and he must pay the penalty. Rather, the penalty is due on the coercer because he committed another transgression beyond indicating the game. Similarly, if coerced to deliver the sold item after coercion to sell, if the agent is made an instrument, the act would be for the coercer, becoming usurpation not delivery, and the subject of coercion is only that, so it is restricted to him and he owns it with invalid ownership as in invalid sale. So reflect, for it is a place of intense reflection. If making him an instrument does not change the subject of coercion, it is attributed to the coercer and liability is on him, and the agent is made an instrument. As in coercion to kill a Muslim human, so retaliation is on the coercer, not the killer. And as in coercion to destroy a Muslim's property, so compensation is on the coercer, not the destroyer. And like coercion to manumit: from the perspective that the agent performs the act of disposition (insha'), he cannot be made an instrument because no one owns to free another's slave; but from the perspective that it is removal of ownership and destruction, it is valid to make him an instrument, so he is made an instrument, and compensation is due on the coercer. And upon this, analogize. And according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him: coercion is two types: upon the truth or upon falsehood. If upon the truth, like coercing a harbi to faith or coercing a debtor to sell, it has no effect and what is coerced is established. If upon falsehood, then consider: if it makes the coerced act permissible, then what is coerced is not established if it can be enforced against the coercer, as in destruction and similar, it is enforced against him; otherwise it becomes void. So all transactions become void according to him, whether reports or constructions, revocable or not, because coercion has cut off the act from the agent, and from other than him it does not take effect. And if it does not take effect on the agent, its ruling is established upon him, like coercion that makes permissible.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rules on the attribution of coerced acts in the Hanafi school. The key principle (ḍābiṭa) is whether the coerced person can be considered an instrument (āla) of the coercer. The text distinguishes between utterances (aqāwīl) and actions. For utterances: reports (ikhbārāt) like testimony or acknowledgment do not convey rulings because the authority lies in the content, and coercion indicates lack of intent to match reality. Constructions (inshā'āt) like divorce, manumission (which are not revocable) are not affected by coercion because even jest has no effect, indicating that serious intent is required; but coercion does not negate the intent to effect the act. For revocable constructions like sale and lease, they are invalidated. For actions: if the agent can be an instrument, the result depends on whether making him an instrument changes the subject (mahall) of coercion. If it changes (e.g., coercion to kill game of a muhrim), the act is attributed to the coerced person alone. If it does not change (e.g., coercion to kill a Muslim), the act is attributed to the coercer, and liability (damān, qisās) falls on the coercer. The text illustrates with examples: killing a muhrim's game, delivery of sold goods, killing a Muslim, destroying property, and manumitting a slave. Finally, it compares the Shafi'i view, which divides coercion into that on truth (haqq) and falsehood (bāṭil). According to Shafi'i, coercion on truth does not affect the ruling, while coercion on falsehood renders transactions void if the coerced act is not permissible, or enforces liability on the coercer if permissible. The Hanafi rule is more nuanced, considering the nature of the act and the possibility of being an instrument.

Key Terms
آلة (āla): Instrument; the coerced person is considered an instrument of the coercer when the act is attributed to the coercer.
الضابطة (al-ḍābiṭa): The rule or principle that governs the attribution of coerced acts.
إخبارات (ikhbārāt): Reports; utterances that convey information, such as testimony or acknowledgment.
إنشاءات (inshā'āt): Constructions; utterances that create legal effects, such as divorce, sale, or lease.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule for coerced acts depends on whether the act is an utterance or action, and whether the coerced person can be an instrument of the coercer without changing the subject.

Hanafi Rulings on Coercion (Ikrah)
Classifying coerced acts and comparing Hanafi and Shafi'i views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between irrevocable and revocable acts under coercion and apply that distinction to determine the validity of a coerced transaction.

Core Idea
Coercion does not void irrevocable acts like divorce; it invalidates revocable ones and shifts liability for physical acts.

Coercion (ikrah) is a common scenario where a person is compelled to act under threat. The Hanafi school offers a nuanced classification that goes beyond a simple yes/no. This lesson unpacks the key distinctions: between speech and physical acts, between irrevocable and revocable constructions, and whether the coerced person is treated as an instrument. Understanding this framework is essential for correctly applying the rules in real cases.

Guided Path
1
Categorize coerced acts
Identify whether the coerced act is a speech act (qawl) or a physical act (fi'l). This determines which sub-rule applies.
Speech acts include divorce, sale, etc.; physical acts include killing, destruction of property.
2
Analyze speech acts: irrevocable vs revocable
For speech acts, determine if the act is irrevocable (like divorce or manumission) or revocable (like sale or lease). Irrevocable acts remain valid under coercion; revocable ones become invalid.
The rationale: jest has no effect on irrevocable acts, so coercion (which includes some volition) also has no effect.
3
Analyze physical acts: instrument and subject change
If the coerced person can be an instrument for the coercer, check whether making him an instrument changes the subject of the coercion. If it does (e.g., killing game in ihram), the act is attributed to the coerced person. If it does not (e.g., killing a Muslim), it is attributed to the coercer.
Liability follows attribution: the coercer bears criminal or civil liability in the latter case.
4
Compare with Shafi'i classification
The Shafi'i school divides coercion into truth and falsehood, and generally holds that all coerced transactions are void. Contrast this with the Hanafi view.
The practical difference: Hanafi says coerced divorce is valid; Shafi'i says it is invalid.
5
Apply to a concrete case
Take a case of coerced divorce. Using the Hanafi rule, conclude it is valid. Then consider the Shafi'i result. This highlights the thamarah al-khilaf.
The same case yields different rulings, so the mufti must know which school to follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضد قدرة على ضد الضد الذي هو عين المكره عليه فصار المكره عليه مقدوراً، وكل مقدور التكليف به هذا ولعله غير واف فإن الخصم لم يجعل المانع من صحة التكليف انتفاء يصح القدرة حتى يرد ما أورد بل أحدث مانعاً آخر وهو انتفاء فائدة التكليف، وهو الامتثال مع الإخلاص في النية، وهذا غير دافع له بل الصواب في الجواب أنا لا نسلم الإتيان بعين المكره عليه لداعي الإكراه لزوماً، فإن الذين بذلوا أنفسهم في سبيل الله لا يقدمون على الفعل إلا لداعي الشرع والعمل بالنية والعالم هو الله تعالى، فتأمل فهو الأحق بالقبول والتفصيل في الإكراه أن الضابطة عندنا أن الفاعل لا يمكن أن يكون آلة للمكره في الفعل أو يمكن الأول هو الأقاويل فإن الشخص لا يتكلم بلسان غيره وهي إخبارات أو إنشاءات، فالإخبارات لا تفيد الحكم في كلا نوعيه لأن الحجية فيها باعتبار المحكى عنه، والإكراه قرينة ظاهرة، على أنه لا يقصد المطابقة والإنشاءات إما أن لا تقبل الفسخ كالطلاق والعتاق ونحوهما مما لا يؤثر فيه الهزل فهي لم تقع أحكامها ولا يؤثر فيها الإكراه، لأنه لما لم يؤثر الهزل مع أنه لا اختيار للحكم فيه، فالأولى أن لا يؤثر الإكراه مع أن فيه اختياراً فإنه إنما أكره عليه بإيقاع الطلاق لا مجرد التلفظ بكلمة الطلاق، وهو قد قصد المكره عليه إبقاء لنفسه وبدنه، فتأمل فيه فإنه محل تأمل، وإما أن تقبل الفسخ كالبيع والإجارة ونحوهما فهي تفسد والثاني وهو ما يمكن أن يكون آلة للمكره فينظر إن كان جعله آلة يغير محل الإكراه أو لا يغير فإن غير اقتصر الفعل على الفاعل كما في الإكراه على قتل المحرم الصيد فإنه وإن كان جعله آلة إلا أنه يغير يصح محل الإكراه، فإنه لو جعل قاتلاً، لكان هذا القتل جناية على إحرامه دون إحرام القتال، وكان الإكراه بالجناية على إحرام المكره، فحينئذ يقتصر عليه ويلزمه الجزاء، وإنما يجب الجزاء على المكره لأنه جنى جناية أخرى فوق الدلالة على الصيد، كما إذا أكره على تسليم المبيع بعد الإكراه على البيع، فإنه لو جعل الفاعل آلة لكان الفعل للمكره فيصير غصباً لا تسلماً للمبيع، ومحل الإكراه هو لا غير فيقتصر عليه ويملك ملكاً فاسداً كما في البيع الفاسد فتأمل فإنه موضع أشد تأمل، وإن كان جعله آلة لا يغير محل الإكراه ينسب إلى المكره ويلزمه العهدة، ويجعل الفاعل آلة كما إذا أكره على قتل إنسان مسلم، فالقصاص على الملجيء دون القاتل، وكما إذا أكره على إتلاف مال المسلم فالضمان عليه دون المتلف والإكراه على الإعتاق فإنه من حيث صدر منه إنشاء التصرف لا يصح جعله آلة إذ ليس يملك أحد أن يعتق عبد غيره، ومن حيث إنه مزيل للملك إتلاف ويصح جعله آلة فجعل آلة، ويجب الضمان على الملجيء، وعلى هذا فقس وعند الشافعي رحمه الله : الإكراه قسمان: على الحق أو على الباطل، فإن كان على الحق كإكراه الحربي على الإيمان وإكراه الدائن المديون على البيع، فلا يؤثر فيه، ويثبت ما أكره عليه، وإن كان على الباطل فينظر إن كان يبيح الفعل المكره عليه فلا يثبت ما أكره عليه إن وجد نفاذاً على الملجيء، كما في الإتلاف ونحوه نفذ عليه، وإلا بطل، فالتصرفات كلها تبطل عنده إخباراً كان أو إنشاء قابلاً للفسخ أولاً، فإن الإكراه قد قطع الفعل عن الفاعل ومن غيره لا ينفذ، وإن كان لا نفذ على الفاعل ويثبت حكمه عليه، كالإكراه يبيح على القتل يقتص من القاتل، وإنما يقتص من الملجىء لأنه مسبب فصار كأنه قتله اثنان، وكإكراه الرجل على الزنا، فيحد الزاني عنده هذا كله في أحكام الدنيا، وأما في أحكام الآخرة فالإكراه إن كان على الباطل فإن كان يباح المكره عليه حقيقة كالميتة والخمر فيؤجر على الفعل ويأثم بالترك، وإن كان لم يبح ولم يعامل الله معاملته أيضاً، بل بقي حراماً، كما كان قبل الإكراه يأثم الفعل ويؤجر على الترك، كالإكراه على القتل أو الزنا للرجل، وإن كان لم يبح لكن عومل به معاملة المباح يؤجر على الترك، ولا يأثم بالفعل كالإكراه على إجراء كلمة الكفر على اللسان، أو تأخير الصلاة عن الوقت، أو الإفطار في الشهر المبارك، أو الجناية على الإحرام، أو إكراه المرأة على الزنا ونحو ذلك، والإكراه على إتلاف مال المسلم، فهو أيضاً باق على الحرمة وعومل معاملة المباح، لكن قال الإمام محمد رحمه الله : أرجو أن لا يأثم بالإتلاف، وإن صبر كان شهيداً ومأجوراً هذا كله في الملجىء، وأما في غير الملجيء فيأثم هذا، ولما كانت مسائل الإكراه بل سائر العوارض مبنية على انتفاء الحرج في الدين، أورد مسألته عقيب الإكراه متخللة بين العوارض فقال .
The opposite is ability over the opposite which is the very thing coerced upon, so the coerced act becomes possible. And everything possible is subject to obligation. This is perhaps insufficient, for the opponent did not make the preventer of validity of obligation the absence of valid ability so that this objection could be raised; rather, he introduced another preventer: the absence of benefit of obligation, which is compliance with sincerity in intention. And this does not refute him. Rather, the correct answer is that we do not concede that performing the very coerced act is necessarily due to the motive of coercion. For those who sacrifice themselves in the path of Allah do not proceed to the act except due to the motive of the Shari'ah and acting by intention, and Allah knows best. So reflect, for it is more deserving of acceptance. And the detail regarding coercion is that the rule with us is: either the doer cannot be an instrument for the coercer in the act, or he can. The first is speech: a person does not speak with the tongue of another, whether it be reports or constructions. Reports do not produce a ruling in either of their types because their authority rests on the reported content, and coercion is an apparent indication that he does not intend conformity. As for constructions, either they are not revocable, like divorce, manumission, and the like, which are not affected by jest. They do not produce their rulings, and coercion does not affect them. Because when jest has no effect despite there being no choice in the ruling, it is more fitting that coercion has no effect even though there is choice in it. For he is coerced into effecting divorce, not merely uttering the word of divorce, and he intends the coerced act to preserve himself and his body. So reflect, for it is a place of reflection. Or they are revocable, like sale, lease, and the like, then they become invalid. The second is that which can be an instrument for the coercer. Then we consider: if making him an instrument changes the subject of coercion or not. If it changes, the act is confined to the doer, as in coercion to kill game while in ihram. For although he made him an instrument, it changes the subject of coercion. If he made him a killer, this killing would be a transgression against his own ihram, not the ihram of the killer, and the coercion would be against the ihram of the coerced. So it is confined to him, and he is liable for the compensation. The compensation is only due from the coerced because he committed another transgression beyond pointing out the game. Similarly, if coerced to deliver the sold item after coercion to sell, if he made the doer an instrument, the act would be attributed to the coercer, becoming usurpation, not delivery of the sold item. The subject of coercion is nothing else, so it is confined to him, and he holds defective ownership as in a defective sale. So reflect, for it is a place of deep reflection. If making him an instrument does not change the subject of coercion, the act is attributed to the coercer, and the liability falls on him, and the doer is made an instrument, as in coercion to kill a Muslim: retaliation is upon the coercer, not the killer. Similarly, coercion to destroy a Muslim's property: compensation is upon him, not the destroyer. And coercion to manumit: in terms of issuing the construction, he cannot be made an instrument because no one can manumit another's slave; but in terms of removing ownership, it is destruction, and he can be made an instrument. So he is made an instrument, and compensation is due from the coercer. Extrapolate from this. And according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him: coercion is of two types: upon truth or upon falsehood. If upon truth, like coercing a war captive to believe or coercing a debtor to sell, it has no effect, and that which he is coerced upon is established. If upon falsehood, then consider: if it permits the coerced act, then that which he is coerced upon is not established if it can be executed against the coercer, as in destruction and the like, it is executed against him; otherwise, it is void. So all transactions are void according to him, whether report or construction, revocable or not. For coercion has severed the act from the doer, and from other than him it cannot be executed. If it cannot be executed against the doer, its ruling is established against him, as coercion permits murder: retaliation is from the killer, but actually retaliation is from the coercer because he is the cause, so it is as if two killed him. And as coercion of a man to commit adultery: the adulterer is punished with hadd according to him. All this pertains to the rulings of this world. As for the rulings of the Hereafter: if coercion is upon falsehood, then if the coerced act is permissible in reality, like carrion and wine, he is rewarded for the act and sins for leaving it. If it is not permissible and Allah does not treat it as such either, rather it remains forbidden as before coercion, he sins for the act and is rewarded for leaving it, like coercion to kill or adultery for a man. If it is not permissible but is treated as permissible, he is rewarded for leaving and does not sin for the act, like coercion to utter words of disbelief, delaying prayer beyond its time, breaking the fast in the blessed month, transgression against ihram, coercion of a woman to adultery, and the like. And coercion to destroy a Muslim's property: it remains forbidden but is treated as permissible. However, Imam Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, said: I hope he does not sin for the destruction, and if he is patient, he will be a martyr and rewarded. All this pertains to the coercer. As for other than the coercer, he sins. And since issues of coercion, indeed all impediments, are based on the absence of hardship in religion, he presents this issue after coercion, interposed among the impediments, saying:
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the Hanafi position on coercion (ikrah) in legal acts. The author begins by addressing a common objection: that the coerced act is impossible because one cannot intend both the act and its opposite. The Hanafi response is that the coerced person can still intend the act to avoid harm, citing the example of those who sacrifice their lives for Allah. The key distinction in Hanafi law is between acts where the coerced person is merely an instrument (like speaking) and acts where he is an agent. For speech acts (igharat/insha'at), if they are irrevocable (like divorce), coercion does not void them; if revocable (like sale), they become invalid. For physical acts where the coerced person can be an instrument, if being an instrument changes the nature of the coerced act (e.g., killing game in ihram), the act is attributed to the doer; if not (e.g., killing a Muslim), it is attributed to the coercer. The passage then presents the Shafi'i classification of coercion into truth and falsehood, with different rulings. Finally, it discusses the Hereafter rewards and sins based on the type of act. The section concludes by noting that these rules derive from the principle of no hardship (la haraj).

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Coercion that compels a person to act against his will under threat of harm; Hanafi fiqh classifies it into types affecting legal rulings.
الملجئ (al-mulji'): The coercer who compels the act.
الإنشاءات (al-insha'at): Legal constructions that create obligations, like sale, divorce, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, what is the effect of coercion on a divorce pronouncement?
A) The divorce is invalid because coercion removes volition.
B) The divorce is valid because coercion does not affect irrevocable acts.
C) The divorce is suspended until coercion ends.
D) The divorce is valid only if pronounced three times.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's classification of coerced acts into speech vs. physical, and within speech into revocable vs. irrevocable, determines whether the act is valid, invalid, or attributed to the coercer.

The Principle of No Hardship (La Haraj): Revealed or Rational?
Definition and source dispute of the principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that the principle 'La haraj fi al-shar' is a revealed (shar'i) principle, differentiate it from the Mu'tazili rational view, and apply the definition of haraj as a graded concept to identify which hardships matter legally.

Core Idea
Hardship is legally relevant only when the Lawgiver recognizes it – not every difficulty counts.

This lesson introduces the foundational usul principle of la haraj (no hardship). The Hanafi school grounds this principle in revelation, not reason. Understanding its nature is crucial for deriving the many concessions (rukhas) in Islamic law. We'll examine the dispute with the Mu'tazila and the definition of haraj as a graded concept (mushakkak).

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The text opens with a question: is la haraj a rational principle (Mu'tazila) or a revealed one (Hanafis)? Recognize the two positions.
2
Define haraj
Hardship is a graded concept (kulli mushakkak): some instances are stronger. Only the levels that the Lawgiver has established as valid have legal effect.
3
Apply the principle
The first application: children (sabi 'aqil) and insane adults (ma'tuh baligh) are not obligated to perform any rulings (la yajibu shay'). This is because obligation would entail hardship given their deficiency.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Zayd al-Qadi argued that acts of worship are obligatory upon them but their performance falls away by necessity (sakat ada'uha lil-darura). This is a nuance in how the principle is applied.
5
Summarize the Hanafi position
The mufta bihi is that they are not obligated at all, as per the principle of la haraj.

Source Text

بنيته المؤمنين مسألة لا حرج في الشرع (عقلاً) كما عند المعتزلة (أو شرعاً) كما عندنا (وهو) أي الحرج كلي (مشكك بعض أفراده أقوى من بعض ولا يعتبر كل مرتبة منه بل ما ثبت من الشارع اعتباره فلهذا) أي فلأجل أنه لا حرج في الدين (لم يجب شيء) من الأحكام على الصبي العاقل) لقصور البدن أو لقصوره وقصور العقل ولا على المعتوه البالغ) لقصور العقل (خلافاً لأبي زيد الإمام القاضي، لأن العبادات واجبة سقط أداؤها للضرورة (و) لأجل أن لا حرج في الدين (لم يجب قضاء الصلاة في الحيض والنفاس دون الصوم فإنه يجب قضاؤه، لقول أم عائشة الصديقة رضي الله عنها : كان يصيبنا ذلك، فنؤمر بقضاء الصوم ولا نؤمر بقضاء الصلاة، كذا في الصحيحين». وفي قضاء الصلاة من الحرج ما لا يخفى، لأن الشهر عادة لا يخلو من الحيض، وهو أيضاً لا يكون أقل من ثلاثة فتبلغ الصلاة كثرة في قضائها حرج عظيم (و) لأجل ذلك شرعت العبادات في المرض على حسب الطاقة (قاعداً ومضطجعاً) لما روى البيهقي والبزار عن جابر رضي الله عنه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عاد مريضاً فرآه يصلي على وسادة، فأخذها فرمى بها، فأخذ عوداً يصلي عليه فأخذه فرمی به وقال: «صل على الأرض إن استطعت، وإلا فأوم إيماءً، واجعل سجودك أخفض من ركوعك (و) لأجل ذلك (انتفى الإثم في الخطأ مجتهداً) وقد ثبت بإجماع قاطع معاضد بأحاديث صحاح، وقد أومأ الله تعالى إليه بقوله: ﴿ لَوْلَا كِتَبُ مِنَ اللَّهِ سَبَقَ لَمَسَّكُمْ فِيمَا أَخَذْتُمْ عَذَابٌ عَظِيمٌ [الأنفال : ٦٨] أي لولا سبق الكتاب بأن لا مؤاخذة في الخطأ في الاجتهاد لمسكم العذاب في أخذ الفداء والله أعلم بمراده (و) لذلك انتفى الإثم ( في النسيان) لما روينا من قبل (و) لذلك (انتفى أكل الصائم ناسياً فلا ينتقض به الصوم ولا إثم أيضاً، لما روى الشيخان عن أبي هريرة عن
His intention [maybe 'Babi' or 'Binyat al-mu'minin'] is a question: There is no hardship in the Shari'ah (rationally), as the Mu'tazila hold, or (legally), as we hold. It (i.e., hardship) is a graded concept; some of its instances are stronger than others, and not every level is considered, but only what the Lawgiver has established as considered. For this reason (i.e., because there is no hardship in religion), nothing of the rulings is obligatory upon the intelligent child, due to weakness of body or weakness of both body and intellect, nor upon the insane adult, due to weakness of intellect, contrary to Abu Zayd the Imam al-Qadi, because acts of worship are obligatory but their performance falls due to necessity. And for the same reason, making up prayers during menstruation and postpartum bleeding is not obligatory, unlike fasting which must be made up, due to the saying of Umm 'A'ishah al-Siddiqah, may Allah be pleased with her: 'That used to happen to us, and we were commanded to make up the fast but not commanded to make up the prayer.' This is in the Sahihayn. And in making up prayers there is manifest hardship, because a month is usually not free from menstruation, and it is not less than three days, so prayers become many in making them up, which is great hardship. For that reason, acts of worship during illness are prescribed according to ability: sitting and lying down, due to what al-Bayhaqi and al-Bazzar narrated from Jabir, may Allah be pleased with him, that the Prophet, peace be upon him, visited a sick person and saw him praying on a cushion, so he took it and threw it away. Then he took a stick to pray on, so he took it and threw it away and said: 'Pray on the ground if you are able; otherwise, gesture with your head, and make your prostrations lower than your bows.' For that reason, sin is removed in error from a mujtahid. This is established by definitive consensus supported by authentic hadiths, and Allah alluded to it in His saying: {If not for a decree from Allah that preceded, a great punishment would have touched you for what you took} [al-Anfal: 68]. That is, if not for the prior decree that there is no accountability for error in ijtihad, you would be punished for taking ransom. And Allah knows best His intent. For that reason, sin is removed in forgetfulness, as we narrated before. For that reason, eating while fasting forgetfully does not break the fast, and there is no sin either, due to what the two Shaykhs narrated from Abu Hurayrah, from
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage establishes the foundational usul principle 'La haraj fi al-shar' (No hardship in the Shari'ah). The author distinguishes between the Mu'tazili view that this is rational and the Hanafi view that it is a revealed principle. Hardship is a graded concept (mushakkak), and only what the Lawgiver considers as such has legal weight. The principle is then applied to several rulings: (1) Obligations are not imposed on children (sabi 'aqil) or the insane (ma'tuh), though there is a disagreement with Abu Zayd al-Qadi who held that acts of worship are obligatory but fall due to necessity. (2) Women do not make up missed prayers during menstruation, but must make up fasts, due to the hadith of 'A'ishah and the principle of avoiding hardship. (3) Sick people may pray sitting or lying down, with gesture, based on the hadith of Jabir. (4) There is no sin for a mujtahid who errs, established by consensus and the Quranic verse (8:68). (5) Forgetfulness removes sin, and eating forgetfully does not break the fast, based on a hadith from Abu Hurayrah. The passage clearly uses the principle derivatively, showing how it underpins various concessions.

Key Terms
الحرج (al-haraj): Hardship; a graded concept (mushakkak) that the Lawgiver may consider in alleviating rulings.
الصبي العاقل (al-sabi al-'aqil): A child who has attained discernment (tamyiz) but is not yet legally responsible (mukallaf).
المعتوه البالغ (al-ma'tuh al-baligh): An adult who is insane or mentally deficient; not obligated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main point of disagreement between the Hanafis and Mu'tazila regarding the principle of la haraj?
A) Whether haraj exists at all.
B) Whether it is rational or revealed.
C) Whether it applies to children.
D) Whether it applies to menstruation.

Key Takeaway
The principle 'la haraj fi al-shar' is a revealed principle; hardship is a graded concept, and only the Lawgiver's recognition gives it legal weight.

Applications of the La Haraj Principle in Rulings
Concrete applications of the principle to individual rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of la haraj to explain why women do not make up missed prayers during menstruation, why sick can pray sitting, and why there is no sin for error in ijtihad and forgetfulness.

Core Idea
La Haraj shapes specific concessions: menstruating women skip prayer qada', sick pray according to ability, erring or forgetful face no sin.

The principle of no hardship is not abstract; it yields concrete rulings. This lesson examines four applications from the passage: menstruation (prayer vs fasting), illness (permission to sit or gesture), error in ijtihad, and forgetfulness. Each shows how the Lawgiver alleviates hardship.

Guided Path
1
Examine menstruation ruling
The text states: women do not make up missed prayers during menses, but must make up fasts. Identify the hadith from Aisha and the reasoning that making up prayers would entail manifest hardship.
2
Examine illness ruling
The sick may pray sitting or lying with gestures. See the hadith of Jabir: the Prophet instructed to pray on ground if able, otherwise gesture.
3
Examine error in ijtihad
There is no sin for a mujtahid who errs. The text cites consensus and Quran 8:68.
4
Examine forgetfulness
Eating forgetfully during fast does not break it, and there is no sin.
5
Unify under la haraj
Each case is a specific application of the principle that the Shari'ah recognizes hardship and provides a concession.

بنيته المؤمنين مسألة لا حرج في الشرع (عقلاً) كما عند المعتزلة (أو شرعاً) كما عندنا (وهو) أي الحرج كلي (مشكك بعض أفراده أقوى من بعض ولا يعتبر كل مرتبة منه بل ما ثبت من الشارع اعتباره فلهذا) أي فلأجل أنه لا حرج في الدين (لم يجب شيء) من الأحكام على الصبي العاقل) لقصور البدن أو لقصوره وقصور العقل ولا على المعتوه البالغ) لقصور العقل (خلافاً لأبي زيد الإمام القاضي، لأن العبادات واجبة سقط أداؤها للضرورة (و) لأجل أن لا حرج في الدين (لم يجب قضاء الصلاة في الحيض والنفاس دون الصوم فإنه يجب قضاؤه، لقول أم عائشة الصديقة رضي الله عنها : كان يصيبنا ذلك، فنؤمر بقضاء الصوم ولا نؤمر بقضاء الصلاة، كذا في الصحيحين». وفي قضاء الصلاة من الحرج ما لا يخفى، لأن الشهر عادة لا يخلو من الحيض، وهو أيضاً لا يكون أقل من ثلاثة فتبلغ الصلاة كثرة في قضائها حرج عظيم (و) لأجل ذلك شرعت العبادات في المرض على حسب الطاقة (قاعداً ومضطجعاً) لما روى البيهقي والبزار عن جابر رضي الله عنه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عاد مريضاً فرآه يصلي على وسادة، فأخذها فرمى بها، فأخذ عوداً يصلي عليه فأخذه فرمی به وقال: «صل على الأرض إن استطعت، وإلا فأوم إيماءً، واجعل سجودك أخفض من ركوعك (و) لأجل ذلك (انتفى الإثم في الخطأ مجتهداً) وقد ثبت بإجماع قاطع معاضد بأحاديث صحاح، وقد أومأ الله تعالى إليه بقوله: ﴿ لَوْلَا كِتَبُ مِنَ اللَّهِ سَبَقَ لَمَسَّكُمْ فِيمَا أَخَذْتُمْ عَذَابٌ عَظِيمٌ [الأنفال : ٦٨] أي لولا سبق الكتاب بأن لا مؤاخذة في الخطأ في الاجتهاد لمسكم العذاب في أخذ الفداء والله أعلم بمراده (و) لذلك انتفى الإثم ( في النسيان) لما روينا من قبل (و) لذلك (انتفى أكل الصائم ناسياً فلا ينتقض به الصوم ولا إثم أيضاً، لما روى الشيخان عن أبي هريرة عن
His intention [maybe 'Babi' or 'Binyat al-mu'minin'] is a question: There is no hardship in the Shari'ah (rationally), as the Mu'tazila hold, or (legally), as we hold. It (i.e., hardship) is a graded concept; some of its instances are stronger than others, and not every level is considered, but only what the Lawgiver has established as considered. For this reason (i.e., because there is no hardship in religion), nothing of the rulings is obligatory upon the intelligent child, due to weakness of body or weakness of both body and intellect, nor upon the insane adult, due to weakness of intellect, contrary to Abu Zayd the Imam al-Qadi, because acts of worship are obligatory but their performance falls due to necessity. And for the same reason, making up prayers during menstruation and postpartum bleeding is not obligatory, unlike fasting which must be made up, due to the saying of Umm 'A'ishah al-Siddiqah, may Allah be pleased with her: 'That used to happen to us, and we were commanded to make up the fast but not commanded to make up the prayer.' This is in the Sahihayn. And in making up prayers there is manifest hardship, because a month is usually not free from menstruation, and it is not less than three days, so prayers become many in making them up, which is great hardship. For that reason, acts of worship during illness are prescribed according to ability: sitting and lying down, due to what al-Bayhaqi and al-Bazzar narrated from Jabir, may Allah be pleased with him, that the Prophet, peace be upon him, visited a sick person and saw him praying on a cushion, so he took it and threw it away. Then he took a stick to pray on, so he took it and threw it away and said: 'Pray on the ground if you are able; otherwise, gesture with your head, and make your prostrations lower than your bows.' For that reason, sin is removed in error from a mujtahid. This is established by definitive consensus supported by authentic hadiths, and Allah alluded to it in His saying: {If not for a decree from Allah that preceded, a great punishment would have touched you for what you took} [al-Anfal: 68]. That is, if not for the prior decree that there is no accountability for error in ijtihad, you would be punished for taking ransom. And Allah knows best His intent. For that reason, sin is removed in forgetfulness, as we narrated before. For that reason, eating while fasting forgetfully does not break the fast, and there is no sin either, due to what the two Shaykhs narrated from Abu Hurayrah, from
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage establishes the foundational usul principle 'La haraj fi al-shar' (No hardship in the Shari'ah). The author distinguishes between the Mu'tazili view that this is rational and the Hanafi view that it is a revealed principle. Hardship is a graded concept (mushakkak), and only what the Lawgiver considers as such has legal weight. The principle is then applied to several rulings: (1) Obligations are not imposed on children (sabi 'aqil) or the insane (ma'tuh), though there is a disagreement with Abu Zayd al-Qadi who held that acts of worship are obligatory but fall due to necessity. (2) Women do not make up missed prayers during menstruation, but must make up fasts, due to the hadith of 'A'ishah and the principle of avoiding hardship. (3) Sick people may pray sitting or lying down, with gesture, based on the hadith of Jabir. (4) There is no sin for a mujtahid who errs, established by consensus and the Quranic verse (8:68). (5) Forgetfulness removes sin, and eating forgetfully does not break the fast, based on a hadith from Abu Hurayrah. The passage clearly uses the principle derivatively, showing how it underpins various concessions.

Key Terms
الحرج (al-haraj): Hardship; a graded concept (mushakkak) that the Lawgiver may consider in alleviating rulings.
الحيض (al-hayd): Menstruation; a state in which women do not pray and do not make up missed prayers.
النفاس (al-nifas): Postpartum bleeding; same ruling as menstruation regarding prayer and fasting.
القضاء (al-qada'): Making up a missed obligatory act after its time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle of la haraj in this passage, why are women not required to make up missed prayers during menstruation?
A) Because menstruation is impure and prayer is invalid.
B) Because making up all those prayers would cause great hardship, contrary to the principle.
C) Because the hadith says to only make up fasts.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The La Haraj principle produces specific concessions: prayers during menses not made up, sick pray as able, no sin for error or forgetfulness.
