The Hanafi Rule on Compulsion (Ikrah) and Its Effects
Usul rule on compulsion and its effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify types of compulsion (ikrah) according to Hanafi usul and determine the corresponding punishment and reward in this world and the Hereafter.

Core Idea
Compulsion does not remove all liability; its effect depends on whether it is overwhelming and on the nature of the compelled act.

Compulsion (ikrah) is a key impediment (arid) that can alter legal liability. The Hanafi school meticulously distinguishes between overwhelming compulsion (mulji') and non-overwhelming compulsion, and further between the nature of the compelled act regarding its permissibility in the Hereafter. This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut presents these distinctions and their consequences in both worldly punishment and afterlife reward/punishment. Understanding this rule is essential for applying the principle of 'no hardship' (la haraj) in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish worldly and afterlife rulings
The passage first separates rulings of this world (hukm dunyawi) from rulings of the Hereafter (hukm ukhrawi). Note that in worldly rulings, compulsion may affect liability for retaliation or hadd, while in afterlife rulings, the focus is on sin and reward.
Worldly rulings often consider the immediate cause, but afterlife rulings evaluate the individual's choice and intention.
2
Identify the two types of compulsion
The passage distinguishes between overwhelming compulsion (mulji') and non-overwhelming compulsion (ghayr mulji'). Mulji' leaves no choice, while non-mulji' leaves some degree of choice.
This distinction is central to Hanafi usul and appears in many chapters of fiqh.
3
Categorize the compelled acts for mulji' compulsion
For mulji' compulsion, the passage describes three categories of acts: (1) acts that are inherently permissible (mubah haqiqatan), like eating carrion when necessary; (2) acts that remain forbidden and are not treated as permissible, like killing; and (3) acts that are forbidden but treated as permissible, like uttering kufr. Each category has different rulings for reward and sin.
The same act can have a different afterlife ruling depending on whether it is treated as permissible or not.
4
Apply the categories to specific examples
The passage provides examples: for killing or zina under compulsion, the person sins for the act and is rewarded for refraining; for uttering kufr or breaking the fast, the person does not sin for the act and is rewarded for refraining. For property destruction, Imam Muhammad expresses hope of no sin, but patience is better.
Note the intra-Hanafi difference regarding property destruction.
5
Contrast with non-overwhelming compulsion
The passage concludes that for non-mulji' compulsion, the person sins for the act, implying that the act remains sinful and the person is accountable.
This reinforces the principle that only overwhelming compulsion may reduce liability in the Hereafter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثانية في الأحكام على القتل يقتص من القاتل، وإنما يقتص من الملجىء لأنه مسبب فصار كأنه قتله اثنان، وكإكراه الرجل على الزنا، فيحد الزاني عنده هذا كله في أحكام الدنيا، وأما في أحكام الآخرة فالإكراه إن كان على الباطل فإن كان يباح المكره عليه حقيقة كالميتة والخمر فيؤجر على الفعل ويأثم بالترك، وإن كان لم يبح ولم يعامل الله معاملته أيضاً، بل بقي حراماً، كما كان قبل الإكراه يأثم الفعل ويؤجر على الترك، كالإكراه على القتل أو الزنا للرجل، وإن كان لم يبح لكن عومل به معاملة المباح يؤجر على الترك، ولا يأثم بالفعل كالإكراه على إجراء كلمة الكفر على اللسان، أو تأخير الصلاة عن الوقت، أو الإفطار في الشهر المبارك، أو الجناية على الإحرام، أو إكراه المرأة على الزنا ونحو ذلك، والإكراه على إتلاف مال المسلم، فهو أيضاً باق على الحرمة وعومل معاملة المباح، لكن قال الإمام محمد رحمه الله : أرجو أن لا يأثم بالإتلاف، وإن صبر كان شهيداً ومأجوراً هذا كله في الملجىء، وأما في غير الملجيء فيأثم هذا، ولما كانت مسائل الإكراه بل سائر العوارض مبنية على انتفاء الحرج في الدين، أورد مسألته عقيب الإكراه متخللة بين العوارض فقال .
The second article on rulings. On killing, retaliation is taken from the killer, and retaliation is taken from the compeller because he is the cause, so it is as if two persons killed him. And like the compulsion of a man to commit zina, then the adulterer is punished (hadd) according to him (Abu Hanifa?). All this is in the rulings of this world. As for the rulings of the Hereafter, if the compulsion is for falsehood, then if the compelled act is permissible in itself like carrion and wine, he is rewarded for the act and sins for leaving it. And if it was not permissible and Allah does not treat it as permissible either, rather it remains forbidden as before compulsion, he sins for the act and is rewarded for leaving it, like compulsion to kill or zina for a man. And if it was not permissible but it is treated as permissible, he is rewarded for leaving it and does not sin for the act, like compulsion to utter a word of kufr, or delaying prayer from its time, or breaking fast in the blessed month, or violating ihram, or compulsion of a woman to commit zina and the like. And compulsion to destroy a Muslim's property also remains forbidden but is treated as permissible. However, Imam Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: I hope that he does not sin by destroying, and if he endures patiently, he becomes a martyr and is rewarded. All this is in the case of overwhelming compulsion (mulji'). As for non-overwhelming compulsion, he sins. And since the issues of compulsion, indeed all impediments, are based on the negation of hardship in religion, he presented his issue after compulsion, interspersed among the impediments, and said:
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage discusses the rulings of compulsion (ikrah) in both worldly and afterlife contexts. The author first distinguishes between overwhelming compulsion (mulji') and non-overwhelming. In worldly rulings, retaliation is taken from the compeller in cases of murder because he is the efficient cause, as if two killed. Similarly, in compulsion to zina, the hadd punishment is applied to the person compelled (according to Abu Hanifa). In afterlife rulings, the effect depends on the nature of the compelled act: if the act is permissible in essence (like eating carrion when necessary), the person is rewarded for doing it and sins for not doing it. If the act remains forbidden but is excused (like killing or zina for a man), the person sins for doing it and is rewarded for refraining. If the act is forbidden but treated as permissible (like uttering kufr under duress), he is rewarded for refraining and does not sin for doing it. For destruction of property, Imam Muhammad expresses hope of no sin for doing it under duress, but patience is better. These distinctions show how the Shari'ah balances hardship and responsibility. The passage concludes by noting that the principle of no hardship (la haraj) underlies all these rules, leading to the next topic.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Compulsion that forces a person to act against his will.
الملجىء (al-mulji'): Overwhelming compulsion where the person has no choice.
القاتل (al-qatil): The killer, but in context, the compeller is treated as the cause of killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a person compelled to utter kufr?
A) He sins and is not rewarded for refraining.
B) He is rewarded for refraining and does not sin for uttering.
C) He is punished with hadd in this world.
D) He is considered a martyr.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school applies a nuanced classification of compelled acts under overwhelming compulsion to determine sin and reward, based on whether the act is intrinsically permissible, remains forbidden, or is treated as permissible.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Principle of No Hardship (La Haraj) and Its Applications
Establishes the principle of no hardship (la haraj) and derives multiple fiqh applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the principle of no hardship (la haraj) in Shari'ah and apply it to various fiqh rulings, demonstrating how the Lawgiver prioritizes ease over strict analogy.

Core Idea
Shari'ah removes hardship, but only the levels recognized by the Lawgiver matter, not every subjective difficulty.

The principle of 'no hardship in religion' is a foundational usul concept in the Hanafi school. This lesson explores its definition as a graded concept and its application across multiple fiqh rulings, from the exemption of women from makeup prayers to concessions for travelers. You will see how the Lawgiver uses ease as a guiding principle, overriding strict qiyas when hardship would result.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
State the principle of la haraj and explain that hardship is a graded concept; only levels recognized by the Lawgiver create legal effects.
The principle is not absolute; subjective difficulty is not enough.
2
Examine the first applications
Analyze the exemptions for children (no obligation), insane adults, menstruating women (no makeup for prayers), and the sick (prayer according to ability).
Each example shows hardship avoidance.
3
Analyze the proofs from Quran and Sunnah
Look at the hadith about forgetful eating (complete fast), the verse about error in ijtihad (Quran 8:68), and the hadith about travel concessions.
These proofs establish the principle authoritatively.
4
Understand the override of qiyas
Note that for travel, the Lawgiver considered the start of the journey as sufficient for concession, contrary to the analogy that would require actual travel.
This shows that la haraj can override strict qiyas.
5
Synthesize the pattern
See how all examples reflect the same principle: the Lawgiver removes hardship by granting concessions (rukhsah) where strict application would cause undue difficulty.
This is a key hermeneutical tool for deriving rulings.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(The chain) The issue: There is no hardship in Shari'ah, (rationally as the Mu'tazila hold, or legally as we hold). And it (hardship) is a graded concept; some instances are stronger than others, and not every level is considered, only what the Lawgiver has deemed significant. Because of this (that there is no hardship in religion), nothing (of obligations) is required from an intelligent child (due to bodily deficiency or deficiency of both body and mind), nor from an adult imbecile (due to mental deficiency), contrary to Abu Zayd al-Qadi (because acts of worship are obligatory but their performance is dropped due to necessity). And because there is no hardship in religion, the makeup of prayer is not required during menstruation and postpartum bleeding, unlike fasting which must be made up, due to the saying of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her): 'We used to experience that, and we were ordered to make up the fast but not the prayer' (agreed upon). The hardship of making up prayers is apparent because a woman's period usually occurs at least three days a month, leading to great hardship. Due to this, acts of worship during illness are prescribed according to ability, sitting or lying down, due to the hadith reported by al-Bayhaqi and al-Bazzar from Jabir that the Prophet (peace be upon him) visited a sick person and saw him praying on a cushion; he took it and threw it away, and told him to pray on the ground if able, otherwise by gestures, making his prostration lower than his bowing. And because of this, sin is negated for an error in ijtihad, established by definitive consensus supported by authentic hadith, and Allah alluded to it in His saying: 'If not for a decree from Allah that preceded, there would have touched you for what you took a great punishment' (Al-Anfal 68), meaning if not for the prior decree that there is no accountability for error in ijtihad, punishment would have touched you for taking ransom. And because of this, sin is negated for forgetfulness, as we narrated before. And because of this, the fasting person who eats forgetfully does not break his fast and there is no sin, due to the hadith of Abu Huraira that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever forgets while fasting and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast, for Allah has fed him and given him drink' (agreed upon). And because of this, concessions are granted in travel due to the likelihood of hardship; thus the four-rak'ah prayer is shortened to two, and wiping over khuffs for three days and nights is prescribed, as established by well-known hadith, such as from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'The Messenger of Allah set for the traveler three days and their nights, and for the resident one day and one night.' And because of the absence of hardship in religion, the concession is established for the traveler upon starting the journey before its actualization; analogy would require that concession not be granted until travel is actualized by exiting for three days, but the Law did not consider that and made the basis of concession the start, due to hardship. The evidence is the practice of the Messenger of Allah and the Rightly Guided Caliphs. And if a traveler takes up residence before completing the travel duration, his residence is valid and the rulings of residence apply, including adherence to the stricter ruling, even if he is in a desert, which is not a place for residence, because the cause of the concession (travel) has not been established; thus by removing the intention to travel, it becomes like not having the cause, and the desert does not prevent the continuation of residence but prevents its initiation. And after the travel duration, residence is not valid except in a place suitable for residence, because it is a lifting of travel after its establishment, requiring a new residence, so a suitable place is needed.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage establishes the overarching usul principle that there is no hardship in religion (la haraj fil-deen). The author explains that hardship is a graded concept; only the levels recognized by the Lawgiver create legal effects. The principle is then applied to numerous fiqh rulings: exemption of children and the insane from obligations (with a note about a contrary view of Abu Zayd al-Qadi); exemption of menstruating women from making up prayers but not fasts, with the wisdom of avoiding great hardship; permitting sick people to pray sitting or lying down; negating sin for an error in ijtihad (supported by consensus and Quranic verse); negating sin for forgetfulness; the ruling that a fasting person who eats forgetfully does not break his fast; and the concessions for travelers, including shortening prayers, wiping over khuffs for three days, and the ruling that the concession for travel takes effect upon starting the journey, not after actual travel, to avoid hardship. The passage also covers the case of a traveler who takes up residence before the journey duration ends, emphasizing that the intention to travel must be removed to end the concession. Each example illustrates how the Lawgiver prioritizes ease over strict analogy (qiyas) when hardship would follow.

Key Terms
الحرج (al-haraj): Hardship or difficulty in religious obligations.
المشقة (al-mashaqqa): Difficult situation that triggers concessions (rukhsah).
الرخصة (al-rukhsah): Concession granted in situations of hardship, e.g., travel, illness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are menstruating women exempted from making up missed prayers according to the principle of la haraj?
A) Because they are not required to make up fasts either.
B) Because the hardship of making up prayers due to frequency would be great.
C) Because prayer is not obligatory on them.
D) Because they can pray while menstruating.

Key Takeaway
The principle of la haraj is a living usul tool that allows the Lawgiver to override strict qiyas when necessary to prevent hardship.

Minority and Insanity as Impediments to Obligations
Definition of minority and insanity as impediments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between non-discerning and discerning minors and between minor and major insanity, and apply the Hanafi rules regarding the validity of acts of worship, obligations, and liability for damages.

Core Idea
A non-discerning minor's Islam is invalid; a discerning minor's is valid. Minor insanity requires makeup; major insanity exempts.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut explains how two celestial impediments—minority (sighar) and insanity (junun)—affect legal capacity (ahliyyah) in Hanafi usul. The author distinguishes between stages of minority and degrees of insanity, each with different consequences for ibadat, financial obligations, and marital rulings. The discussion also includes a difference of opinion among Hanafi imams regarding the threshold for minor insanity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two impediments and their category
Recognize that minority and insanity are classified as 'celestial impediments' (awāriḍ samāwiyya) – unavoidable conditions that affect legal capacity.
These are distinct from acquired impediments like ignorance or coercion.
2
Distinguish between non-discerning and discerning minor
A non-discerning minor (ghayr mumayyiz) lacks the ability to understand basic matters; a discerning minor (mumayyiz) has reached tamyiz (around age 7). Each has different rules for the validity of Islam, obligations, and liability.
Tamyiz is a key concept: it enables certain legal acts to be valid.
3
Understand the rulings for each type
For a non-discerning minor: acts of worship are not obligatory, but damages (mutlifāt) are due; the guardian pays maintenance; his Islam is invalid. For a discerning minor: Islam is valid and obligatory; he may perform worship without compulsion; penalties and maintenance are due; jizya is paid for him.
Note the asymmetry: financial liability exists even for non-discerning, but religious obligations do not.
4
Analyze the insanity classification
Insanity is divided into minor (junūn qalīl) and major (junūn kathīr). Minor insanity lasts less than a day and a night (or six hours per Imam Muhammad) and does not nullify obligations – missed acts must be made up. Major insanity suspends all obligations.
The threshold for minor insanity varies by act: salah (day/night), fasting (month), zakat/hajj (year).
5
Note the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad set the limit for minor insanity at six hours, while Abu Yusuf relied on the majority of the period. Abu Hanifa’s view (day/night) is the most common. Also, the passage notes a difference regarding imbecility ('atah) whether it resembles minor with discernment or requires obligations as a precaution.
This ikhtilaf affects whether missed prayers must be made up after short episodes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا وأما العوارض السماوية فمنها الصغر، فإنه إذا لم يبلغ حد التمييز يمنع وجوب العبادات البدنية والمالية، ولا يمنع وجوب المتلفات، لأنه لجبر المتلف ولا وجوب المؤونات من العشر والخراج وصدقة الفطر، ويؤديها الولي من ماله ولا يصح إسلامه لعدم ركنه وهو الاعتقاد، ولا اعتبار لردته لذلك، وأما البالغ حد التمييز فيجب عليه أداء الإيمان عند الإمام علم الهدى الشيخ أبي منصور الماتريدي وسائر مشايخ العراق، ويصح إيمانه باتفاق مشايخنا، ويصح أداء العبادات من غير لزوم، وتجب الغرامات والمؤونات وتدفع عنه الأجزية، وبينونة المرأة الكافرة لإسلامه والمؤمنة للارتداد ليس لأجل الجزاء، بل الاختلاف الديني مفسد للنكاح، ويعرض الإسلام على المميز عند إسلام زوجته دون غير المميز ، بل يؤخر ويصير غير المميز مؤمناً تبعاً لأحد الأبوين أو الدار، وكذا يصير مرتداً بارتدادهما ولحاقهما معه في دار الحرب، وكذا المميز تابع لأحدهما دون المظهر للإسلام أو الكفر.

And among the celestial impediments are: minority (sighar). If a child has not reached the age of discernment (tamyiz), it prevents the obligation of physical and financial acts of worship, but does not prevent the obligation for damages (because it is for compensation), and no obligation for maintenance payments such as 'ushr, kharaj, and sadaqat al-fitr; the guardian pays from his own wealth. His Islam is not valid because the pillar (belief) is missing, so his apostasy is not considered. As for one who has reached the age of discernment, he must perform the acts of faith according to al-Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and the other scholars of Iraq; his Islam is valid by consensus of our scholars, and he can validly perform acts of worship without compulsion. Penalties and maintenance are obligatory, and the jizya is paid for him. The separation of a disbelieving woman due to his Islam and a believing woman due to apostasy is not for retribution, but rather difference of religion invalidates marriage. Islam is presented to the discerning child when his wife accepts Islam, but not to the non-discerning; rather he is delayed, and the non-discerning child becomes a believer by following one of the parents or the abode. Similarly, he becomes an apostate by their apostasy and their joining him in dar al-harb. Likewise, the discerning child follows one of them, except when he manifests Islam or disbelief. ... And among them: insanity and imbecility. Minor insanity does not nullify any acts of worship, so they must be made up. The limit of minor insanity with respect to prayer is if it does not extend a day and a night (because that is likely to be repetitive), and according to Imam Muhammad if it reaches six (hours?), considering its reality. For fasting, a month; for zakat and hajj, a year. From Abu Yusuf: relying on the majority. Major insanity is like minority. However, Islam is presented to his parents when his wife accepts Islam, and it is not delayed as in the case of a child, because it has no known end. A Muslim who becomes insane after Islam is perpetually considered a Muslim and does not follow anyone, and his apostasy is not judged by his parents' apostasy. Imbecility ('atah) is like minor with discernment, so nothing of worship is obligatory on him. In al-Tahrir, quoting from al-Taqwim, it is obligatory on him as a precaution.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage details the rulings for minors and insane persons in Hanafi fiqh. It distinguishes between non-discerning and discerning minors regarding validity of Islam, obligation of worship, and consequences of marriage. For insanity, it differentiates between minor (short-term) and major (long-term) insanity, with different rules for making up missed acts. The passage also includes the ruling for imbecility, which is like minor with discernment.

Key Terms
الصغر (al-sighar): Minority, the state of being a child before puberty.
التمييز (al-tamyiz): The age of discernment, roughly around seven years, when a child can understand basic matters.
الجنون القليل (al-junun al-qalil): Minor insanity that lasts less than a day and a night (or six hours according to Imam Muhammad).
الجنون الكثير (al-junun al-kathir): Major insanity that lasts more than the threshold; it suspends all obligations.
العته (al-'atah): Imbecility, a mental deficiency short of full insanity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is not valid because he lacks belief (i'tiqad).
C) It is valid but he must confirm after puberty.
D) It is invalid only if he is not raised by Muslim parents.

Key Takeaway
A non-discerning minor's Islam is invalid and worship is not obligatory; a discerning minor's Islam is valid and obligatory; minor insanity requires makeup, major insanity suspends obligations.

The Beginning of Travel Concessions: Shuru' vs. Tahqiq
Explaining the basis and application of travel concessions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi principle that travel concessions begin at the commencement of travel (shuru') and apply it to distinguish between the Hanafi position and the opposing analogy.

Core Idea
Travel concessions begin with the intention to travel, not after three days — the Law prioritises the likelihood of hardship over its actuality.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut discusses a key usul principle: when does a travel concession (rukhsa) take effect? The Hanafi school argues that the concession begins as soon as one intends to travel (shuru'), not after actual travel of three days, even though analogy (qiyas) would suggest otherwise. This lesson unpacks the textual evidence and legal reasoning behind this position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the passage
Read the first few lines to see what the passage is about: travel concessions (rukhsa) and their basis.
The passage starts with 'ولذلك خفف في السفر' - 'Therefore, concessions were made in travel.'
2
Extract the key evidence (dalil)
Find the hadith quoted from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and note what it says about duration for the traveler versus the resident.
The hadith states: 'جعل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم للمسافر ثلاثة أيام ولياليهن ويوما وليلة للمقيم'
3
Analyse the Hanafi derivation
The passage says the concession is established 'بالشروع في السفر قبل تحققه' (by the commencement of travel before its actualization). Contrast this with analogy (qiyas) that would require actual travel of three days.
The phrase 'والقياس يقتضي أن لا يرخص إلا بعد تحقق السفر بالخروج ثلاثة أيام' presents the opposing view.
4
Understand the refutation of analogy
The Law (shar') did not consider the qiyas and instead made hardship (haraj) the basis. The hadith itself shows the concession is for the traveler generally.
The passage uses 'لكن لم يعتبره الشرع' to indicate the overriding of analogy.
5
Apply the ruling to scenarios
Note the examples given: if a traveler stays before completing the travel period, he becomes a resident; even in a desert, if he abandons intention, the concession lifts.
This shows that the cause (travel) is based on intention, not location.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولذلك خفف في السفر لأنه مظنة المشقة فشرعت الصلاة الرباعية ركعتين لما و شرع فيه مسح الخف ثلاثة أيام ولياليها والأحاديث في ذلك شهيرة مستفيضة كما عن أمير المؤمنين علي رضي الله عنه جعل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم للمسافر ثلاثة أيام ولياليهن ويوماً وليلة للمقيم و لذلك من عدم الحرج في الدين ثبتت الرخصة للمسافر بالشروع في السفر قبل تحققه والقياس يقتضي أن لا يرخص إلا بعد تحقق السفر بالخروج ثلاثة أيام لأن الشيء لا يثبت قبل تحقق سببه لكن لم يعتبره الشرع وجعل مناط الرخصة الشروع للحرج والدليل عليه التوارث من رسول الله هو من الخلفاء الراشدين رضوان الله تعالى عليهم أجمعين و لو أقام المسافر قبل تمام المدة للسفر صح كونه مقيماً ولزمت عليه أحكام الإقامة من الأخذ بالعزيمة ولو كان في المفازة الأخذ بالعزيمة ولو كان في المفازة مع أنها ليست محلاً للإقامة رفع لها أي للرخصة فإن سببها الذي هو السفر لم يتقرّر فبإزالة نية السفر يصير من إتيان سببها فلا تمنع المفازة فإنها إنما تمنع لابتداء الإقامة دون بقائها وبعدها أي بعد مدة السفر لا تصح الإقامة إلا فيما يصح فيه من العمران لأنه رفع للسفر بعد تحققه وتجديد إقامة فلا بد من موضع يصلح لابتدائها
Therefore, concessions were made in travel because it is a situation of hardship. Thus, the four-cycle prayer was prescribed as two cycles, and wiping over the socks for three days and nights was prescribed, and the hadiths about that are well-known and widespread, as from Amir al-Mu'minin Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, that the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, made for the traveler three days and their nights and for the resident one day and one night. And therefore, due to the absence of hardship in the religion, the concession was established for the traveler by the commencement of travel before its actualization, while analogy would require that concession be given only after the travel is actualized by departing for three days, because a thing is not established before its cause is actualized. But the Law did not consider that, and made the basis of the concession the commencement of travel due to hardship. The evidence for that is the tradition from the Messenger of Allah, who is from the rightly guided caliphs, may Allah be pleased with them all. And if the traveler stays before the completion of the period for travel, his being a resident is valid, and the rulings of residence become binding upon him, including adhering to the main ruling. And if he is in the desert, adhering to the main ruling. And if he is in the desert, even though it is not a place suitable for residence, the concession is lifted from him because its cause, which is travel, was not established. So by removing the intention of travel, he becomes one who has abandoned its cause, so the desert does not prevent [the concession] because it only prevents the beginning of residence, not its continuation. And after that, i.e., after the period of travel, residence is not valid except in a place where it is possible, i.e., in a populated area, because it is the lifting of travel after its actualization and a renewal of residence, so a place suitable for its beginning is necessary.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage discusses the rulings on travel concessions. It states that the sharia grants concessions from the moment one intends to travel, not after physically departing for three days, which would be the analogy. The evidence is a hadith from Ali. It also explains that if a traveler interrupts his travel and stays before completing the travel period, he becomes a resident and must follow the stricter rules of residence, even if he is in a desert. However, if he abandons his intention to travel, the concession is lifted because the cause (travel) no longer exists. After the travel period ends, he cannot resume concession unless he is in a populated area.

Key Terms
رخصة (rukhsa): A concession granted by the Law due to hardship, allowing departure from the original ruling.
عزيمة (azima): The original, stricter ruling that applies in normal circumstances.
مظنة (mazanna): A situation that commonly gives rise to something, here hardship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) After traveling for three days
B) As soon as they intend to travel
C) After leaving the city limits
D) Only when hardship is actual

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school derives the start of travel concessions from the general term in the hadith and the principle of hardship, overriding qiyas.

Legal Effects of Minority
Explains how minority affects obligation (wujub) and validity (sihha) of acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal capacity of non-discerning and discerning minors and identify which obligations apply to each.

Core Idea
Minority prevents obligation of worship but not compensation; discernment partly lifts the bar.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, childhood (sighar) is a natural attribute that affects the mahkum alayh (the person addressed by law). This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut explains how minority suspends certain obligations while imposing others, and how the onset of discernment changes the child's legal standing. We examine the rulings for both non-discerning and discerning minors.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two stages of minority
Identify the non-discerning child (ghayr mumayyiz) and the discerning child (mumayyiz). Note that tamyiz is the ability to understand basic matters, typically around age 7.
2
Identify obligations prevented vs. not prevented for non-discerning
For the non-discerning: physical and financial worship is not obligatory. However, compensatory payments (mutlafat) and levies (mu’an) like 'ushr, kharaj, and sadaqat al-fitr are due from the guardian from the child’s wealth.
The talil is that compensation is to repair loss, not a pure act of devotion.
3
Analyze faith and apostasy for non-discerning
The non-discerning child's Islam or apostasy is invalid because the pillar of belief (i'tiqad) is missing. The child follows the religion of parents or the abode of peace (dar al-Islam).
4
Apply rulings to the discerning child
For the discerning: professing faith (iman) becomes wajib (according to Imam al-Maturidi). Acts of worship are valid (sahih) but not obligatory (la yalzam). Compensations and levies are due. Marriage dissolves if the child embraces Islam while the spouse is non-Muslim, or apostatizes.
The difference in religion dissolves the marriage, not as punishment but due to the impediment of kufr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمنها الصغر فإنه إذا لم يبلغ حد التمييز يمنع وجوب العبادات البدنية والمالية ولا يمنع وجوب المتلفات لأنه لجبر المتلف ولا وجوب المؤونات من العشر والخراج وصدقة الفطر ويؤديها الولي من ماله ولا يصح إسلامه لعدم ركنه وهو الاعتقاد ولا اعتبار لردته لذلك وأما البالغ حد التمييز فيجب عليه أداء الإيمان عند الإمام علم الهدى الشيخ أبي منصور الماتريدي وسائر مشايخ العراق ويصح إيمانه باتفاق مشايخنا ويصح أداء العبادات من غير لزوم وتجب الغرامات والمؤونات وتدفع عنه الأجزية وبينونة المرأة الكافرة لإسلامه والمؤمنة للارتداد ليس لأجل الجزاء بل الاختلاف الديني مفسد للنكاح ويعرض الإسلام على المميز عند إسلام زوجته دون غير المميز بل يؤخر ويصير غير المميز مؤمناً تبعاً لأحد الأبوين أو الدار وكذا يصير مرتداً بارتدادهما ولحاقهما معه في دار الحرب وكذا المميز تابع لأحدهما دون المظهر للإسلام أو الكفر
Among them is minority. If the child has not reached the age of discernment, it prevents the obligation of physical and financial acts of worship, but it does not prevent the obligation of compensatory payments because they are to compensate for loss, nor the obligation of levies such as tithe, land tax, and charity of fitr, which the guardian pays from his wealth. And his Islam is not valid because its pillar is belief, and his apostasy is not considered. As for one who has reached the age of discernment, he must profess faith according to Imam al-Maturidi and the other Iraqi scholars, and his faith is valid by agreement of our scholars. He may validly perform acts of worship without obligation. And penalties and levies become due, and they are paid from his property. And the separation of a disbelieving woman due to his Islam, or a believing woman due to his apostasy, is not for punishment, but rather difference of religion dissolves the marriage. Islam is presented to the discerning child when his wife becomes Muslim, but not to the non-discerning one; rather it is delayed, and the non-discerning child becomes a believer by following one of his parents or the abode. Similarly, he becomes an apostate by their apostasy and joining them in the abode of war. Likewise, the discerning child follows one of them unless he manifests Islam or disbelief.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage discusses the rulings on minority (sighar). It divides children into two categories: non-discerning (ghayr mumayyiz) and discerning (mumayyiz). For the non-discerning, obligatory acts of worship are not required, but compensatory payments for damages are due from their guardian. Their acceptance of Islam or apostasy is invalid due to lack of reason. For the discerning child, they are required to profess faith (according to Maturidi), and their acts of worship are valid but not obligatory. They must pay for damages. Their Islam affects marriage: if a discerning child becomes Muslim, his marriage to a non-Muslim is dissolved. If he apostatizes, similarly. However, the non-discerning child follows his parents in religion.

Key Terms
تمييز (tamyiz): The age or state of discernment when a child can understand basic matters.
مميز (mumayyiz): A child who has reached the age of discernment.
مؤونة (mu'na): Financial levies such as 'ushr, kharaj, and sadaqat al-fitr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are invalid
B) They are valid but not obligatory
C) They are obligatory and binding
D) They are sinful if omitted

Key Takeaway
Minority in Hanafi usul is not a uniform bar; it distinguishes between non-discerning and discerning, each with distinct effects on obligation and validity.

Insanity and Imbecility: Legal Obligations
Differentiating types of madness and their legal effect on worship obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between short and long insanity and apply the Hanafi ruling on whether missed prayers must be made up.

Core Idea
Short-term insanity does not waive worship obligations; long-term insanity does.

This lesson addresses the state of the mahkum alayh (the person subject to rulings) when sanity is compromised. The Hanafi school distinguishes between short and long insanity, and between insanity and imbecility, each with different consequences for the obligation of worship. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for determining qada (making up) obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
Recognize that the text treats two mental conditions: insanity (junun) and imbecility ('atah), and further divides insanity into short (qalil) and long (kathir).
Imbecility is equated to a discerning child, so no worship is obligatory, though a precautionary view exists.
2
Determine the ruling for short insanity
Short insanity does not nullify any acts of worship; all missed acts must be made up. The defining limit for prayer according to Abu Hanifa is that the period does not exceed one day and one night.
The reason given (ta'lil) is that this period is the usual limit for repetition (mazanna al-tikrar).
3
Note intra-madhhab differences
Imam Muhammad considers short insanity as six prayers (effectively a day), while Abu Yusuf relies on the majority of the period. These differences affect how many prayers are forgiven.
The source presents these differing views without explicit preference.
4
Apply limits to other acts
The text provides analogous limits for fasting (one month), zakat and hajj (one year). Long insanity is like being a minor: obligations are fully waived.
Long insanity has no known end, so it removes obligation entirely.
5
Understand imbecility and special cases
Imbecility ('atah) is like a discerning child: no worship is obligatory, though a cautious opinion holds it is obligatory. A Muslim who becomes insane remains Muslim and is not affected by parents' apostasy.
This reinforces that faith is not nullified by insanity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها الجنون والعته فالجنون القليل لا يسقط شيئاً من العبادات فيجب قضاؤها وحد القلة في حق الصلاة ما لم يمتد يوماً وليلة لأنه مظنة التكرار وعند الإمام محمد إذا بلغ ستاً اعتباراً لحقيقته وفي الصوم الشهر وفي الزكاة والحج الحول وعن أبي يوسف الاكتفاء بالأكثر والكثير منه مثل الصغر إلا أنه يعرض على أبويه الإسلام عند إسلام امرأته ولا يؤخر كما في الصبي لأنه ليس له نهاية معلومة والمسلم الذي جن بعد الإسلام يحكم بإسلامه أبداً ولا يتبع أحداً ولا يحكم بردته بردة أبويه والعته مثل الصبا مع التمييز فلا يجب عليه شيء من العبادات وفي التحرير نقلاً عن التقويم أنه يجب عليه العبادات احتياطاً
Among them are insanity and imbecility. As for short insanity, it does not nullify any acts of worship, so they must be made up. The limit of short in respect of prayer is that it does not extend a day and a night, because that is the usual limit for repetition. According to Imam Muhammad, if it reaches six [prayers?] considering its reality. For fasting, the limit is a month. For zakat and Hajj, it is a year. According to Abu Yusuf, reliance on the majority. And long insanity is like minority, except that his parents are presented with Islam when his wife becomes Muslim, and it is not delayed as in the case of a child, because it has no known end. A Muslim who becomes insane after Islam is always judged to be Muslim, and does not follow anyone, and is not judged to be an apostate by his parents' apostasy. Imbecility is like the discerning child, so no acts of worship are obligatory upon him. In al-Tahrir, quoting from al-Taqwim, that acts of worship are obligatory upon him as a precaution.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage treats insanity (junun) and imbecility ('atah). It distinguishes short-term insanity from long-term. Short-term insanity (less than a day and night for prayer) does not forgive missed obligations; they must be made up. There is a difference of opinion: Imam Muhammad sets the limit at six prayers, while Abu Yusuf relies on the majority of the period. Long-term insanity is like minority (sighar) in that it removes obligation, but with the difference that if an insane person's wife becomes Muslim, his parents are presented with Islam (since the insane cannot decide). A Muslim who becomes insane remains Muslim and does not follow his parents in apostasy. Imbecility is like a discerning child: no worship is obligatory, but there is a precautionary opinion that it is obligatory.

Key Terms
الجنون (al-junun): Loss of reason due to mental illness.
العته (al-'atah): Imbecility, a condition of weak intellect but not full insanity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) They are forgiven entirely.
B) They must be made up.
C) Only the first prayer is forgiven.
D) They are optional.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between short and long insanity determines whether missed acts of worship are obligatory to make up.

Forgetfulness as an Excuse in Hanafi Usul: Definition and Legal Effect
Defining nisyān and its effect on sin and legal rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-nisyān, distinguish its effect on sin versus legal rulings, and apply the muthakkir condition to huqūq Allāh cases.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness is always an excuse from sin, but its effect on legal rulings depends on the presence of a reminder (muthakkir).

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, forgetfulness (al-nisyān) is not simply a psychological state; it has a precise legal definition and carries different consequences depending on the context. The passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut lays out this definition and then distinguishes between its impact on sin (ithm) and on legal rulings (hukm), and further divides the rulings between huqūq al-‘ibād and huqūq Allāh. Understanding this classification is essential for constructing accurate legal derivations.

Guided Path
1
Define al-nisyān linguistically and legally
State the definition given in the text: 'adam al-istihḍār waqt al-ḥājah (absence of recollection at the time of need). This is a legal definition that grounds its treatment as an excuse.
The definition is concise and applies to all subsequent distinctions.
2
Distinguish between excuse from sin and excuse from legal ruling
The text says nisyān is an excuse from sin (ithm) absolutely, but not necessarily from the legal ruling (ḥukm). This is the foundational split.
Sin is removed, but the legal consequence may remain.
3
Differentiate huqūq Allāh and huqūq al-‘ibād
In matters between humans (huqūq al-‘ibād), forgetfulness does not cancel compensation (ḍamān). In matters of Allāh's rights, the presence of a muthakkir (reminder) determines whether forgetfulness is an excuse.
Memory-assisting elements like the form of prayer or state of iḥrām count as muthakkir.
4
Apply the distinction to the examples
Work through the given examples: eating in prayer (muthakkir present → no excuse), hunting in iḥrām (muthakkir → no excuse), eating in Ramaḍān (no muthakkir → excuse), early salām (no muthakkir? Actually the first sitting form is not a reminder? The text says salām in first sitting is excused because no muthakkir?), omitting tasmiyah at slaughter (no muthakkir → excuse).
Each example illustrates the general principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : النسيان وهو عدم الاستحضار وقت الحاجة، وهو عذر في حق الإثم مطلقاً، وأما مذكر في حق الحكم فيجب الضمان في حقوق العباد، وأما في حقوق الله تعالى فإن كان مع فلا عذر كأكل الناسي في الصلاة، إذ هيئتها مذكرة وصيد المحرم ناسياً، إذ الإحرام مذكر، وإن لم يكن هناك مذكر يكون عذراً، كالأكل في نهار رمضان ناسياً، وسلام المصلي في القعدة الأولى ناسياً، وترك التسمية عند الذبح ناسياً .
And among them: forgetfulness – it is the absence of recollection at the time of need. It is an excuse with regard to sin absolutely. As for a reminder regarding the ruling, compensation is obligatory in the rights of servants. As for the rights of Allah the Most High, if there is a reminder, there is no excuse, such as the eating of a forgetful person in prayer, since its form is a reminder; and the hunting of a pilgrim forgetfully, since ihram is a reminder. And if there is no reminder, it is an excuse, such as eating in Ramadan during the day forgetfully, and the salam of a praying person in the first sitting forgetfully, and omitting the tasmiyah at the time of slaughter forgetfully.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage defines nisyan (forgetfulness) as the absence of recall at the time of need. It then distinguishes between its effect on sin and on legal rulings. In matters between humans (huquq al-ibad), forgetfulness does not excuse liability; compensation is due. In matters of Allah's rights, if there is a muthakkir (reminder) present, forgetfulness is not an excuse—for example, eating in prayer (since the prayer's very form is a reminder) or hunting while in ihram (since ihram is a reminder). If no reminder exists, forgetfulness is an excuse, such as eating forgetfully in Ramadan or saying salam too early in prayer. This distinction is a key Hanafi principle in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
النسيان (al-nisyān): عدم الاستحضار وقت الحاجة
المذكر (al-muthakkir): ما يذكر الإنسان بالحكم الشرعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the rights of Allah, when does forgetfulness NOT excuse a person from sin?
A) When the forgetfulness is about a small matter
B) When there is a reminder (muthakkir) present
C) When the act is done accidentally
D) Forgetfulness always excuses in rights of Allah

Key Takeaway
Forgetfulness is always an excuse from sin, but in huquq al-ibad it does not cancel compensation; in huquq Allah, if a reminder exists, it is not an excuse.

Sleep's Impact on Legal Capacity and Rulings
Legal analysis of sleep's effects on capacity and rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how sleep affects legal capacity (liability, validity of acts, speech) and apply the Hanafi usul principles to evaluate the internal ikhtilaf on laughter during sleep.

Core Idea
Sleep suspends address and agency, but not liability; laughter in sleep may break prayer but not wudu according to the preferred analogy.

Sleep (nawm) is a common state that temporarily suspends a person's ability to understand divine address and act voluntarily. This raises important usul questions: does sleep cancel legal capacity entirely? How do we rule on actions and speech during sleep? In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on sleep's legal effects, including a well-known internal dispute about whether laughing in sleep invalidates wudu and prayer. The passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahamut' presents multiple views and a preferred tarjih based on analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal nature of sleep
Extract from the passage the definition of sleep and its key characteristic (loss of perception while reason remains). Note why this leads to removal of address (khitab).
Sleep is not total unconsciousness; the sleeping person still has reason but cannot perceive or act voluntarily.
2
Analyze the effects on actions and speech
Determine what rulings apply to a sleeping person's actions (liability for harm) and statements (invalid for legal acts, meaningless for prayer). Link to the usul principle that lack of understanding removes address.
The passage distinguishes between rights of servants (damages) and rights of Allah (acts of worship).
3
Map the internal ikhtilaf on laughter (qahqaha)
List the four opinions transmitted: (1) neither breaks; (2) both break; (3) wudu breaks, prayer builds; (4) prayer breaks, wudu does not. Note which imams are attributed to each view.
Al-Tahrir prefers the fourth: prayer invalidated because it is mere speech; wudu not invalidated because no 'violation' (jinaaya).
4
Understand the analogical reasoning (qiyas)
Reconstruct the qiyas used in al-Tahrir: laughter during sleep is likened to speech (which invalidates prayer absolutely) but not to a hadath (which would invalidate wudu). Note why wudu is not broken: the cause of hadath is relaxation, not laughter.
The text uses 'al-aqyas' indicating preference based on analogical strength.
5
Apply the exclusion for the Prophet
Note the exception: the Prophet's sleep is not a hadath because his heart remains awake. This is a rule specific to him, not generalizable.
This is based on hadith: 'Inna al-nabiyya tanamu 'aynahu wa la yanamu qalbuhu.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها: النوم وهو فترة تعرض للإنسان مع بقاء العقل توجب العجز عن إدراك المحسوسات واستعمال العقل، وعن الأفعال اختياراً، ولما لم يكن النائم فاهماً للخطاب أخر عنه، ولم تعتبر أفعاله في حق الإثم، وأما في حق الحكم فيجب الضمان في حقوق العباد، فيجب ضمان مال تلف بانقلاب النائم وكذا دية إنسان قتل بانقلابه عليه، ولا تعتبر أقواله لا أيضاً، حتى يصح طلاقه وعتاقه وبيعه وغير ذلك، ولا يوصف كلامه بخبر أو إنشاء، بل كألحان الحيوانات، فلا تعتبر قراءته في الصلاة ولا يسقط بها الفرض، صرح به الإمام فخر الإسلام، ولا تفسد بقهقهته الصلاة ولا الوضوء صرح به هو أيضاً، وقيل: يفسدان لعدم فرق النص، وعن الإمام الهمام يفسد الوضوء دون الصلاة كسائر الأحداث، فيتوضأ ويبني، وقيل: لا يفسد الوضوء، وتفسد الصلاة، وفي «التحرير»: هو الأقيس عندي، لأن نقض الوضوء لكونها جناية، ولا جناية فبقي مجرد كلام فتفسد به الصلاة، لأن الكلام يفسدها مطلقاً لعدم فرق النص كالساهي ثم النوم تسترخي منه الأعضاء، وهو سبب لخروج شيء، ولذا جعل الشرع الموجب للاسترخاء منه حدثاً إقامة للسبب مقام المسبب دون غيره، إلا من تنام عيناه ولا ينام قلبه، كالرسول ﷺ، فليس في حقه حدثاً.
And among them: sleep – it is a temporary state that befalls a person while reason remains, causing inability to perceive sensory objects and use intellect, and to perform actions voluntarily. Since the sleeping person does not understand address, it is removed from him, and his actions are not considered in terms of sin. As for the ruling, compensation is obligatory in the rights of servants. For example, compensation is due for property destroyed by the turning over of a sleeping person, likewise blood money for a person killed by his turning over. His statements are not considered either, so his divorce, emancipation, sale, etc. are not valid. His speech is not described as news or composition; rather it is like the melodies of animals. So his recitation in prayer is not considered, and the obligation is not fulfilled by it – Imam Fakhr al-Islam explicitly stated this. And his laughter (qahqaha) does not invalidate prayer nor wudu – he also stated that. But it is said: both are invalidated due to lack of textual distinction. And from Imam al-Hammam: it invalidates wudu but not prayer, like other minor impurities, so he should perform wudu and build upon the prayer. And it is said: it does not invalidate wudu but invalidates prayer. In al-Tahrir: this is more analogical to me, because the nullification of wudu is because it is a violation, but there is no violation, so it remains mere speech, and therefore prayer is invalidated because speech invalidates it absolutely due to lack of textual distinction, like the forgetful person. Then sleep: from it the limbs relax, and it is a cause for something to exit. Therefore, the Law made that which causes relaxation a hadath (ritual impurity), establishing the cause in place of the effect, but not for others. Except for one whose eyes sleep but heart does not, like the Messenger ﷺ, for whom it is not a hadath.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage discusses sleep (nawm) as a temporary state that impairs perception and voluntary action. In Hanafi law, a sleeping person is not addressed by commands, so his actions are not sinful, but he is liable for damages caused by his actions (e.g., property destroyed by turning over). His statements are not valid legal acts (divorce, sale, etc.) because he lacks awareness. His speech is like meaningless sound; thus recitation in prayer does not count. Regarding laughter (qahqaha) during sleep, several opinions are transmitted: Imam Fakhr al-Islam says it does not break wudu or prayer; others say both break; Imam al-Hammam holds it breaks wudu but not prayer; al-Tahrir prefers analogy that breaks prayer because it is mere speech. Sleep itself is a cause of hadath (minor impurity) because it relaxes limbs, but the Prophet ﷺ is excluded as his heart remains awake.

Key Terms
النوم (al-nawm): فترة تعرض للإنسان مع بقاء العقل توجب العجز عن إدراك المحسوسات واستعمال العقل
القهقهة (al-qahqaha): الضحك العالي في الصلاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the view preferred in al-Tahrir regarding a sleeping person's laughter (qahqaha) during prayer?
A) It invalidates both wudu and prayer
B) It invalidates neither wudu nor prayer
C) It invalidates prayer but not wudu
D) It invalidates wudu but not prayer

Key Takeaway
Sleep suspends address and legal agency but not liability for damages; internal Hanafi ikhtilaf on laughter during sleep is resolved by preferring the analogy that invalidates prayer only.

Unconsciousness and its Legal Effect
Defining ighma and its similarity to sleep and insanity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ighma and distinguish its legal effects for short and long durations.

Core Idea
Short unconsciousness is like sleep; long unconsciousness removes prayer obligation like insanity.

This passage introduces the concept of ighma (unconsciousness) and its legal classification in Hanafi usul. It clarifies how the duration of unconsciousness affects the obligation of prayer, drawing analogies to sleep and insanity. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly determining when missed prayers require qada.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Locate the precise legal definition of ighma. Look for the phrase 'آفة يصير بها العقل في كلال' which describes it as an affliction dulling the intellect and suspending perception.
2
Note the comparison to sleep
The passage says 'قليله كالنوم'. Identify the similarities: no address during the state and obligatory qada afterwards.
3
Distinguish the effect on hadath
Why is ighma considered a hadath in all cases? The reasoning is that it causes greater relaxation of limbs than sleep. This means one cannot build upon previous prayer after regaining consciousness.
4
Separate short vs long duration
For 'كثيره', the ruling changes: the obligation of prayer is completely lifted, like insanity. Note the threshold: a month or a year.
5
Recognize the scope
The text says 'دون غيرها', meaning long ighma does not affect obligations other than prayer, due to rarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : الإغماء، وهو آفة يصير بها العقل في كلال وتتعطل بها القوى المدركة، فجعل قليله كالنوم في عدم توجه الخطاب، ووجوب القضاء من غير فرق، إلا أنه لما كان فوقه في إرخاء الأعضاء جعل حدثاً في كل حال، ومنع بناء الصلاة على ما صلى قبله، والكثير منه يمنع وجوب الصلاة، كالجنون دون غيرها لندرة الإغماء شهراً أو سنة، هذا
And among them: unconsciousness (ighma) – it is an affliction whereby the intellect becomes dull and the perceptive faculties become suspended. So its small amount is made like sleep in that address is not directed and qada is obligatory without difference. However, since it is above it in relaxing the limbs, it is made a hadath in all circumstances and prevents building prayer upon what was prayed before. And its large amount prevents the obligation of prayer, like insanity, but not other acts, due to the rarity of unconsciousness lasting a month or a year. This.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage defines ighma (unconsciousness) as a condition that incapacitates intellect and perception. In Hanafi fiqh, a short episode of unconsciousness is treated like sleep: the person is not addressed by commands, and must make up missed acts (qada). However, because it causes more relaxation of the limbs than sleep, it is considered a ritual impurity (hadath) in all cases, and thus one cannot continue prayer after regaining consciousness; one must start over (no building upon previous prayer). Prolonged unconsciousness (e.g., a month or year) negates the obligation of prayer entirely, similar to insanity, but does not affect other obligations like fasting (since such length is rare).

Key Terms
الإغماء (al-ighmā'): آفة يصير بها العقل في كلال وتتعطل بها القوى المدركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It nullifies the obligation of prayer entirely
B) It is like sleep: the person must make up the prayer
C) It is like insanity: no qada required
D) It does not affect prayer at all

Key Takeaway
The legal effect of ighma depends on its duration: short requires qada like sleep, long excuses prayer like insanity, but with a hadath distinction.

Hayd and Nifas: Effect on Obligations of Worship
Usul rule: effect of temporary impurity on obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on how hayd and nifas affect obligations of prayer, fasting, and tawaf, and explain the distinction between postponement and dropping.

Core Idea
Hayd and nifas do not lift legal responsibility but prevent acts requiring purity; prayer is dropped entirely, fasting is postponed.

This lesson examines a key usul al-fiqh rule from the Hanafi school: the effect of menstruation (hayd) and postpartum bleeding (nifas) on the obligation of acts of worship. The passage clarifies that while legal responsibility (taklif) remains, the performance of acts requiring ritual purity is affected. The rule distinguishes between acts that are merely postponed (fasting, tawaf al-ziyara) and those that are completely dropped (prayer, tawaf al-wada') due to hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the effect on taklif
The passage states that hayd and nifas do not remove taklif (legal responsibility). The woman remains a mukallaf but is temporarily unable to perform certain acts.
Taklif is continuous; the impediment is only on performance.
2
Determine which acts require purity
Acts of worship that require taharah (ritual purity) are affected: prayer, fasting, and tawaf. The passage specifies two types of tawaf: ziyara and wada'.
Acts that do not require purity (e.g., du'a) remain valid.
3
Distinguish between postponement and dropping
Fasting and tawaf al-ziyara are postponed (akhir) until after purity returns. Prayer and tawaf al-wada' are dropped (saqat) entirely because of hardship (haraj).
Postponement implies qada is required; dropping means no qada.
4
Analyze the reason for dropping prayer
The passage explains that the very obligation of prayer is dropped because there is no benefit: neither immediate performance nor qada is possible or beneficial.
This is a unique Hanafi position; other schools often require qada.
5
Note the unique Hanafi position
In Hanafi fiqh, prayer is completely waived during hayd and nifas, while many other schools require makeup prayers. This is based on the principle of removing hardship.
This ruling is from the category of 'rukhsah' (concession).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها الحيض والنفاس، وهما لا يمنعان التكليف إلا أنه لا يصح معه من العبادات التي شرط لأدائها الطهارة، فأخر عنهما خطاب الصوم وطواف الزيارة إلى زوالهما، وسقط عنهما الخطاب بالصلاة وطواف الوداع للحرج، وسقط نفس وجوب الصلاة أيضاً حتى لم تبق محلا للوجوب لعدم الفائدة لأنه إما وجوب الأداء وإما وجوب القضاء، وقد انتفيا
And among them: menstruation (hayd) and postpartum bleeding (nifas). They do not prevent taklif (legal responsibility), but acts of worship for which purification is a condition are not valid during them. So the address of fasting and the circumambulation of visitation (tawaf al-ziyara) is postponed until after they cease. And the address of prayer and the circumambulation of farewell (tawaf al-wada') is dropped due to hardship. And the very obligation of prayer is dropped, so that the woman is not a locus of obligation because there is no benefit, since either it is obligation of performance or obligation of qada, and both are negated.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage explains the effect of hayd (menstruation) and nifas (postpartum bleeding) on worship. They do not remove the person from being mukallaf (legally responsible), but they prevent the validity of acts requiring ritual purity (taharah). Therefore, fasting and tawaf al-ziyara (essential pilgrimage circumambulation) are postponed until purity returns. For prayer and tawaf al-wada' (farewell circumambulation), the very command (khitab) is dropped due to hardship, and the obligation of prayer itself is lifted: the woman is not required to perform them nor to make them up later, because neither immediate performance nor qada is possible or beneficial. This is a unique ruling of Hanafi fiqh: prayer is completely waived during these times.

Key Terms
الحيض (al-ḥayḍ): دم يخرج من الرحم في أوقات معتادة
النفاس (al-nifās): الدم الخارج بعد الولادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the obligation of prayer completely dropped for a menstruating woman in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Because she is not legally responsible
B) Because neither immediate performance nor qada is possible or beneficial
C) Because the prayer time is not valid for her
D) Because she can substitute it with other acts

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the obligation of prayer is completely lifted during hayd and nifas, while fasting is postponed and must be made up.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Slavery as a Legal Incapacity: Definition and Effects
Definition and effects of slavery as a legal incapacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define slavery (riqq) as a legal incapacity, state the Hanafi positions on the divisibility of emancipation, and identify the effects of slavery on legal capacity across various domains (testimony, marriage, punishments, property, etc.).

Core Idea
Slavery is an indivisible legal incapacity that halves honors and punishments, and prevents ownership of property and authority.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut provides a comprehensive overview of the legal status of slavery (riqq) in the Hanafi school. It defines riqq as a legal incapacity (عجز شرعي) and examines its various effects on a slave's capacity in matters of testimony, marriage, punishments, property, and more. Understanding these effects is crucial for grasping the Hanafi conceptualization of legal capacity (ahliyyah) and its limitations. The passage also presents an internal Hanafi dispute on whether emancipation (itq) can be partial, illustrating the school's methodology in deriving rulings.

Guided Path
1
Define riqq and its nature
Extract the definition of riqq from the text: it is a legal incapacity that prevents holding authorities (like testimony and judiciary). Note that this incapacity is indivisible – you cannot have partial testimony or partial judiciary.
The indivisibility is a key principle that will apply to many effects.
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on emancipation
Identify the two positions: Imam Abu Hanifa allows partial emancipation because ownership is divisible, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that emancipation is a yielding (muta'wi) and thus cannot be partial. Understand the reasoning behind each view.
This dispute shows how different analogies (qiyas) lead to different rulings.
3
List the effects of slavery on honors and punishments
The text states that slavery 'halves the honors' and 'halves the hudud'. Note specific examples: a slave may marry only two wives, a slave woman's divorce is two pronouncements, her waiting period is two menstrual cycles, and her share of time is half. For punishments, a slave receives half the punishment of a free person.
These effects are derived from the hadith of al-Daraqutni and the principle 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm'.
4
Understand the impact on ownership and authority
Slavery prevents ownership of property (malikiyyat al-mal) but not ownership of self (malikiyyat al-nafs). Hence a slave's confessions regarding hudud are valid, but he cannot own property. Slavery also prevents holding positions of authority (testimony, judiciary, governance). Finally, it diminishes the dhimma: debts are not binding without the slave's person as collateral.
This explains why a slave needs permission for many acts and why his voluntary gifts are not allowed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها الرق وهو عجز شرعي مانع للولايات من الشهادة والقضاء ،وغيرهما، وهذا العجز غير متجزء، فلا يصح أن يقال : شهادة نصفه مقبولة دون النصف الآخر، وكذا القضاء، فكذا ،العتق وهو القوة الشرعية المنافية لهذا العجز غير متجزء، وعلى هذا قالا فلا يتجزأ الإعتاق لأن العتق مطاوعة، فلو تجزأ لزم تجزئته، وقال الإمام التصرف الصادر من السيد في الإعتاق إزالة الملك، ولما كان الملك : متجزئاً كانت الإزالة أيضاً متجزئة، وليس نفس الإعتاق سبباً للعتق حتى يلزم من وجوده، وجوده، بل إعتاق الكل موجب للعتق كالوضوء متجزء، وسببيته لزوال الحدث غير متجزئة، فالرق في معتق البعض كامل فهو كالمكاتب عنده وحر عندهما، ثم الرق ينصف الكرامات، ولهذا يحل له نكاح اثنتين ويكون طلاق الأمة تطليقتين وعدتها حيضتين بالنص الذي رواه الدارقطني : طلاق الأمة تطليقتان وعدتها حيضتان ويكون قسمها نصف قسم الحرة، ويكون حلها قبل الحرة ومنفردة دون بعدها ومعها وكذا ينصف الحدود، فحد العبد نصف حد الحر، لأن الغرم بالغنم والرق يمنع مالكية المال لأنه مملوك نفسه ذليل مهان، فكيف يكون مالكاً، بخلاف مالكية النفس فإنها تبقى على أصل الحرية فتصح أقاريره بالحدود والقصاص والسرقة هالكاً وقائماً في المأذون وفي المحجور عند الإمام فيقطع ورد المال، وعند أبي يوسف : يقطع ولا يردّ، وعند الإمام محمد لا يقطع ولا يرد هذا كله إذا كذبه المولى، ولا يصح إقرار المولى في حقه بحد أو قصاص، وكذا يملك هو ملك النكاح، وإنما يحتاج إلى الإذن لأنه يجب به المال في الذمة والرقبة هي ملك المولى، ولذا لا يملك طلاق امرأته، وكذا يملك دم نفسه فلا يحل للسيد قتله ولا إتلاف عضو من أعضائه، ولذا يقتل الحرّ به عندنا، وأما عند غيرنا فإنما لا يقتل لأجل كرامة الحر، والرق أيضاً يمنع مالكية المنافع، بل المنافع كلها للسيد إلا ما استثنى منها كالصوم والصلاة، فلا يخرج للجمعة والعيدين والحج إلا بإذن السيد وكذا الجهاد لا يخرج له إلا بإذنه أو بإذن الشرع عند النفير العام، وإنما يصح أمانة مأذوناً، لأنه مالك للغنيمة، فينفذ عليه بالذات وعلى غيره بالتبع، والرق يمنع الولايات فلا تصح الشهادة على أحد ولا قضاؤه ولا حكومته، وكذا إمارته، والرق ينقص الذمة فلا يجب على ذمته شيء إلا بضم مالية رقبته إليه فلا يجب بإقراره المال في الحال، إلا في المأذون للضرورة، وتجب الغرامات لأجل الجنايات في الذمة، فتؤدى فتباع رقبته إلا أن يفدي المولى، وكذا تباع رقبة المأذون فيما بقي من الدين بعد الأداء من الكسب أو لم يكن له كسب ولا تجوز تبرعاته من أكسابه لأنه ملك المولى أو الدائنين، ولا تقبل هديته إلا اليسير بالنص، ولا يجوز له التسري من إمائه وإن كان مكاتباً .
And among them: slavery (riqq) – it is a legal incapacity that prevents authorities such as testimony, judiciary, and others. This incapacity is indivisible, so it cannot be said that half of his testimony is accepted and the other half not; likewise for judiciary. Similarly, emancipation (itq) – which is the legal strength contrary to this incapacity – is indivisible. On this basis, the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: emancipation cannot be partial, because emancipation is a yielding; if it were partial, its partiality would be necessary. And Imam (Abu Hanifa) said: the act of the master in emancipation is removal of ownership; since ownership is divisible, its removal is also divisible. And emancipation itself is not the cause of freedom such that its existence necessitates the existence of freedom; rather, emancipating the whole necessitates freedom, like wudu which is divisible but its causation of removing impurity is indivisible. So the slavery in a partially emancipated slave is complete; he is like a mukatab according to Imam and free according to them. Then, slavery halves the honors: hence it is permissible for him to marry two women, and the divorce of a slave woman is two divorces, and her waiting period is two menstrual cycles, based on the text narrated by al-Daraqutni: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two divorces and her waiting period is two menstrual cycles.' And her share (in division of nights) is half that of a free woman. And she is permissible before the free woman and alone, not after or with her. Likewise, it halves the hudud punishments: the punishment of a slave is half that of a free person because 'loss is according to gain'. And slavery prevents ownership of property because he is owned himself, low and contemptible – how can he be an owner? Unlike ownership of himself, which remains based on original freedom, so his confessions regarding hudud, qisas, and theft are valid whether he is bankrupt or solvent, in the case of a permitted slave (ma'dhun) and a restricted one (mahjur). According to Imam: he is amputated and he returns the money; according to Abu Yusuf: he is amputated and does not return; according to Imam Muhammad: he is not amputated and does not return – all this if the master denies him. And the master's confession against him regarding a hadd or qisas is not valid. He also possesses ownership of marriage (i.e., the right to marry) but needs permission because it necessitates financial obligation in the dhimma, and the slave's person is the property of the master. Therefore, he does not own divorce of his wife. Similarly, he owns his own life: it is not permissible for the master to kill him or destroy any of his limbs, hence a free person is killed for him (a slave) according to our school. According to others, the free is not killed due to the honor of the free. Slavery also prevents ownership of benefits; rather all benefits belong to the master except what is excluded, like fasting and prayer. So he may not go out for Friday prayer, the two Eids, or Hajj except with the master's permission; likewise jihad only with his permission or with the permission of the law in a general mobilization. His grant of aman (safe conduct) is valid only if he is permitted (ma'dhun) because he owns the spoils; it is effective on himself directly and on others indirectly. Slavery prevents authorities: his testimony is not accepted against anyone, nor his judiciary, nor his governance, nor his leadership. Slavery diminishes the dhimma (legal personality): nothing is binding on his dhimma except by joining the value of his person to it, so money is not immediately binding by his confession, except in the case of a permitted slave out of necessity. And penalties due to torts become binding on the dhimma, then they are paid, and his person is sold unless the master ransoms him. Similarly, the person of a permitted slave is sold for what remains of debt after payment from his earnings or if he has no earnings. His voluntary gifts from his earnings are not allowed because it is the property of the master or the creditors. His gift is not accepted except for a small amount by text. And he is not allowed to take a concubine from his female slaves even if he is a mukatab.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive exposition of the legal effects of slavery (riqq) in Hanafi fiqh. It begins by stating that slavery is a legal incapacity that prevents holding positions of authority (testimony, judiciary, etc.) and that this incapacity is indivisible – you cannot have partial testimony. The same applies to emancipation (itq): according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, it cannot be partial, but Imam Abu Hanifa allows partial emancipation because ownership is divisible. The 'halving' effect of slavery is then detailed: it halves honors (marriage limits, divorce waiting period, share of time with wife) based on a hadith from al-Daraqutni. It also halves hudud punishments. Slavery prevents ownership of property (malikiyyat al-mal) but not ownership of self (malikiyyat al-nafs), so a slave's confession regarding hudud is valid, with differences among the early Hanafi masters. The slave owns his own life; the master cannot kill him. Slavery prevents ownership of benefits (except worship), so the slave needs permission for congregational and Hajj. It prevents legal authorities. It diminishes the dhimma such that debts are not immediately binding without the slave's person as collateral. This is a key usul topic showing how a legal attribute (riqq) affects various rulings.

Key Terms
الرق (al-riqq): عجز شرعي مانع للولايات من الشهادة والقضاء وغيرهما
العتق (al-'itq): القوة الشرعية المنافية للرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of slavery on a slave's testimony?
A) A slave's testimony is accepted in non-criminal cases.
B) A slave's testimony is never accepted because slavery is an incapacity preventing authority.
C) A slave's testimony is accepted if he is a mukatab.
D) A slave's testimony is accepted only in matters of hajj.

Key Takeaway
Slavery is an indivisible legal incapacity that fundamentally alters legal capacity across multiple domains, halving honors and punishments, and preventing ownership of property and authority.
