Hajr al-Marid: The Hanafi Restriction on the Sick Person's Gratuitous Transactions
Explains the Hanafi rule on hajr al-marid and its exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi ruling on hajr al-marid and apply it to distinguish valid from invalid transactions of a terminally ill person.

Core Idea
Sickness limits property disposal only for gratuitous acts; fair sales and proper marriages remain valid.

Terminal illness does not remove one's capacity for worship or obligation, but it does trigger a restriction on property transactions to protect creditors and heirs. The Hanafi usul lays out a nuanced rule: gratuitous transfers are limited, while fair exchanges are free. Let's unpack the reasoning and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal capacity of the sick person
Note that sickness is a type of incapacity but does not negate understanding or obligation (taklif). The sick person remains mukallaf for acts of worship, albeit with allowances for hardship.
This sets the baseline: capacity for transactions remains, but with restrictions.
2
Distinguish gratuitous vs. non-gratuitous transactions
Gratuitous transactions (tabarru'at) like gifts, charity, and freeing slaves are restricted. Non-gratuitous transactions like sale at fair price and marriage with proper dower are permitted without restriction.
The key test: is there equivalent compensation ('iwad)?
3
Apply the two limitations based on the right of creditors and heirs
For creditors, the sick person cannot give away any of his property gratuitously. For heirs, he cannot give away more than one-third of his estate gratuitously. This protects the rights of both parties that materialize upon death.
The restriction applies only when death is imminent (marad al-mawt).
4
Classify the consequences of restricted contracts
If a restricted contract is subject to annulment (e.g., a gift not yet delivered), it can be rescinded. If it is not annullable (e.g., freeing a slave), it is treated as a suspended contract (mu'allaq) – it takes effect after death subject to estate rules (e.g., the freed slave must earn the value or excess).
This ensures the rights of creditors and heirs are not entirely defeated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها المرض، وهو نوع من العجز ولا ينافي فهم الخطاب وأهلية العبادات، ولذا لا ينافي التكليف إلا أنه لما كان نوعاً من العجز شرعت العبادات على حسب المكنة وأخر ما لا قدرة عليه أو ما فيه حرج، ثم هو سبب للموت، وهو سبب الخلافة في المال، ولذا المال، ولذا حجر المريض عن التبرعات والتصرفات المشتملة عليها في كل المال لحق الغرماء والثلثين لحق الورثة، لكن إذا اتصل به الموت، وأما التصرفات التي ليس فيها تبرع كالبيع بمثل القيمة والنكاح بمهر المثل لا يمنع منه، والشرع إذ أبطل التوارث علمنا أنه يتعلق بالنظر إليه حق الورثة في الصورة أيضاً، فمنعنا من الإقرار له والبيع معه ، ثم إنه تفسخ العقود المحجورة عليه إن كانت قابلة للفسخ، وإلا فحكمها حكم المعلق كإعتاق العبد من التركة المستغرقة بالدين أو قيمته تزيد على الثلث، فإنه يعتق بعد الموت، ويسعى في قيمته في الأول والزائد على الثلث في الثاني، هذا : ومنها الموت.
And among them is sickness, and it is a type of incapacity and does not contradict understanding of address and capacity for worship, so it does not contradict obligation, but since it is a type of incapacity, acts of worship are prescribed according to ability, and what one is unable to do or involves hardship is delayed. Then it is a cause of death, and death is a cause of succession in property, so the sick person is restricted from gratuitous transfers and transactions including them in all his property for the right of creditors, and from two-thirds for the right of heirs, but when death is imminent. As for transactions that are not gratuitous, like sale for the equivalent value and marriage with an appropriate dower, they are not prevented. And since the Law forbade inheritance, we know that the right of heirs is also attached to his affairs, so we prevent him from acknowledging (a debt) and selling with him. Then the restricted contracts are voidable if they are subject to annulment; otherwise their ruling is like that of suspended contracts, like freeing a slave from an estate consumed by debt or whose value exceeds one-third, for then he is freed after death and must earn his value in the first case and the excess over one-third in the second. This: and among them is death.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage explains the category of hajr (restriction) due to terminal illness (marad al-mawt). The author first defines sickness as a type of incapacity that does not remove the capacity for understanding address or worship, thus the sick person remains obligated (mukallaf). However, because sickness is a cause of death, and death transfers property to heirs, the sick person's freedom to dispose of his property is limited: he may not make gratuitous transfers (tabarru'at) from the entire property for the benefit of creditors, nor from more than one-third for the benefit of heirs, since these rights materialize upon death. But non-gratuitous transactions like a fair sale or marriage with proper dower are permitted. If the sick person makes a restricted transaction, it may be annullable; if not annullable (like freeing a slave), it takes effect after death subject to the rules of the estate (e.g., the slave must work to pay off debt or excess over one-third). This is a complete legal unit showing the Hanafi position on the restriction of the sick person.

Key Terms
حجر المريض (hajr al-marīḍ): Legal restriction placed on a terminally ill person limiting gratuitous transactions to protect creditors and heirs.
تبرع (tabarru‘): A gratuitous transaction without equivalent compensation, like gifts or charity.
معلق (mu‘allaq): A suspended contract that takes effect upon a future condition, such as death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, which transaction of a terminally ill person is valid without restriction?
A) Gifting his entire property to a stranger
B) Selling his car for fair market value
C) Freeing a slave worth more than one-third of his estate
D) Acknowledging a debt to a relative

Key Takeaway
The terminally ill person's property rights are limited only for gratuitous acts; fair exchanges and proper marriages remain fully valid, while certain restricted transactions are either annulled or become suspended obligations after death.

Effect of Death on Legal Liability (Dhimma)
States the general rule on dhimma after death and exceptions via assets

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that death empties the dhimma of obligations except acts of worship, and identify which rights survive through attachment to assets.

Core Idea
Death destroys taklif, but acts of worship remain on the dhimma; financial rights attach to the estate, not the dhimma.

This lesson explores the foundational Hanafi principle that death terminates legal capacity for obligations. However, some rights persist, not on the dhimma but attached to specific property or the estate. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping later disputes like kafala.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
State that death destroys the basis of taklif (legal obligation) and empties the dhimma of obligations except those attached to a specific item.
The text uses a linguistic analysis of 'illa' to clarify the exception.
2
Recognize what remains on the dhimma
Acts of worship (financial and physical) not performed remain on the dhimma, such as missed prayers or unpaid zakat.
This is unique to Hanafi: the dhimma holds a liability for unperformed worship.
3
Identify what does NOT remain on the dhimma
Things attached to a specific item (deposits, usurped goods, debts, bequests) do not remain on the dhimma but the right-holder may take them from the estate.
The text emphasizes that the dhimma is empty for these, but the heirs must satisfy them from the estate.
4
Understand the heirs' duty
Heirs must return deposits and usurped goods, pay debts, and execute bequests from the estate before distribution.
Preparation (kafan) is given priority over debts and bequests by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الموت هادم لأساس التكليف لأنه عن إتيان العبادات أداء وقضاء، ولأنه كله عجز ذهب من دار الابتلاء إلى دار الجزاء فلا يبقى على ذمة الميت إلا ما كان متعلقاً بعين) لعل إلا ها هنا صفة بمعنى غير هو فاعل لا يبقى، ولا يصح أن يكون استثناء مفرغاً متصلاً، فإنه ليس على ذمة الميت ما كان متعلقاً بعين أيضاً، ولا أن يكون منقطعاً أيضاً، لأنه مفرغ يصح فالحاصل أنه لا يبقى على ذمة الميت غير المتعلق بعين كالعبادات مالية كانت أو بدنية، وأما المتعلق بعين أو مال فلا يبقى على ذمته أيضاً، لكن لصاحب الحق أن يأخذه من العين أو المال كالودائع والغصوب) فإن للمودع والمغصوب منه أن يأخذه كما كان في الحياة وعلى الورثة أن يردوه (أو) غير ما كان متعلقاً بمال تركه كالديون فإن الدائنين لهم أن يأخذوها منه، وعلى الورثة أن لا يتصرفوا من دون الأداء والوصايا) فإن الموصى له يكون خليفة في ملك الثلث والتجهيز ويقدم) على الديون والوصايا (بالإجماع) وإذا لم يبق في ذمة الميت شيء أصلاً فلا تصح الكفالة بما عليه من الدين (بعد الموت عند من الدين ( بعد الموت عند أبي حنيفة) إذا لم يترك وفاء من المال (لأنها ) أي الكفالة ضم الذمة إلى الذمة في المطالبة فيجوز للدائن مطالبة أيهما شاء (ولا (مطالبة ها هنا على الأصيل فلا ضم) فيها قال مطلع الأسرار قدس سره: ها هنا قولان: الأول : أن الكفالة ضم الذمة إلى الذمة في المطالبة، والآخر أنها ضم الذمة إلى الذمة في هذا الاستدلال إلا إذا ترجح القول الأول، وما في «الهداية» الأول أصح دعوى الدين، فلا يتم بن من غير دليل، وما في بعض شروحه أن جعل دين واحد على اثنين غير معقول، ولا ضرورة ملجئة إليه، ففيه أنه أنه يجوز أن يصير مثل الواجب على الكفاية فإنه كان واجباً على الأصيل، ثم بالالتزام وجب على الكفيل، وأيهما أدّى سقط عن صاحبه كوجوب قبول الإمامة الكبرى على كل واحد من الصالحين لها وبقيام أحدهم بها سقطت عن الآخرين، بل حرمت، هذا: ولم يظهر لهذا العبد وجه التوقف على القول الأول، فإنه يمكن أن يقرر على القول الثاني بأنها ضم الذمة إلى الذمة في الدين والدين قد سقط بالموت، فإن ذمة الميت غير صالحة للاشتغال بالواجبات، وإذ لا دين في الذمة فلا ضم فتدبر، فإنه لا يرد عليه شيء إلا ما تقرر في مذهبهما (وعندهما تصح الكفالة عن الميت ولو لم يترك مالاً ولا كفيلاً وبه قالت الأئمة الثلاثة، لحديث جابر) قال : كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يصلي على رجل مات وعليه دين، فأتي بميت فقال : علیه دین؟ قالوا نعم دیناران قال : صلوا على صاحبكم» فقال أبو قتادة الأنصاري هما) (علي) يا رسول الله (فصلى عليه) رواه النسائي وفي «صحيح البخاري» عن سلمة الأكوع أن النبي يا الله أتي بجنازة ليصلي عليها فقال : هل عليه من دين» قالوا: نعم، قال: صلوا على صاحبكم فقال أبو قتادة علي دينه، فصلى عليه ولأن الموت لا يبرىء) الميت عن الدين (ولذا يطالب به في الآخرة إجماعاً) ولو لم يكن عليه دين لما طولب (و) لذا يصح التبرع بالأداء ولو لم يكن عليه دين فأي شيء يؤدي، وإذا ثبت على ذمة الميت دين فتصح الكفالة ويلزمه المطالبة : لأنه في عالمنا بخلاف الأصيل والجواب أنه أي قول أبي قتادة ( يحتمل العدة) وفي «التحرير» وهو الظاهر إذ لا تصح الكفالة للمجهول، وفي «التقرير» وهو مشكل لما في لفظ عن جابر للحاكم وقال : صحيح الإسناد فجعل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : هي عليك وفي مالك الميت عنها بريء فقال : نعم، فصلى عليه أقول ظاهره ينافي الكفالة إذ المكفول عنه لا يبرأ كما ينافي العدة كذا في «الحاشية»، فإن قلت : لعله أراد كأنه للكفالة؟ قلت : نقول هذا بعينه في العدة، أي كأنه برىء تأكيداً في العدة (و) أيضاً يحتمل أن يكون إقراراً بكفالة سابقة وفيه ما فيه إشارة إلى ما في رواية صحيح ابن حبان فقال أبو قتادة : أنا أكفل به فأتى بالوفاء فصلى عليه ، وكان عليه ثمانية وعشرون درهماً أو ثمانية عشر درهماً، وفي كونه منافياً للوعد كما في التقرير»، نظر لجواز المبالغة في وفاء الوعد، كما هو المتعارف كذا في الحاشية، فإن قلت هذا صرف عن الظاهر من غير ضرورة، قلت: الضرورة ما في «التحرير» من لزوم جهالة المكفول عنه فتأمل فيه والمطالبة الأخروية باعتبار الإثم أي إثم عدم إيصال الحق إلى المستحق (لا تفتقر إلى بقاء الذمة) إذ الإثم باعتبار عدم الامتثال بالإتيان بالواجب، والمراد من بقاء الذمة بقاء ما تتوجه إليه به المطالبة، وإلا فالدين في الذمة باق، ولذا يعطى الدائن حسنات المديون عوضاً عنه يوم القيامة، كما ورد في الخبر الصحيح وصحة التبرع) إنما هو البقاء الدين من جهة من الدين (له) وهو الدائن (فإن السقوط بالموت لضرورة فوت (المحل لا لأنه وصل حق الدائن ( فيظهر ) هذا السقوط (في حق من عليه دون حق من له فيبقى من جهته والحاصل أن الدين وإن كان ثابتاً على ذمة الميت، ولذا
Issue: Death demolishes the foundation of obligation because it prevents performing worship both in time and as makeup, and because it is total incapacity; one goes from the abode of trial to the abode of recompense. So nothing remains on the deceased's dhimma except what is attached to a specific [item]. (The word 'illa' here is a description meaning 'other than', and it is the subject of 'la yabqa' — it is not a continuous exceptive, because what is attached to a specific does not remain either, nor a discontinuous one because it is a valid empty exceptive.) The conclusion: nothing remains on the deceased's dhimma other than what is not attached to a specific thing, like acts of worship, whether financial or physical. As for what is attached to a specific item or property, it does not remain on his dhimma either, but the right-holder may take it from the item or property, like deposits and usurped goods — the depositor and the one whose property was usurped may take it as in life, and the heirs must return it. Or what is attached to his estate left as property, like debts: the creditors may take it from the estate, and the heirs must not transact without paying. And bequests: the legatee becomes a deputy in ownership of one-third, and preparation (kafan) is given priority over debts and bequests by consensus. And if nothing remains on the deceased's dhimma at all, then kafala for the debt owed by him after death is not valid according to Abu Hanifa, if he did not leave sufficient assets, because kafala is joining dhimma to dhimma for claim; the creditor may demand from either. Here there is no claim against the principal, so no joining. ... (continues with dispute and hadith) ... And the result is that the debt, even though it is established on the deceased's dhimma, therefore it appears...
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a detailed mas'ala on the effects of death on legal liability. The author first establishes that death terminates the capacity for obligation (taklif) regarding worship, whether performed or missed. However, certain rights remain attached not to the deceased's dhimma (which is now empty) but to specific items or the estate: deposits, usurped goods, debts, and bequests. The heirs must satisfy these from the estate. Then the text enters a major dispute regarding kafala (suretyship) for a debt of the deceased. According to Abu Hanifa, if the deceased left no assets, kafala is invalid because the principal's dhimma is empty — there is no debt to join. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the other three imams hold kafala valid, based on the hadith of Jabir where Abu Qatada guaranteed the debt of a deceased man, and the Prophet (ﷺ) led the funeral prayer. The text engages in a detailed analysis of the hadith, considering interpretations that it might be a promise (wa'd) rather than kafala, or an acknowledgment of a prior kafala. The author refutes these and argues that the debt remains on the dhimma for afterlife accountability, even if the legal claim here is on the estate. Ultimately, the Hanafi position of Abu Hanifa is presented alongside the stronger alternative view of the Sahibayn, with the text leaning toward the latter in some expositions.

Key Terms
ذمة (dhimma): Legal liability or capacity to owe obligations; after death, it becomes empty except for certain residual rights attached to assets.
التكليف (taklīf): Legal obligation; death destroys the foundation of taklif because the person is no longer in the abode of trial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of obligation remains on the deceased's dhimma after death?
A) Debts
B) Deposits
C) Acts of worship not performed
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
Death empties the dhimma for new obligations, but missed acts of worship remain as a liability, while financial rights attach to the estate.

The Dispute on Kafala for a Deceased Person's Debt
Presents the ikhtilaf on kafala after death with dalil from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute over kafala for a deceased person's debt, state Abu Hanifa's position and its basis in the emptiness of the dhimma, and evaluate the opposing position of the Sahibayn and other imams based on the hadith of Jabir.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa says kafala is invalid because the dhimma is empty; Sahibayn and others say the hadith of Jabir proves it valid.

Building on the previous lesson's rule that death empties the dhimma, this lesson dives into a classic usul dispute: can a person guarantee (kafala) the debt of a deceased if no assets are left? The text presents Abu Hanifa's logical argument, the opposing evidence from the hadith of Jabir and Abu Qatada, and the Hanafi responses through interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Recall the general rule
After death, the dhimma is empty except for acts of worship; financial debts are attached to the estate, not the dhimma.
Abu Hanifa applies this strictly to kafala.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Kafala requires joining two dhimmahs for a claim. Since the deceased's dhimma has no debt, there is nothing to join, so kafala is invalid if no assets are left.
His dalil is logical reasoning based on the nature of dhimma.
3
State the opposing position and proof
The Sahibayn and the other three imams hold kafala valid based on the hadith where Abu Qatada guaranteed a deceased's debt, and the Prophet (ﷺ) prayed over him.
They also argue that death does not extinguish the debt; it remains in the dhimma for the afterlife.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The text offers interpretations: Abu Qatada's statement could be a promise (wa'd) or acknowledgment of a prior kafala, not new suretyship.
The author also argues that afterlife accountability does not require a worldly dhimma.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text seems to favor the Sahibayn's view, as the hadith is strong and the arguments for reinterpretation are weak.
The mufta bihi in later Hanafi practice leans toward validity, but the lesson presents both sides.

This passage is a detailed mas'ala on the effects of death on legal liability. The author first establishes that death terminates the capacity for obligation (taklif) regarding worship, whether performed or missed. However, certain rights remain attached not to the deceased's dhimma (which is now empty) but to specific items or the estate: deposits, usurped goods, debts, and bequests. Key Terms
كفالة (kafāla): Suretyship; joining one's dhimma to another's for the claim of a debt.
عدة (ʿidda): Promise; a commitment to do something in the future, not a binding guarantee.

Definition of Language and Types of Indication (Mutabaqah and Tadammun)
Defining language and analyzing types of linguistic indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define language as a word indicating by convention, distinguish between mutabaqah and tadammun, and evaluate the claim that they are identical for simple words using the Hanafi critique.

Core Idea
Mutabaqah and tadammun are not identical for simple words — parts become understood only after mental analysis.

This passage from the third article on linguistic principles establishes the foundational definition of language (al-lughah) as a word that indicates by convention (wad'). It then introduces the two primary types of indication: mutabaqah (whole meaning) and tadammun (part meaning). A key debate arises: some scholars argue that for simple words, these two indications are essentially one. The author critically examines this claim, revealing the subtlety required in usul al-fiqh analysis.

Guided Path
1
Define language and its condition
Identify the author's definition: 'a word that indicates by convention'. Note that the condition is wad' (positing), not natural or rational indication. This is the standard Hanafi usul definition.
This definition excludes natural sounds (e.g., coughing) and logical implications (e.g., smoke indicates fire).
2
Distinguish mutabaqah and tadammun
Mutabaqah is indication of the whole meaning (e.g., 'house' indicates the entire building). Tadammun is indication of a part (e.g., 'house' implies 'room' as part of the whole). These correspond to the logical relations of identity and inclusion.
A third type, iltizam (implication by correlation), is not discussed here but exists.
3
Understand the claim of identity for simple words
Some scholars claim that for simple words (like 'water'), mutabaqah and tadammun are identical because the word is understood as a unitary image without distinguishing parts. They argue that the whole and part are understood simultaneously.
This view would simplify linguistic analysis but is challenged.
4
Analyze the Hanafi critique
The author counters: if the whole is truly undetailed, then parts are not in the mind at the moment of understanding. Only after analytical reflection do parts emerge. Thus the two indications are distinct after analysis, though phenomenologically one before analysis. The critique is expressed as 'fihi nazar zahir' (apparent flaw).
This shows the Hanafi emphasis on precise mental processes in usul al-fiqh.
5
Apply to mushtarak (homonyms)
The discussion extends to common words (al-mushtarak), where multiple meanings are due to multiple conventions. A single convention unifies multiple meanings, but two opposites cannot be united under one convention. This illustrates the principle that convention (wad') determines indication.
Example: 'ayn can mean eye, spring, or gold coin - each a separate convention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية (من لطف الله سبحانه إحداث (اللغون الظاهر اللغين فإنه جمع سالم للغة إما على خلاف القياس نحو سنون أو بناء على أن الناقص قياسه أن يجمع سالماً وإن كان غير عاقل على اختلاف الرأيين ((فمنها أي من اللغين (تنشعب غصون (الفنون من العربية والفارسية والهندية. ويحصل من احداثه عرض عريض من إفادة ما في الضمير واستفادة ما في ضمير آخر غيره وكيفيتهما من الكيفيات والمزايا والصراحة والكناية والتشبيه والحقيقة والمجاز والنظم والنثر وغيرها، فتحدث فنون عجيبة فله شكر عظيم غير (ممنون على هذه النعمة العظمى، ومن جملة أنحاء الشكر إظهار النعمة فالبحث عنها نوع من الشكر (وهو) أي اللغة والتذكير باعتبار الخبر (اللفظ الدال وضعاً وهي أي الدلالة المفهومة من الدال (في كمال معناه مطابقة وفي جزئه تضمن وهما واحدة بالذات في دلالة المفردات التي يتحققان فيها لا أنهما متحدتان مطلقاً حتى يلزم الانتقاض بالألفاظ الموضوعة في مقابلة البسائط (فإن) اللفظ المفرد لا يدل إلا على الكل إجمالاً، والكل إنما يتعقل بصورة وحدانية لا تفصيل فيها) للأجزاء (إلا بعد التحليل أي بعد تحليل الذهن تلك الصورة فوقت فهم المعنى المطابقي من اللفظ ليست صور الأجزاء في الذهن، فليست الأجزاء منفهمة بنفسها إلا بعد التحليل، فليس التضمن مغايراً للمطابقة إلا بعد التحليل، فهذه الدلالة من حيث إنها على صورة الكل مطابقة، ومن حيث إنها منحلة إلى الأجزاء تضمن كذا قالوا وفيه نظر ظاهر، فإنهم إن أرادوا بالإجمال المدلول من اللفظ المفرد التوحد الحقيقي بحيث لا يكون فيها كثرة إلا بعد التحليل، فلا نسلم أن اللفظ المفرد لا يدل إلا على هذا الإجمال، إنما هو دعوى محض، مثل المدعي، فلا بد من الإبانة، وإن أرادوا الصور الكثيرة الملحوظة بلحاظ واحد أو المعروضة للوحدة الاجتماعية، فمسلم أن المفرد لا يدل إلا على هذا الإجمال ولا يلزم منه المطلوب من اتحاد الدلالتين، فإن الصور في الحقيقة كثيرة (۱) وفهم أشياء كثيرة معروضة لنوع من الوحدة فتدبر، فإن قلت : فما يصنعون بالمفرد المشترك فإنه يدل على أشياء كثيرة؟ أجاب بقوله : (وفي المفرد المشترك إنما تتعدد الصور) عند انفهام المعنى (التعدد الوضع ونحن إنما ندعي وحدة الصورة عند وحدته كما قال (وأما الوضع الواحد فكأنه موحد للكثير) فلا يحصل الكثير في الذهن إلا بعد التوحد (ومن ها هنا لا لفظ) یری واحد موضوع الضدين بوضع واحد وإن جاز) وضعه المختلفين، كالبيت) إذ لا يحصل من الضدين أمر واحد وإلا لزم من وجوده في موضوع وجود الضدين المندمجين (1). (۱) قوله : (وفهم أشياء كثيرة) الخ كذا بالأصل وتأمل، كتبه مصححه.
The third article on linguistic principles: From the grace of Allah is the creation of languages. 'Al-lughun' appears to be a sound plural of 'lughah', either irregularly like 'sinun' or based on the rule that the deficient noun is soundly pluralized even if non-rational, according to two opinions. From these languages branch out the various arts: Arabic, Persian, Hindi. From their creation arises a broad spectrum of conveying what is in the mind and understanding what is in another's mind, and the manners thereof in terms of qualities, advantages, explicitness, implicitness, simile, literal and figurative, prose and poetry, etc. Thus marvelous arts are produced, so He deserves immense gratitude for this great blessing. Among the forms of gratitude is manifesting the blessing, so research about it is a kind of gratitude. And it (the language) is a word that indicates by convention. And the indication understood from the indicator is either in the complete meaning (correspondence) or in its part (implication). And they are one in essence in the indication of simple words where they are realized, not that they are absolutely identical, otherwise it would lead to contradiction with words placed for simple entities. For the simple word only indicates the whole in an undetailed manner, and the whole is only conceived as a unitary image without detail of parts except after analysis. So at the moment of understanding the correspondence meaning, the images of the parts are not in the mind; thus the parts are not understood by themselves except after analysis. So implication is not different from correspondence except after analysis. So this indication is correspondence in that it is upon the image of the whole, and implication in that it is analyzable into parts. Thus they said, but there is an apparent flaw in it, because if they mean by the non-detailed (ijmal) of the simple word a real unity that contains no multiplicity except after analysis, then we do not accept that the simple word only indicates this non-detailed (concept); it is a pure claim like the claimant, so proof is needed. And if they mean the multiple images perceived in one regard or presented for social unity, then it is acceptable that the simple word only indicates this non-detailed (concept) but the required conclusion of the unity of the two indications does not follow, for the images are in reality multiple and the understanding of multiple things presented with a kind of unity, so ponder. If you say: What do they do with the common word (mushtarak) for it indicates multiple things? He answers: In the common word, the images are multiplied due to multiplicity of convention. And we only claim unity of image when the convention is one, as he said: 'As for a single convention, it seems to unify the many.' So the many do not occur in the mind except after unification. Hence no word is seen placed for two opposites with one convention, although it is permissible for two different conventions, like 'bayt' (house/verse). For no single thing results from two opposites, otherwise its existence in a subject would necessitate the existence of the two integrated opposites.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage opens the third article on linguistic principles. It begins by praising Allah for creating languages, which enables diverse fields of knowledge (Arabic, Persian, Hindi, etc.). The author then defines language (lughah) as 'a word that indicates by convention.' This is the standard definition in usul al-fiqh. He then discusses the two primary types of indication (dalalah): mutabaqah (correspondence) and tadammun (implication). For simple words (al-mufradat), some scholars claim that the two indications are essentially one because the word's meaning is understood as a whole. The author critiques this view: if the whole is truly undetailed, then the parts are not understood until analysis; therefore the two indications only become distinct after analysis. He argues that the claim of absolute unity is a mere assertion not proven. He then addresses the problem of common words (al-mushtarak): a single word used for multiple meanings (like 'ayn for eye, spring, etc.) does not have unity of meaning because it has multiple conventions. For a single convention, the word unifies the meaning in the mind. He concludes that no word can be placed for opposites with one convention, though it is possible with separate conventions.

Key Terms
اللغة (al-lughah): اللفظ الدال وضعاً (a word that indicates by convention)
دلالة مطابقة (dalalah mutabaqah): دلالة اللفظ على كمال معناه (indication of the word upon the complete meaning)
دلالة تضمن (dalalah tadammun): دلالة اللفظ على جزئه (indication of the word upon a part of its meaning)
المفرد المشترك (al-mufrad al-mushtarak): اللفظ الواحد الذي وضع لمعان كثيرة (a single word placed for multiple meanings)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are mutabaqah and tadammun not considered identical for simple words?
A) Because the parts are understood without analysis.
B) Because the simple word's indication is undetailed, but parts become understood only after analysis, making the two indications distinct after analysis.
C) Because the simple word indicates both the whole and the parts simultaneously due to its simplicity.
D) Because the author rejects the existence of tadammun altogether.

Key Takeaway
Mutabaqah and tadammun are analytically distinct for simple words; the parts emerge only after mental analysis, confirming the Hanafi emphasis on precise linguistic analysis in usul al-fiqh.

Means of Knowing Linguistic Conventions: Tawatur, Ahad, and the Role of Reason
Discussion of tawatur, ahad, and aql as means to know linguistic conventions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three means of knowing linguistic conventions in Hanafi usul and apply the principle that reason alone is insufficient without transmitted evidence.

Core Idea
Tawatur gives certainty, ahad gives probability, and reason only aids with transmission — never replaces it.

In this passage, the author explains how we come to know the meanings of words as established by the original linguistic convention (wad'). He first refutes the idea that there is an inherent necessary connection between words and meanings, then affirms that knowledge is acquired through three channels: widespread transmission (tawatur), solitary reports (ahad), and reason aided by transmitted evidence. This is fundamental for usul al-fiqh because the interpretation of Qur'an and Sunnah depends on knowing the conventions of the Arabic language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rejected view
What is the view of 'Abbad ibn Sulayman regarding the relationship between words and meanings? How does the author refute it?
The author calls it 'ba'id' (far-fetched) and argues that inherent connection between a thing and its opposites is rationally impossible.
2
Extract the three means
From the text, list the three means of knowing linguistic conventions: tawatur, ahad, and aql with transmission.
The phrase 'والطريق الآن في معرفة الأوضاع التواتر... والآحاد... وقد يستمد بالعقل' indicates these three.
3
Analyze the example of tawatur
Why are words like 'light' and 'fire' given as examples? What epistemic level do they produce?
These words are universally known; their transmission is qat'i (certain). Doubting them is sophistry.
4
Analyze the example of ahad
What is the significance of naming al-Asma'i and al-Khalil?
They are trusted linguists; their solitary reports provide probable (zanni) knowledge but are sufficient for linguistic conventions.
5
Understand the role of reason
How does the example of 'definite plural with lam' show reason's role? Why must it be combined with transmission?
The rule that the definite plural admits exception is derived inductively from observed usage (transmission) and rational necessity. Reason alone cannot establish it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية ١٤٩ قوم بهم بحسب هذه المناسبة، وإلا فإعطاء الهندية لأهل الهند والعربية للعرب ليس أولى من العكس ومن ها هنا أي من أجل اعتبار المناسبة بين كل قوم ولغتهم (رأينا لسان سكان الجبال صلبة ثقيلة لكون أمزجتهم كذلك هذا : (وأما القول بالتناسب الذاتي) بين الألفاظ ومعانيها بأن يكون بين ذاتيهما مناسبة تقتضي عدم الانفكاك لو خليت وطباعها والاكتفاء به في الدلالة) بأن يكون العلم بهذا التناسب يكفي في انفهام المعنى منها كما ذهب إليه عباد بن سليمان وغيره من أتباعه ( فهو بعيد) لأن المناسبة الذاتية بين الشيء والضدين مما يمجه العقل، وأما الرد بأنه يلزم أن لا ينفك اللفظ عن الدلائل أصلاً فإن مقتضى الذات لا ينفك عنها فغير وارد، فإنه أراد بالاقتضاء ما ذكرنا ولو أراد بالاقتضاء الذاتي مقابل الوضع وإن كان من أعراض مفارقة لم يبعد وحينئذ لا يرد عليه ما ذكر ثم بعد لا يخلو عن بعد ومكابرة (وسمعت من بعض الشيوخ) سمعت من الثقات أنه أراد ببعض الشيوخ جدي المولى قطب الملة والدين الشهيد قدس سره أنه لقيه) أي لقي بعض الشيوخ رجل من البراهمة من جبال الشمال كان عنده قوانين يفهم منها كل لسان على وجه كلي هكذا سمعت ويمكن أن يرجع الضمير المنصوب إلى عباد بن سليمان وعلى كل تقدير يؤيد هذا النقل مذهب عباد بن سليمان نحو تأييد، ويجوز أن يكون فهمه بمعرفة تلك المناسبة التي وضعت الألفاظ لأجلها لا للاكتفاء بالمناسبة فقط، فحينئذٍ احتيج إلى الوضع والطريق) (الآن في معرفة الأوضاع (التواتر كالنور والنار والتشكيك فيه بأن التواتر غير مفيد للعلم، وأن لفظ الله أكثر دوراً مع الاختلاف فيه وأمثالهما (سفسطة) لا يلتفت إليه لكونه مخالفاً للضرورة القاطعة (و) الطريق في المعرفة (الآحاد أيضاً، كنقل الأصمعي والخليل (وقد يستمد بالعقل) في إثبات الوضع لكن مع اشتراك النقل (كقولنا : الجمع المحلى باللام ( يدخله الاستثناء) وهذه المقدمة استقرائية (وكل ما يدخله المستثنى منه لأنه أي الاستثناء لإخراج ما لولاه لوجب دخوله) وهذه مقدمة يعم الاستثناء عقلية .
The third article on linguistic principles. [149] ... [Discussion of the opinion of 'Abbad ibn Sulayman on inherent appropriateness between words and meanings...] And the means for knowing [linguistic] conventions: tawatur (widespread transmission) like [the words] 'light' and 'fire', and doubting it is sophistry. And [also] ahad (solitary reports) like the transmission of al-Asma'i and al-Khalil. And it [knowledge] may be derived through reason ('aql) in establishing the convention, but with the support of transmission, like our saying: 'The definite plural [article] with lam admits exception' — this premise is inductive. 'And everything that admits the excepted-from [must have an exception] because the exception is to exclude what would otherwise be included' — this is a rational premise.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the sources for knowing the meanings of words as established by the original linguistic convention (wad'). The author refutes the view of 'Abbad ibn Sulayman that there is an inherent, necessary connection between words and their meanings, arguing that such a claim is rationally implausible. He then recounts a story about an encounter with a Brahmin who claimed to have universal linguistic knowledge, which is seen as supporting 'Abbad's view, though it may only imply knowledge of a principle of appropriateness rather than dispensing with convention altogether. The main point is that linguistic knowledge is acquired through tawatur (massively transmitted reports) such as the words 'fire' and 'light', and through ahad reports from reliable linguists like al-Asma'i and al-Khalil. Reason also plays a role, but only in conjunction with transmitted evidence, as in the example: 'the definite plural with lam admits exception' — this is an inductive observation used to establish a rule of grammar, but it is ultimately based on transmitted usage.

Key Terms
التواتر (al-tawatur): Widespread transmission of a word or report that produces certain knowledge of its meaning.
الآحاد (al-ahad): Solitary reports from reliable individuals, which provide probable knowledge and are accepted in linguistic matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a means of knowing linguistic conventions?
A) Tawatur (widespread transmission)
B) Ahad reports (solitary reports)
C) Reason without any transmission
D) Reason with the support of transmission

Key Takeaway
Tawatur yields certain knowledge of linguistic conventions; ahad yields probable knowledge; reason must be accompanied by transmitted evidence.

Derived Words (Mushtaq): Definition, Conditions, and the Mu'tazila Dispute
Defining mushtaq, its conditions, types, and a theological dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mushtaq, distinguish between regular and irregular derivatives, and evaluate the Hanafi position on the condition of root meaning against the Mu'tazila objection.

Core Idea
A derivative is true only when its root meaning actually exists in the subject.

In usul al-fiqh, derived words (mushtaq) are crucial for understanding how linguistic forms relate to meanings. This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahmut' defines mushtaq, classifies its types, and sets the condition that the root meaning must truly apply. It then engages in a theological debate with the Mu'tazila over divine attributes, affirming that Allah is truly 'alim (omniscient) because knowledge exists in His essence, without implying multiplicity of eternal beings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of mushtaq and the circularity issue
The author defines mushtaq as agreeing with a root in meaning and original letters. He notes that defining it by 'root' risks circularity and prefers a verbal definition.
Circularity arises if 'root' is itself defined as what derivation is taken from.
2
List the types of change from the root
Changes can be by movement only, letter only, increase, or decrease. Combinations of these yield up to 15 types as illustrated.
Examples are left to the student to derive; the author says 'the examples are clear'.
3
Differentiate regular (muttarid) from irregular (ghayr muttarid) derivatives
In regular derivatives, the root meaning is included in the denomination, so wherever the meaning exists, the derivative applies. In irregular ones, the root meaning is only a condition for the name, not part of it.
Example of irregular: qarurah (vessel) – a broken vessel is still called qarurah even though the original meaning (vessel for holding liquid) may not fully apply.
4
State the condition for correct application of a derivative
The root meaning must actually exist in the subject for the derivative to be applied truthfully. The whole (derivative) cannot exist without its part (root meaning).
This is the majority view; a later author may disagree on the composition of the concept.
5
Analyze the Mu'tazila claim and the Hanafi response
The Mu'tazila denied that knowledge subsists in Allah to avoid multiplicity of eternal beings. The Hanafis respond that attributes are necessary for the essence (wajib li al-dhat) but not independent essences, so no multiplicity arises. Also, knowledge can mean the essence itself in one sense.
The verse about Christians is used to show that the rebuke is for believing in multiple eternal essences, not multiple attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمفرد انقسامات باعتبارات شتى شتيت كجرحى وجريح (فلنفصلها مع أحكامها في فصول) : جمع (الفصل الأول) (وهو) أي المفرد مشتق إن وافق أصلاً) في المعنى بحروفه الأصول) التي تبقى في التصريفات ولا يخفى أن أخذ أصله الذي هو العلم وإنما قالوا (هرباً عن لزوم تعدد القدماء) بالقول بقيام الصفات إذ يستحيل قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى وتعدد القدماء باطل، ألا ترى أنه كيف ذم الله سبحانه وتعالى النصارى وقال: ﴿لَقَدْ كَفَرَ الَّذِينَ قَالُوا إِنَّ اللهَ ثَالِث ثَلَاثَة﴾ [المائدة: ۷۳] فإن قيل : هذا يلزمهم أيضاً، فإنهم قالوا بقيام العالمية بذاته سبحانه، قالوا : (وأما العالمية فإنما هي من النسب الاعتبارية دون الصفات العينية (والجواب أن الممتنع) إنما هو (تعدد قدماء هي (ذوات قديمة، وإنما ذم الله القول بهذا فإن النصارى إنما يقولون بآلهة ثلاثة، والإله لا يكون إلا ذاتاً قديمة واجبة (وأما الصفات التي نقول بها فواجبة للذات لا بالذات فإنها محتاجة إلى الذات فلا تكون واجبة وإذا لم تكن واجبة لا الألوهية من أحد من الحمقى والعقلاء، فلا يكون هذا مشمول الآية، وليس المقصود من الجواب أن تعدد ذوات قديمة يستلزم الوجوب بالذات دون تعدد الصفات حتى يرد عليه أن القدم وإن كان للذات لا يوجب الوجوب ولا ينافي الإمكان، ألا ترى أن الفلاسفة يقولون بقدم الفلك وغيره مع القول بإمكانه فتدبر، ولمطلع الأسرار ههنا كلام يجب التنبيه عليه هو : أن العلم وغيره من الصفات يطلق على معنيين أحدهما المعنى المصدري المفهوم للكافة، الثاني : ما به تنكشف الأشياء ويترتب عليه هذا المفهوم، فالثاني عندهم في الباري عز وجل نفس ذاته، مما يصح دعوى
And for the single word there are divisions according to various diverse considerations (like juraha and jarih). So let us detail them with their rulings in chapters. A collection: (Chapter One) (And it is) that the single word is a derivative if it agrees with a root in meaning in its original letters that remain in conjugations. It is not hidden that taking the root in the definition is not free from a hint of circularity. So either it is meant by it that from which something is taken, not that from which a derivation is taken, or the definition is made verbal, which is preferable. And it is necessary for the derivative to have a change in what is in the original beginning, either by movement only or by letter only, and each of them is either by increase or decrease; these are four. And the combination, i.e., the composite change consisting of more than one, while the combination is binary: taking change by increase of movement with the three remaining, and by decrease of movement with the last two, and by increase of letter with the last. And while the combination is ternary: these are four resulting from the first with two each of the remaining. And while the combination is quaternary: this is one, resulting from the combination of all the singles. It reaches fifteen as we explained, and the examples are clear. And it (the derivative) is regular like the active participle and others that fall under a universal rule; and others are irregular like al-qarurah and others that do not fall under a rule. The difference between them is that the original meaning is either included in the denomination, so wherever the meaning exists, the derivative exists, so it is regular; or it is not included but is a condition for the correctness of the denomination by this name only, so it is not regular. And the irregular is only a derivative by consideration of the root, which is apparent, for the existence of that from which it is derived is not a condition there for the correctness of its application, such that a broken qarurah is called qarurah. Issue: The condition for the correctness of applying a derivative is the truth of its original derived from it upon it, because the impossibility of the realization of the whole (which is the derivative) without the part (which is the root). This is apparent according to the view of the majority, and it is not valid according to what he will choose that there is no composition in the concept of the derivative, so what is meant is what is in the ruling of the part, or it is built upon the well-known. Contra the Mu'tazila regarding the attributes of Allah Almighty, for they said His being omniscient without His knowledge, so they admitted the truth of 'alim' derived without the realization of its root which is knowledge. They only said this fleeing from the necessity of multiplicity of eternal beings by saying that attributes subsist, since it is impossible for accidents to subsist in His Essence and multiplicity of eternal beings is false. Do you not see how Allah Almighty rebuked the Christians and said: 'They have certainly disbelieved who say that Allah is the third of three' [5:73]. If it is said: This also applies to them, for they say the subsistence of omniscience in His Essence. They said: As for omniscience, it is but from relational aspects, not from essential attributes. The answer: That which is impossible is multiplicity of eternal beings (i.e., eternal essences). Allah only rebuked this, for the Christians only say three gods, and god is only an eternal necessary essence. As for the attributes that we affirm, they are necessary for the essence, not by themselves, for they need the essence, so they are not necessary. And if they are not necessary, none of the foolish or rational claim divinity for them, so this is not covered by the verse. And the purpose of the answer is not that multiplicity of eternal essences entails necessity by essence while multiplicity of attributes does not, such that it could be objected that eternity even if for essence does not necessitate necessity nor negate possibility; do you not see that the philosophers say the eternity of the sphere and other things while asserting its possibility? So ponder. And for the one who examines secrets, here there is a speech that must be noted: that knowledge and other attributes are used in two senses: one is the infinitive meaning understood by all; the second: that by which things are disclosed and upon which this concept is built. So the second according to them in the Almighty is His essence itself, which makes it possible to claim
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of derived words (al-mushtaq). The author explains that a word is derived if it shares original root letters and meaning with its root. However, defining it by 'root' risks circularity, so he suggests a verbal definition. He then lists necessary changes in the root (by movement, letter, increase, decrease, and their combinations up to 15 types). He distinguishes between regular (muttarid) derivatives like active participles that follow a universal rule, and irregular (ghayr muttarid) like qarurah (vessel) where the original meaning is not always present. The key condition is that the root meaning must actually apply to the subject for the derivative to be correctly used. This leads to a theological dispute with the Mu'tazila, who claimed that Allah is called 'alim (omniscient) without knowledge subsisting in Him, to avoid multiplicity of eternal beings. The author refutes this, explaining that divine attributes are necessary for the essence (wajib li al-dhat) but not necessary by themselves, thus not eternal beings in the sense that would constitute shirk. He also clarifies that attributes like knowledge are used in two senses: the conceptual sense and the reality that exists in essence.

Key Terms
المشتق (al-mushtaq): A word that agrees with its root in meaning and original letters, with a necessary change in form.
المطرد (al-muttarid): A derivative that follows a universal rule, so wherever the root meaning exists, the derivative can be applied.
غير المطرد (ghayr al-muttarid): An irregular derivative where the root meaning is a condition for the name but not always present, so it does not apply universally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a regular derivative (muttarid) from an irregular one (ghayr muttarid)?
A) Regular derivatives have no change from the root.
B) In regular derivatives, the root meaning is included in the denomination; in irregular ones, it is only a condition for the name.
C) Regular derivatives are only used for Allah's attributes.
D) Irregular derivatives do not have a root.

Key Takeaway
A derivative is truthfully applied only when its root meaning actually exists in the subject, a principle that defends both linguistic precision and sound theology.

Abandoning Haqiqah in Oaths: Impossibility, Difficulty, and Disuse
Rules for abandoning haqiqah in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four grounds for abandoning haqiqah in oaths and apply the Hanafi rule to determine the customary meaning in new scenarios.

Core Idea
Haqiqah is abandoned in oaths when literal understanding is impossible, difficult, or disused; custom governs.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the literal meaning (haqiqah) is normally preferred in interpreting oaths. However, this passage introduces a crucial exception: when the literal meaning is impossible (intellectually or customarily), excessively difficult, or has fallen into disuse, the oath is interpreted according to customary meaning (majaz 'urfi). This rule reflects the principle that oaths are understood by what people customarily intend, not by impossible or impractical literalism. We will work through the four categories presented, understand each with examples, and examine a scholarly disagreement in the case of disuse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four grounds for abandoning haqiqah
Read the passage and list the four scenarios: (1) intellectual impossibility, (2) customary impossibility, (3) difficulty, (4) disuse. For each, note the condition that triggers abandonment.
The passage uses key terms: al-ta'dhhur al-'aqli, al-ta'dhhur al-'adi, al-ta'assur, al-hajr.
2
Analyze each ground with its example
For each ground, connect the example given (oath statement) to the resulting interpretation. Understand why the literal meaning is abandoned and what customary meaning replaces it.
For difficulty (al-ta'assur), note that the oath attaches to what is customarily eaten from the tree, not the tree itself.
3
Examine the ikhtilaf in the disuse case
Focus on the example of swearing not to eat flour. Two opinions are reported: one says violation occurs only if one eats what flour is normally used for (bread, etc.); the other says violation can occur by eating flour itself because haqiqah never lapses. Identify which view Fakhr al-Islam supports and note the reasoning (general majaz).
This ikhtilaf shows that disuse (hajr) is the most contested ground.
4
Extract the underlying usul principle
From the examples, deduce the principle: the literal meaning of a word in an oath is abandoned when it is impossible, difficult, or not customary to apply literally. The oath then refers to the customary meaning (ma'na 'urfi). This is an application of the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are judged by intentions) and consideration of 'urf.
The Hanafi school gives significant weight to custom in interpreting legal utterances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الحقيقة تترك لتعذرها عقلاً) كأنت ابني الأكبر سناً، وهذا القسم لا يكاد يوجد عندهما (أو) لتعذرها (عادة) وإن جاز عقلاً (كلا يأكل من هذا القدر) فإنه محال في العادة (فلما يحلها) أي فينعقد لما يحل القدر، والأظهر يحله ( أو ) تترك (التعسرها) وإن لم يكن متعذراً (كمن الشجرة) أي لا يأكل منها، فإن أكل عين الشجرة متعسر ، وإن أمكن (ف) يحنث (لما يخرج) منه (مأكولاً) كالثمر والشيرج وإن لم يأكل شيء منه فعلى ثمنه (أو) تترك الهجرها عادة، وإن سهل كمن الدقيق أي لا يأكل منه (فلما) الدقيق متخذ (له) أي لأجله كالخبز وغيره، ولو تكلف وأكل عين الدقيق فقيل : لا يحنث لأنه سقط اعتبارها وقيل يحنث، لأن الحقيقة لا تسقط بحال، فعندهم ههنا عموم المجاز، قال الإمام فخر الإسلام الأول أشبه، ثم إن فخر الإسلام
Issue: The literal meaning (haqiqah) is abandoned due to its impossibility intellectually, such as if one says 'You are my older son in age'—this type hardly exists according to them. Or due to its impossibility customarily, even if possible intellectually, such as 'I will not eat from this pot'—for it is customarily impossible. So the oath applies to what the pot would normally contain, and the more apparent view is that it applies. Or it is abandoned due to its difficulty, even if not impossible, such as 'I will not eat from the tree'—for eating the tree itself is difficult, though possible. So the violation occurs by what emerges from it as edible, like fruit and sesame oil. And if he did not eat anything from it, then upon its price. Or it is abandoned due to its disuse customarily, even if easy, such as 'I will not eat the flour'—so the oath applies to what flour is customarily made for, like bread etc. And if he goes to the trouble and eats the flour itself, it is said: he does not violate because its consideration has lapsed; and it is said: he does violate because haqiqah never lapses. According to them, here there is general majaz. Imam Fakhr al-Islam said the first is more appropriate. Then Fakhr al-Islam...
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage outlines four scenarios where the literal meaning (haqiqah) of an oath is abandoned in favor of customary meaning: (1) intellectual impossibility, e.g., swearing by a son's age—unlikely to occur; (2) customary impossibility, e.g., swearing not to eat from a pot—one cannot eat the pot itself, so the oath applies to its contents; (3) customary difficulty, e.g., swearing not to eat from a tree—one cannot eat the tree easily, so the oath applies to its fruits; (4) customary disuse, e.g., swearing not to eat flour—flour is not normally eaten raw, so the oath applies to bread etc. In the last case, there is a disagreement: some say if one deliberately eats the flour, he does not violate because haqiqah is abandoned; others say he does violate because haqiqah is never completely abandoned. The preferred view, according to Imam Fakhr al-Islam, is the first.

Key Terms
التعذر العقلي (al-ta'dhhur al-'aqli): Impossibility by intellect; something that cannot rationally occur.
التعذر العادي (al-ta'dhhur al-'adi): Impossibility by custom; something that is not normally done though possible in theory.
التعسر (al-ta'assur): Difficulty; something possible but with great hardship.
الهجر (al-hajr): Disuse; the literal meaning is no longer customarily practiced.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone swears 'I will not eat from this tree,' what is the interpretation of the oath?
A) He violates only if he eats the tree itself
B) He violates if he eats fruit from the tree
C) He does not violate at all
D) It depends on his intention

Key Takeaway
The literal meaning (haqiqah) in oaths is abandoned when it is impossible, difficult, or disused; custom determines the binding interpretation.

Ruling When Both Haqiqah and Majaz Are Impossible
Explaining the case of linguistic impossibility and its legal effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of ta'addhur (impossibility) to determine when an utterance is legally void (laghw) and when intention can salvage it to produce a legal effect such as divorce or oath.

Core Idea
When both literal and metaphorical meanings fail, intention may rescue a legally impossible utterance.

In this passage, the author delves into a subtle usul scenario: what happens when an utterance cannot be interpreted literally (haqiqah) nor metaphorically (majaz) due to logical or legal contradiction? The default ruling is that the utterance is legally void (laghw) — it produces no legal effect. However, the Hanafi school carves out an exception: if the speaker intends a meaning that the wording can possibly bear, even if that meaning is not the primary metaphor, the utterance may become effective, such as resulting in divorce or an oath. This lesson unpacks that argument, the objections from other scholars, and the underlying usul principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario of ta'addhur
Locate in the passage where both haqiqah and majaz are impossible. The key example is 'my daughter to my wife' — the literal meaning is impossible (she is not his daughter), and the metaphorical meaning (simile for zihar) is also invalid because it would require an eternal prohibition that the speaker cannot effect.
The impossibility is both logical (relative to the specific utterance) and legal (the consequence cannot be realized by the speaker).
2
Understand the default ruling: laghw
When both meanings are impossible, the utterance is laghw: it has no valid legal meaning. The author clarifies that this does not mean the utterance has no meaning at all (e.g., it could imply compassion), but it has no meaning that results in a shari' ruling.
Laghw here is a technical term: speech that cannot produce a legal consequence.
3
Analyze the Hanafi exception through intention
The Hanafi position argues that if the speaker intends a meaning that the words can possibly bear (even if not the primary metaphor), the utterance may become legally effective. For example, if he intends divorce by 'my daughter to my wife', the common ground of prohibition (the daughter is haram to him just as divorce makes her haram) allows the utterance to be interpreted as a divorce (ba'in).
This is based on isti'arah (metaphor) patterns and the widespread understanding among people that such expressions imply separation.
4
Examine the qiyas with the 'my son' case
The passage analogizes to the case where a man says 'my son' to his slave: emancipation is possible because the speaker can free him from ownership. Similarly, in the 'my daughter' case, if he intends divorce, the necessary consequence (tahrim al-wat') is within his capacity because divorce is an act he can perform.
This qiyas highlights the illa: whether the intended legal effect is within the speaker's capacity.
5
Consider the subtlety of intending the necessary consequence
The author raises a further point: if the speaker intends the necessary consequence of the wording (e.g., the prohibition of intercourse that necessarily follows from the utterance), does that count as intending the legal effect (divorce)? The ruling is that it does, because the necessary consequence can be intended just as an oath can be intended from a vow's consequence.
The phrase 'fafham' (understand) indicates that this is the relied-upon position.

That he inserted these examples in the category of impossibility, for perhaps he took a broader meaning than hardship and the like. And he gave an example for hijr (boycott) like: 'He does not set foot in so-and-so's house.' And if the boycott is due to custom, the ruling changes with its change, and that is apparent. Or due to religious boycott, for what is boycotted religiously is like what is boycotted customarily, for the Muslim's custom is nothing but what he takes from the Law. As for the impossibility of the literal, it is apparent. As for the impossibility of the metaphorical, divorce does not occur due to the contradiction between the prohibition of lineage and the prohibition of marriage (which is divorce). The first is an eternal prohibition that contradicts marriage, unlike the second which is temporary and an effect of it. The eternal prohibition is not within his capacity, unlike freedom from the beginning of ownership. And it is not interpreted as a simile, as transmitted from scholars of rhetoric, so that it would be zihar (injurious comparison). Ponder this. And we do not mean by 'null' that it has no meaning at all, so that it would be objected that the invalidity of one metaphor does not necessitate the invalidity of other metaphors, for it could be that it means compassion and the like. Rather, we mean that it has no meaning that results in a legal ruling. Then the speech is not free from something; why should it not be permissible to intend divorce by it due to the commonality in the very prohibition and the widespread knowledge of the prohibition of the daughter? Do you not see that dullness in a stupid person is of one kind and in a donkey of another kind, yet it is metaphorically applied to him? If you trace the instances of metaphors, you will be convinced that commonality in the point of comparison in all respects is not necessary, and in this expression there is a certain intent of exaggeration, so it should be a final divorce. And we may also say: If he intended this eternal prohibition, it must be an oath; indeed, if he intended prohibition absolutely, it is an oath, as in the prohibition of a slave woman, for prohibiting the lawful is an oath. This, and Allah Most High is knower of His rulings. The author said: 'I say: If he intended divorce from the necessary prohibition of intercourse that is a necessary consequence of the wording, understood as secondary to it, like the oath intended from the necessary consequence of the vow, does it occur or not? It occurs, so understand.' For if this intention suffices, then the occurrence of divorce is necessary here; otherwise, your argument there is incomplete, and you know that speech of this kind is also incomplete there.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a ruling about the scenario when both the literal meaning (haqiqah) and the metaphorical meaning (majaz) of an utterance are impossible to apply, rendering the utterance legally void (laghw). The author first discusses examples from previous contexts, such as the word 'hijr' (boycott) which changes a ruling based on custom or religious practice. He then focuses on a specific problem: a man says 'my daughter to my wife' — since the literal meaning (a daughter being a wife) is impossible, and the metaphorical meaning (e.g., simile for zihar) is also invalid because it would require an eternal prohibition that the speaker cannot effect, the utterance has no legal effect. However, if the speaker intended something that the words could bear, such as intending divorce by the common ground of prohibition, then it may be effective as a final divorce (ba'in). The passage also engages with rhetorical scholars' views on simile and metaphor, concluding that the intended meaning here is a rational metaphor, not a linguistic one. Finally, the author raises a subtle issue: if someone intends the consequence (tahrim al-wat') that necessarily follows from the wording, does that count as intending divorce? The author rules that it does occur, based on the principle that the necessary consequence can be intended just as an oath can be intended from a vow's consequence.

Key Terms
التعذر (al-ta'addhur): Impossibility of applying either the literal or metaphorical meaning to a given utterance due to logical or legal contradiction.
اللغو (al-laghw): An utterance that has no valid meaning resulting in any legal ruling; it is legally void and ignored.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath; in this context, an utterance of prohibition that may be treated as an oath if intended to invoke a legal consequence such as divorce.
الاستعارة (al-isti'arah): Metaphor; the use of a word in a sense different from its original meaning based on similarity and a claim of identity (tashbiyya baligha).
المجاز العقلي (al-majaz al-'aqli): Rational metaphor; an attribution of an action to something other than its true agent due to a mental connection, such as 'Zayd is a lion' meaning he is brave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling when a man says to his wife 'You are like my daughter to me' (without an intention) and both literal and metaphorical interpretations are impossible?
A) It counts as zihar (injurious comparison) because it is a simile.
B) The utterance is legally void (laghw) and no divorce or zihar occurs.
C) It is a valid divorce because the word 'daughter' implies separation.
D) It is considered an oath, requiring kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows intention to salvage an utterance otherwise impossible in both literal and metaphorical meaning, provided the wording can bear the intended legal effect and the speaker has the capacity to execute it.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
10 min
The Core Dispute: Are Shar'i Terms Haqiqah Shar'iyyah?
Defense of haqiqah shar'iyyah through Companions' understanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on haqiqah shar'iyyah and evaluate the deniers' argument that Companions would have transmitted explicit coinage by tawatur.

Core Idea
Companions understood shar'i terms through prophetic context; explicit coinage transmission is not required.

The debate over haqiqah shar'iyyah is foundational in Hanafi usul: are terms like salat and zakat new legal coinages or mere metaphors? This lesson unpacks the deniers' primary objection—that if the Lawgiver coined these terms, the Companions would have transmitted the act of coinage by tawatur—and the Hanafi response that understanding through usage suffices. We'll trace the logic and its implications for interpreting sacred texts.

Guided Path
1
Define haqiqah shar'iyyah and the opposing view
Clarify that haqiqah shar'iyyah means the Lawgiver assigned new meanings to existing Arabic words. The opposition claims these words remain metaphorical or literal in the original linguistic sense.
2
State the deniers' transmission argument
Deniers argue: if the Lawgiver coined these terms, the Companions would have understood the coinage and transmitted it to us by tawatur (mass transmission). Since no such tawatur exists, coinage did not occur.
The deniers demand explicit evidence of the act of coinage.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis counter that understanding the meaning does not require knowledge of explicit coinage. Companions understood through prophetic explanation and contextual usage. The meaning (not the wording) was transmitted mutawatir.
Analogous to children learning language without formal instruction.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If haqiqah shar'iyyah, the literal meaning (shar'i) is the default in interpretation; if metaphor, a qarinah (contextual indicator) is needed to shift from the linguistic meaning. This affects how we derive rulings from Quran and Sunnah.
The Hanafi position gives priority to the shar'i meaning.

Source Text

يستلزم عدم سقوط الصلاة بلا (دعاء) لأن المفروض على هذا القول الدعاء بالذات، والأركان لأجله (و) الحال أن الدعاء (ليس بفرض كما في الأخرس) بالاتفاق، وللمناقش أن يقول : إن القراءة فرض عندكم فيلزم منه أن لا يكفي كونها دعاء إلا أن يوجبوا دعاء نفسياً على الأخرس بعد النية، وفيه ما فيه ومنع كون صلاته صلاة شرعاً) بل عبادة أخرى أقيمت مقام الصلاة كالفدية أقيمت مقام الصوم للعذر (كما قيل) في حواشي میرزاجان»: على أنه حصل الفهم بالبيان النبوي، وقد نقل متواتر (المعنى وإن لم يكون متواتر اللفظ (مع أنه قد يحصل) البيان من غير تصريح كما للأطفال فليجز أن يكون الفهم ههنا بهذا الوجه، هذا والذي يظهر من تتبع كلام وآله وأصحابه أجمعين وسلم أن هذه الألفاظ موضوعة لهذه المعاني، ولم ينقل هذا آحاداً فضلاً عن التواتر، وأما الثالث فله نحو ورود، إذ يجوز أن يكونوا علموا بالتجربة، ثم من بعدهم كذلك، والتصريح ليس ضرورياً لكن الأمر غير خاف على ذي كياسة، فإنه ما علم بهذا النحو أيضاً كما هدانا إليه مطلع الأسرار الإلهية ، ومما ينبه عليه أنه لو كان كذلك لصرح به أحد ولو في وقت كما صرحوا ببعض أوضاع اللغة، ثم إن دعوى النقل دعوى على الله تعالى، فلا بد لإثباتها من قاطع وليس ههنا أمارة ظنية فضلاً عن القاطع، فلا يليق بحال مسلم أن يجترىء على الله بما لا يعلم (وأما قولهم في نفي النقل الشرعي : لو كان حقيقة شرعية لكانت غير عربية لعدم وضع العرب ولكان القرآن غير عربي) لاشتماله عليه وقد قال الله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّا أَنزَلْنَهُ قُرْمَانًا عَرَبِيًّا ﴾ [يوسف: ٢] (فقد مر الجواب عنه في مسألة المعرب .
[It is a] sacred reality without this excuse [being available] and the discussion about it that it is linguistically for purification as well, so it is purification of the heart, and the specific ownership is a condition for it or a means to it, it does not harm much, for after completion it is a discussion in the example. (He responded that this statement necessitates that prayer does not fall without supplication, because what is required on this statement is supplication per se, and the pillars are because of it, while supplication is not obligatory as in the mute by consensus. And the discussant may say: Recitation is obligatory according to you, so it follows that it is not performed without recitation, while the mute performs it. And if he excuses that its substitute is moving the tongue due to the excuse, then for this speaker also this excuse is possible. Yes, the deduction in this manner is complete from the Hanafis, for they can say: It follows from this that the prayer of one able to supplicate without supplication is not valid, while it is valid. And this statement is not possible from the Shafi'is, for al-Fatiha is obligatory according to them and it contains supplication. The truth in the response is that this is obstinacy, for it is known from the necessities of religion that prayer is these pillars, and the hadiths explaining prayer are also definitive in it. If you say: Intention is a condition in prayer, and it is internal supplication, he answered: Intention does not necessitate internal supplication such that it becomes internal speech, and also intention is outside prayer, preceding it, so it is not sufficient that it is supplication unless they make internal supplication obligatory upon the mute after intention, and there is something in it, and the denial that his prayer is legally prayer rather it is another worship substituted in place of prayer like the ransom substituted for fasting due to the excuse, as said in the glosses of Mirzajan: it necessitates that the mute is not obligated to prayer, and it is false. If you say to them: Do they concede that? I say: perhaps it is contrary to consensus. The deniers of sacred reality said: If the Lawgiver had transferred them, the Companions would have understood them, for understanding is a condition of obligation, and they are obligated so they understand. And if they understood, it would have been transmitted to us by tawatur, and there is no ahad, let alone tawatur. We said: The understanding is shared between being sacred realities and being metaphors, so whatever your answer is, it is our answer, that understanding occurred through prophetic explanation, and it has been transmitted mutawatir in meaning even if not mutawatir in wording, and it may occur without explicit explanation as with children, so it is possible that understanding here occurs in this manner. And what appears from following the words of the verifiers is that there is distortion in the transmission of the proof from what it was. The gist: If the legal transmission were realized, the Companions would have first understood that these words were coined for these meanings, because understanding is a condition of obligation, and understanding of coined meanings only occurs after knowledge of the coinage. Also, they are skilled in types of indications, and if they knew that they were transferred by the Lawgiver's coinage, it would have been transmitted to us by tawatur as in the coinages of languages due to the multiple incentives to transmit it, and it was not even transmitted by ahad let alone tawatur. So then the first objection is not refuted, for it is not a shared obligation, because on metaphor, understanding the meaning occurs via a qarinah, and they understood and transmitted to us, unlike [literal] transmission, for it requires knowledge of the coinage to understand the meaning. Similarly the second: The Prophet did not explain that these words are coined for these meanings, and this was not transmitted even as ahad let alone tawatur. As for the third, it has some validity, for it is possible that they knew through experience, then those after them similarly, and explicit statement is not necessary, but the matter is not hidden from the intelligent, for it was not known in this manner either, as the Revealer of Divine Secrets guided us. And one thing that alerts: If it were so, someone would have stated it even once, just as they stated some coinages of the language. Then, claiming transmission is a claim against Allah, so proof requires decisive evidence, and there is no even conjectural sign here, let alone decisive, so it is not fitting for any Muslim to be audacious towards Allah with what he does not know. As for their statement in denying legal transmission: If it were a sacred reality, it would be non-Arabic due to the lack of Arab coinage, and the Qur'an would be non-Arabic because it includes it, while Allah said: 'Indeed, We have sent it down as an Arabic Qur'an.' The answer has already been given in the issue of mu'rab.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument in the debate over whether Shar'i terms (like 'salat', 'zakat') are haqiqah shar'iyyah (sacred realities with new coinage) or remain metaphorical usages of the original linguistic meanings. The author, a Hanafi, defends the view that they are haqiqah shar'iyyah. The text begins with a refutation of an objection regarding the definition of 'tahara'. It then moves to the core argument: Deniers claim that if the Lawgiver had coined these terms, the Companions would have understood and transmitted this tawatur. The Hanafi response counters that understanding can come through prophetic context and usage, not necessarily explicit coinage. The argument also addresses the status of the mute in prayer and the role of intention. The passage then presents the deniers' final objection that if shar'i terms were haqiqah, the Quran would not be Arabic, referring to the verse 'We have sent it down as an Arabic Qur'an.' The author notes this is answered elsewhere in the issue of mu'rab (words of non-Arabic origin). The overall tone is defensive of the Hanafi position against Shafi'i and other schools that reject haqiqah shar'iyyah.

Key Terms
حقيقة شرعية (haqiqah shar'iyyah): A term that has been legally redefined by the Lawgiver (Shari') for a specific religious meaning, distinct from its original linguistic meaning.
تواتر (tawatur): Mass transmission that yields certainty; a chain of narrators so numerous that collusion on falsehood is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, how did the Companions understand the meanings of shar'i terms without explicit knowledge of coinage?
A) Through direct revelation from Allah.
B) Through prophetic explanation and contextual usage, similar to how children learn language.
C) Through a formal declaration by the Prophet at the beginning of his mission.
D) Through consensus (ijma) reached after the Prophet's death.

Key Takeaway
Companions understood shar'i terms through prophetic context and usage; their understanding was transmitted mutawatir in meaning, even if not in wording.

Answering the Arabicness Objection: Quran and Shar'i Terms
Refutation of the Arabicness objection using mu'rab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi response to the objection that haqiqah shar'iyyah would make the Quran non-Arabic, using the concept of mu'rab.

Core Idea
Shar'i terms in the Quran do not compromise its Arabicness; the question is answered in the issue of mu'rab.

A powerful objection against haqiqah shar'iyyah is that if shar'i terms were new coinages, they would be non-Arabic, contradicting the Quran's declaration of Arabicness. This lesson dissects that objection, the Hanafi reliance on the issue of mu'rab (words of foreign origin that have been Arabized), and how this preserves both the Quran's Arabic nature and the validity of shar'i haqiqah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection from Quran 12:2
Deniers argue that if shar'i terms like 'salat' are new legal coinages (not original Arabic), then the Quran containing them would be non-Arabic, contradicting the verse 'Indeed, We have sent it down as an Arabic Qur'an.'
2
Understand the mu'rab (Arabized words) concept
Hanafis respond that shar'i terms are not non-Arabic; they are Arabic in form, even if the Lawgiver assigned new meanings. The issue is similar to mu'rab—words of foreign origin that have been fully adopted into Arabic and are considered Arabic once modified.
The text says 'the answer has already been given in the issue of mu'rab'.
3
Apply the Hanafi response to the objection
The presence of shar'i terms in the Quran does not make it non-Arabic, because these terms are Arabic words with additional legal meanings. They remain Arabic in lexicography and morphology, similar to how Arabized foreign words are accepted as Arabic.
4
Evaluate the strength of this response
The objection loses force because it conflates linguistic form with meaning. The Quran is Arabic in its words and grammar; new legal definitions do not change the Arabicness of those words.
This response is found in the author's reference to the prior discussion on mu'rab.
