And if they understood, it would have been transmitted to us by tawatur, and there is no ahad, let alone tawatur. We said: The understanding is shared between being sacred realities and being metaphors, so whatever your answer is, it is our answer, that understanding occurred through prophetic explanation, and it has been transmitted mutawatir in meaning even if not mutawatir in wording, and it may occur without explicit explanation as with children, so it is possible that understanding here occurs in this manner. And what appears from following the words of the verifiers is that there is distortion in the transmission of the proof from what it was. The gist: If the legal transmission were realized, the Companions would have first understood that these words were coined for these meanings, because understanding is a condition of obligation, and understanding of coined meanings only occurs after knowledge of the coinage. Also, they are skilled in types of indications, and if they knew that they were transferred by the Lawgiver's coinage, it would have been transmitted to us by tawatur as in the coinages of languages due to the multiple incentives to transmit it, and it was not even transmitted by ahad let alone tawatur. So then the first objection is not refuted, for it is not a shared obligation, because on metaphor, understanding the meaning occurs via a qarinah, and they understood and transmitted to us, unlike [literal] transmission, for it requires knowledge of the coinage to understand the meaning. Similarly the second: The Prophet did not explain that these words are coined for these meanings, and this was not transmitted even as ahad let alone tawatur. As for the third, it has some validity, for it is possible that they knew through experience, then those after them similarly, and explicit statement is not necessary, but the matter is not hidden from the intelligent, for it was not known in this manner either, as the Revealer of Divine Secrets guided us. And one thing that alerts: If it were so, someone would have stated it even once, just as they stated some coinages of the language. Then, claiming transmission is a claim against Allah, so proof requires decisive evidence, and there is no even conjectural sign here, let alone decisive, so it is not fitting for any Muslim to be audacious towards Allah with what he does not know. As for their statement in denying legal transmission: If it were a sacred reality, it would be non-Arabic due to the lack of Arab coinage, and the Qur'an would be non-Arabic because it includes it, while Allah said: 'Indeed, We have sent it down as an Arabic Qur'an.' The answer has already been given in the issue of mu'rab.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument in the debate over whether Shar'i terms (like 'salat', 'zakat') are haqiqah shar'iyyah (sacred realities with new coinage) or remain metaphorical usages of the original linguistic meanings. The author, a Hanafi, defends the view that they are haqiqah shar'iyyah. The text begins with a refutation of an objection regarding the definition of 'tahara'. It then moves to the core argument: Deniers claim that if the Lawgiver had coined these terms, the Companions would have understood and transmitted this tawatur. The Hanafi response counters that understanding can come through prophetic context and usage, not necessarily explicit coinage. The argument also addresses the status of the mute in prayer and the role of intention. The passage then presents the deniers' final objection that if shar'i terms were haqiqah, the Quran would not be Arabic, referring to the verse 'We have sent it down as an Arabic Qur'an.' The author notes this is answered elsewhere in the issue of mu'rab (words of non-Arabic origin). The overall tone is defensive of the Hanafi position against Shafi'i and other schools that reject haqiqah shar'iyyah.

Key Terms
حقيقة شرعية (haqiqah shar'iyyah): A term that has been legally redefined by the Lawgiver (Shari') for a specific religious meaning, distinct from its original linguistic meaning.
معرب (mu'rab): A word of foreign origin that has been Arabized, i.e., adopted into the Arabic language with morphological adaptation, and is considered Arabic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the source text, how does the Hanafi school respond to the objection that haqiqah shar'iyyah would make the Quran non-Arabic?
A) They deny that shar'i terms are haqiqah shar'iyyah.
B) They argue that the Quranic verse 12:2 is not absolute.
C) They refer to the issue of mu'rab (Arabized words).
D) They claim that shar'i terms are not present in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi response to the Arabicness objection relies on the established usul principle of mu'rab: shar'i terms are Arabic in form and do not violate the Quran's Arabic nature.

Interpreting 'Inda' in Acknowledgments: The Hanafi Principle of Bara'ah
Defining the word 'inda' and its legal effect in acknowledgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the absolute use of 'inda' in an acknowledgment and explain the role of the principle of bara'at al-dhimma in that interpretation.

Core Idea
When 'inda' is used absolutely, it is a deposit, not a debt, because the default is freedom from liability.

The word 'inda' (عند) can refer to physical possession or a claim of debt. When someone says 'I have for so-and-so a thousand' without further qualification, what is the legal status of that property? In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi rule that such a phrase is interpreted as a deposit (wadi'ah) rather than a debt (dayn), and we see how the fundamental principle of bara'at al-dhimma (presumption of innocence of liability) drives this interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic scope of 'inda'
The word 'inda' can denote physical presence (e.g., 'I have a jug') or figurative presence (e.g., 'I have a debt'). Recognize this duality.
2
Recognize the ambiguity in the acknowledgment
When someone says 'I have for so-and-so a thousand' without specifying debt or deposit, the phrase is ambiguous. Determine which legal category fits the ambiguity.
3
Apply the principle of bara'at al-dhimma
The default presumption is that a person's liability is free from obligation. Therefore, the interpretation that imposes the least liability is preferred. Debt imposes liability, deposit does not (except for negligence).
This is a key usul principle: al-asl bara'at al-dhimma.
4
Derive the ruling
Since deposit (wadi'ah) does not impose liability for loss (unless negligence), it is the interpretation that aligns with bara'ah. Thus, the absolute use of 'inda' is ruled as a deposit.
5
Note the exception
If the speaker explicitly mentions 'debt' (dayn), the ruling changes. The principle applies only when the phrase is unqualified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: (عند للحضرة الحسية نحو عندي كوز (والمعنوية) نحو : عندي دين لفلان فالعندية أعم من الدين والوديعة وإنما تثبت الوديعة بإطلاقها) بأن يقول : عندي لفلان ألف، من غير تقييد بالدين أو الوديعة (لأنها) أي الوديعة (أدنى) ما تتناوله العندية (بل لأن الأصل البراءة) للذمة، وفي الحمل على الوديعة براءتها.
Question: 'When' (inda) is for physical presence like 'I have a jug', and for figurative presence like 'I have a debt for so-and-so'. So 'indiyyah' is more general than debt and deposit. The deposit is established by its absolute usage when one says 'I have for so-and-so a thousand' without specification of debt or deposit, because deposit is the lowest thing that 'indiyyah' covers, but rather because the default is freedom of liability, and interpreting it as deposit clears the liability.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a ruling. The word 'inda' (عند) can mean physical presence (e.g., 'I have a jug') or figurative presence (e.g., 'I have a debt'). When someone acknowledges 'I have for so-and-so a thousand' without specifying whether it is a debt or a deposit, the default ruling is that it is a deposit (wadi'ah). This is because the default is that the liability is free (al-asl bara'at al-dhimma), and interpreting it as a deposit clears the liability (since the depositor still owns the item), whereas a debt would impose liability. The text gives two reasons: first, deposit is the lowest level of 'indiyyah' (minimal possession), and second, the principle of Bara'at Asliyyah (original freedom from obligation).

Key Terms
عندية (indiyyah): The state of being with someone; possession in a broad sense covering both physical custody and debt.
أصل البراءة (asl al-bara'ah): The default principle that the liability is free from obligation until proven otherwise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person says, 'I have for Zayd a thousand' without specification. According to Hanafi fiqh, what is the legal interpretation?
A) It is a loan (qard) owed to Zayd.
B) It is a deposit (wadi'ah) belonging to Zayd.
C) It is a gift (hibah) from the speaker to Zayd.
D) It is a sale (bay') of an item worth a thousand.

Key Takeaway
The absolute use of 'inda' in acknowledgment is interpreted as a deposit, not a debt, due to the principle of bara'at al-dhimma.

Defining Shart: Condition as Connected Specification
Defining shart as a connected specification, with critique and analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'shart' in Hanafi usul, distinguish it from 'sabab', and evaluate the objections raised against its definition.

Core Idea
A condition is necessary but not sufficient: without it the ruling fails, but with it the ruling may still not apply.

This passage refines the definition of 'shart' (condition) as a connected specification. Imam al-Ghazali's classic definition is subjected to critical analysis, especially the distinction from 'sabab' (cause) and the handling of potential circularity. Understanding this distinction is foundational for correct legal reasoning in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Break down al-Ghazali's statement: 'Shart is that without which the conditioned does not exist, and it is not necessary that the conditioned exists with it.' The first part establishes necessity, the second part denies sufficiency.
This distinguishes shart from sabab, which requires the effect.
2
Distinguish from sabab
The phrase 'and it is not necessary that the conditioned exists with it' is added to exclude sabab. For a cause, the effect necessarily exists when the cause is present.
Sabab is both necessary and sufficient; shart is only necessary.
3
Address the circularity objection
An objector claims the definition is circular because 'mashrut' presupposes 'shart'. The response reinterprets 'mashrut' as 'the thing' (al-shay'), meaning the conditioned entity itself, not the concept. This breaks the circularity.
The definition is taken as real (haqiqi), not conceptual.
4
Clarify the scope
The definition might include material and final causes. The Hanafi clarification specifies that shart is external to the conditioned, and that final causes are included only by convention, not as essential posteriority.
Final causes are prior in intention but posterior in essence via the agent's action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثاني) من المخصصات المتصلة الشرط (قال الإمام حجة الإسلام أبو حامد الغزالي) الشرط (ما لا يوجد المشروط دونه ولا يلزم أن يوجد المشروط (عنده وأورد أولاً أنه دوري لأن المشروط لا يعلم إلا بعد العلم بالشرط (ويجاب) عنه بأن المراد بالمشروط الشيء) والحاصل ما لا يوجد الشيء بدونه ولا يلزم أن يوجد الشيء عنده وهو الظاهر وإلا لضاع قوله : و يلزم الخ . لأن ذلك لإخراج السبب، ولو كان المشروط على معناه لم يدخل من أوّل الأمر حتى يخرج فإنه ليس مما لا يوجد المشروط دونه بل يوجد المسبب دونه (قيل) إذا كان المراد به الشيء فيصدق على العلة المادية والغائية) فإنهما مما لا يوجد الشيء دونهما ولا يوجد عندهما (أقول : إلا أن يقال : المراد خارج عن الشيء (كذلك) أي لا يوجد الشيء دونه الخ . بناء على ما عرف واشتهر أن الشرط من العلل الخارجية فهذه الشهرة قرينة الإرادة (وأما الغائية فإنا نلتزم كونها شرطاً في هذا (الاصطلاح المذكور ههنا (كما قيل، أو لفاعلية الفاعل فليست موقوفاً عليها) المعلول (إلا لا يلتزم بل يقال (كما أقول هي * بالواسطة باعتبار أنه موقوف على الفاعل بما هو فاعل وفاعليته موقوفة عليها (والمتبادر من عدم الوجود دونه التأخر عنه (ذاتاً بالذات من غير واسطة فالمعنى الشرط لا يوجد الشيء دونه، أي يتأخر الشيء عنه بالذات، بخلاف العلة الغائية (فتأمل) وقد يقال : يخرج على هذا
(The second) of the connected specifications: the condition. (Imam Hujjat al-Islam Abu Hamid al-Ghazali said) The condition is that without which the conditioned does not exist, and it is not necessary that the conditioned exists (with it). First, it is objected that it is circular because the conditioned is not known except after knowledge of the condition. (And it is answered) about it that what is meant by the conditioned is the thing. The gist is: what does not exist without it, and it is not necessary that the thing exists with it. This is apparent, otherwise his statement 'and it is not necessary' etc. would be wasted, because that is to exclude the cause. If the conditioned were taken in its sense, it would not enter from the beginning to be excluded, for it is not something without which the conditioned does not exist; rather, the effect exists without it. (It is said) if what is meant is the thing, it applies to the material and final causes, for they are things without which the thing does not exist and does not exist with them. (I say: unless it is said that what is meant is external to the thing (likewise) i.e., the thing does not exist without it, etc., based on what is known and well-known that the condition is among external causes. This renown is a presumption of intent. (As for the final cause, we commit that it is a condition in this terminology mentioned here (as it was said, or due to the agency of the agent, it is not dependent on it) the effect (except that it is not committed, rather it is said (as I say it is * indirectly, considering that it is dependent on the agent in that it is an agent, and its agency is dependent on it. (And what is understood from 'non-existence without it' is posteriority to it (essentially) without intermediary. So the meaning is: the condition is that the thing does not exist without it, i.e., the thing is posterior to it essentially, unlike the final cause. (So reflect) And it is said: this includes...
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage introduces the second type of connected specification in usul al-fiqh: the condition (shart). The author quotes Imam al-Ghazali's definition: a condition is that without which the conditioned (mashrut) does not exist, but the conditioned does not necessarily exist when the condition exists. The definition is analyzed critically. First, a circularity objection is raised: knowledge of the conditioned requires prior knowledge of the condition. The response clarifies that 'conditioned' here refers to the thing itself, not the knowledge of it. The phrase 'it is not necessary that the conditioned exists with it' is to distinguish the condition from the cause (sabab), where the effect necessarily accompanies the cause. Then the definition is tested against material and final causes, which also fit, but the author specifies that the intention is external causes, as is well-known. The final cause is included as a condition in this terminology, though some object that the effect is not dependent on it directly but through the agent. The Hanafi stance is that the condition implies essential posteriority: the conditioned is later in essence, unlike the final cause which is prior in intention. This technical definition helps differentiate between shart, sabab, and illa in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): That without which the conditioned does not exist, but not necessarily exists when the condition exists.
مشروط (mashrut): The conditioned entity or ruling that depends on the condition.
سبب (sabab): A cause that necessitates its effect; here distinguished from shart.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does al-Ghazali add 'wa la yalzam an yujad al-mashrut 'indahu' to the definition of shart?
A) To include material causes
B) To exclude the cause (sabab)
C) To prevent circularity
D) To include final causes

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of shart excludes causation: a condition is necessary but not sufficient, and it must be external to the conditioned.

Condition vs. Cause: Definition and Multiplicity in Hanafi Usul
Defining shart and sabab with multiplicity rule and rationale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define and distinguish between shart and sabab according to Hanafi usul, and explain why a condition is a common factor while causes are multiple and individually sufficient.

Core Idea
The condition is the common factor without which the conditioned cannot exist; causes are individually sufficient and act like doers.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut tackles a key distinction in Hanafi usul: why can a single conditioned thing (e.g., ownership) have multiple apparent conditions but only one true condition? And why can multiple causes each independently bring about the same effect? The author defines shart and sabab, addresses the multiplicity rule, and provides a rational justification that causes are analogous to doers. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying usul principles to furu' cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Locate the terminological definitions of shart and sabab in the passage. Note the key phrases: for shart, 'adam al-wujud bi-dunihi' (non-existence without it); for sabab, 'istitba' al-wujud' (entailment of existence).
The passage also clarifies that shart is the common factor (al-qadr al-mushtarak) when multiple conditions appear.
2
Understand the multiplicity rule
The author states that shart cannot be truly multiple because the conditioned would then exist without each individual condition. Instead, multiplicity is only apparent, and the true condition is the shared element. For sabab, multiplicity is genuine: each cause is a distinct alternative that brings about the effect.
Example: ownership can arise from sale, gift, inheritance — each is a separate sabab. But the condition for validity of a sale, e.g., offer and acceptance, is a single condition under the definition.
3
Grasp the inner reason (al-sirr)
The text offers a rationale based on causality: causes are like doers (asbab bi-manzilat al-fawa'il). A single effect cannot have multiple efficient causes, so causes cannot be a common factor. Conditions are merely prerequisites and can be common.
The author then critiques this rationale, noting it applies only to real efficient causes, not conventional ones.
4
Recognize the author's preferred justification
After refuting the causal analogy, the author settles on a simpler point: the generally one (al-wahid bi'l-umum) cannot be the doer of the particular. This avoids the theological complexity.
This leads to a discussion of essence and particularization, which is beyond the core lesson.
5
Apply to a fiqh example
Use the example of ownership: multiple causes (sale, gift, inheritance) each individually confer ownership, so they are multiple sabab. But the condition for ownership in a gift is possession (qabd), which is a single shart. If possession is missing, gift does not confer ownership — but sale does not require possession.
This illustrates that shart is tied to a specific cause, while multiple causes are independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دون البيع فإنه يفيد الملك بنفس العقد دون الهبة (فلو قطع النظر عن خصوص السبب) ويؤخذ شرط للملك مثلاً (خرج ذلك الحد) لأنه يوجد للمشروط دونه بل لا يصدق الحد أنه عن على شرط أصلاً، فإن نوع الشرطية لم يأب عن وجود المشروط دونه (إلا أن يقال) ليس المحدود الشرط العام بل المحدود شرط الشيء (مطلقاً أي من كل وجه مع كل سبب (وهذا) المذكور (شرط من وجه دون وجه فتدبر) والحق في الجواب أن كون القبض شرط الملك ممنوع، وإنما هو شرط لحصة الملك الحاصلة من الهبة ولا يلزم من اشتراط الخاص بشيء اشتراط المطلق به، بل هو شرط إيجاب الهبة الملك وقبله لم يوجد السبب تاماً، كما يفصح عنه عبارات الفقهاء فتدبر فإن قلت ما وجه قولهم : الشرط لا يتعدد) بدلاً بأن يكون لمشروط واحد شروط متعددة يوجد هذا المشروط تارة مع هذا الشرط وتارة مع آخر (والسبب يتعدد) على هذا النمط، فإن الملك يحدث بأسباب شتى، ولم يرد أن الشرط لا يتعدد أصلاً حتى يرد فيه) أي في اعتبار القدر المشترك في الشروط دون الأسباب ما تقرر) في العلوم العقلية أن فاعل الواحد بالعدد لا بد أن يكون واحداً بالعدد إذ لولاه لجاز فاعلية الواحد بالشخص للواحد بالعموم (إذ العقل ينقبض عن أن يكون تحصيل الفاعل دون تحصيل (معلوله والأسباب بمنزلة الفواعل فلا يجوز أن تكون قدراً مشتركاً، وإلا لكان الواحد بالعموم الأضعف سبباً وفاعلاً للواحد بالعدد الأقوى بخلاف الشرط) فإنه لا انقباض عن كون الأقوى تحصيلاً متوقفاً على الأضعف فيه لعدم كونه متحصلاً بخلاف الفاعل هذا، ويرد عليه أن امتناع كون تحصيل الفاعل أضعف إنما في الفاعل الحقيقي المؤثر دون المؤثر الجعلي فلا يتم هذا السر والأولى الاكتفاء بما سبق (أقول خلاصة ذلك) أن الواحد بالعموم لا يكون فاعلاً للمتشخص (منقوض باقتضاء الماهية فرداً معيناً كالواجب عند المتكلمين الذاهبين إلى زيادة التشخص فإنه متعين بنفسه ومعلول للماهية الواجبة (والعقل) على رأي الفلاسفة الذاهبين إلى انحصار نوعه في شخصه لاقتضائه التشخص بنفسه (فتأمل) بل نقول اقتضاء الماهية التشخص غير معقول، لأن نسبتها إلى الأشخاص على السواء، والمقتضى لا يكون متساوي النسبة إلى المعلول وغيره وأيضاً جاعل الوجود والتشخص واحد بل التشخص نحو الوجود على التحقيق، فلو اقتضى الماهية التشخص لاقتضت الوجود، فيوجد قبل الوجود، وبهذا أبطل الفلاسفة زيادة الوجود والتعين عليه سبحانه ونسبة علية الماهية للعقل التعين لا يقبلها النقاد من المهرة والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال
Except for sale, for it confers ownership by the contract itself, unlike gift. (So if one disregards the specific cause) and takes possession as a condition for ownership, for example, (that definition is invalid) because the conditioned exists without it; indeed the definition does not apply that it is a condition at all, for the nature of conditionality does not preclude the existence of the conditioned without it. (Unless it is said) that what is defined is not the general condition, but rather the condition of a thing (absolutely i.e., in every respect with every cause. And this) mentioned (is a condition in some respects but not others, so reflect.) The correct answer is that possession being a condition for ownership is denied; rather it is a condition for the portion of ownership resulting from gift, and the specific conditioning of something does not necessitate the absolute conditioning of it; rather it is a condition for the gift to confer ownership, and before it the cause is not complete, as is clarified by the expressions of the jurists, so reflect. If you say, 'What is the basis of their statement: the condition is not multiplied, meaning that a single conditioned cannot have multiple conditions such that it exists sometimes with this condition and sometimes with another, while the cause is multiplied in this manner, for ownership arises from various causes; and it is not intended that the condition is not multiplied at all, such that the objection would arise that multiplicity of conditions is obvious, and no one said that condition is not multiplied?' I say: What is considered in the conceptual definition of condition terminologically is non-existence without it; thus the aforementioned multiplicity is impossible, otherwise the conditioned would exist without each. (So in the case of multiplicity) apparently, (the condition is the common factor among the multiple conditions. And what is considered) in the conceptual definition of cause is the entailment of existence, and each specific one of the multiple causes alternatively (is likewise) i.e., entails the existence of the effect, like injury on a fast and zihar both lead to the obligation of expiation. (And the inner reason) for considering the common factor in conditions but not in causes is what is established in the rational sciences: that the doer of a single thing by number must be one by number, otherwise it would be possible for one person to do the generally one thing. (For the intellect recoils from the attainment of the doer being weaker than the attainment of its effect. And causes are like doers, so they cannot be a common factor, otherwise the generally weaker one would be a cause and doer of the numerically stronger one, unlike the condition) for there is no aversion to the stronger being dependent in attainment on the weaker, since it is not the one attaining, unlike the doer. This is refuted by the fact that the impossibility of the doer's attainment being weaker applies only to the real efficient doer, not the conventional doer, so this inner reason is invalid; it is better to suffice with what preceded. (I say: the summary of that) is that the generally one cannot be a doer for the particularized (object). This is refuted by the essence requiring a specific individual, like the Necessary Being according to the theologians who hold to the addition of particularization, for it is necessary by itself and caused by the necessary essence. (And the intellect) according to the philosophers who hold that its kind is confined to its individual, because its essence requires particularization by itself. (So reflect.) Rather we say: essence requiring particularization is unintelligible, because its relation to individuals is equal, and the requirer cannot be equally related to the effect and others. Also, the maker of existence and particularization is one; indeed particularization is a mode of existence in reality. So if essence required particularization, it would require existence, and thus it would exist before existence. With this the philosophers invalidated the addition of existence and determination to Him, and the attribution of causality of essence to the intellect's determination is not accepted by the expert critics. And Allah knows the truth of the matter.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi usul discussion on the definitions of 'shart' (condition) and 'sabab' (cause). The author addresses an objection that if conditions could be multiple, then a conditioned thing could exist without any single condition. He clarifies that the condition is the common factor among multiple conditions, while each specific cause entails its effect. The rationale for this distinction is that causes act like doers, and a single effect cannot have multiple efficient causes; conditions are merely prerequisites. The author critiques this rationale, arguing that it only applies to real efficient causes, not conventional ones. He then summarizes that the universal cannot be the doer of the particular, and engages with theological and philosophical arguments about essence and particularization. The key takeaway is that in Hanafi usul, conditions are defined by the impossibility of the conditioned without them, and they can be multiple only as a common factor, while causes are individually sufficient.

Key Terms
الشَّرْط (al-shart): ما يتوقف عليه المشروط بحيث لا يوجد المشروط بدونه؛ وهو القدر المشترك بين الشروط المتعددة
السَّبَب (al-sabab): ما يستتبع وجود المعلول، وكل سبب معين بديل عن غيره، ولا يكون قدراً مشتركاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this passage, what is the 'condition' when multiple conditions appear to exist for the same conditioned thing?
A) Each of the multiple conditions is a separate condition.
B) The condition is the common factor shared by all the apparent conditions.
C) There is no condition at all.
D) The condition is the cause.

Key Takeaway
The condition is the common factor without which the conditioned cannot exist; each cause individually entails its effect, like a doer.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
10 min
Refining the Definition of Shart: The Muqtada vs Muaththir Distinction
Critiquing and refining the Minhaj definition of shart, with Hanafi response to eternal attributes objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between muqtada and muaththir to resolve objections against the definition of shart, and explain why eternal attributes can be shurut without requiring an efficient cause.

Core Idea
A condition is distinct from the cause; eternal attributes are conditions because they depend on the essence as muqtada, not muaththir.

This passage critically refines the definition of 'shart' (condition) from al-Minhaj, defending it against a powerful objection from kalam: how can eternal attributes like life be conditions if they require no cause? The Hanafi answer introduces a crucial usul distinction between muqtada (that which gives existence, whether by will or not) and muaththir (the efficient cause with will). Understanding this distinction allows us to see that conditions can be rational relations rather than temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Minhaj definition and its key elements
Locate the definition: 'al-shart: ma yatawaqqaf alayhi ta'thir al-mu'aththir, 'aqliyyan kan aw ja'liyyan.' Note that it excludes the cause itself and any part of the cause. Also note the unstated restriction 'la wujudahu' (not its existence).
The definition aims to cover both rational and conventional conditions.
2
Understand the objection from eternal attributes
The objection: Life is a condition for knowledge in Allah, but life is eternal and has no efficient cause (since need for cause arises from origination, according to the majority of theologians). So how can it be a shart?
The objector assumes that shart must involve some change or origination.
3
Trace the Hanafi response: muqtada vs muaththir
The Hanafi response distinguishes: the muaththir is the giver of existence by will and choice; the muqtada is the giver of existence absolutely. Eternal attributes need a muqtada (the essence), but not a muaththir. The definition of shart only requires dependence on the effect of the muqtada, not necessarily on an efficient cause.
Thus, life is a condition because knowledge depends on life as a muqtada, even though life itself is eternal.
4
Apply the distinction to resolve the objection
Show that the attributes are possible (mumkin) and thus need a cause (the essence) for their existence—but that cause is a muqtada, not a muaththir. Therefore, the door of proving Allah through signs (istiqlal) remains open, since the need for a muqtada is sufficient.
The Hanafi position removes the specter of multiple necessary beings or complete independence.

(And it is said) in al-Minhaj: (Condition: that upon which the effect of the effective cause depends, whether rational or conventional; thus it is not objected that legal causes have no effect to depend on conditions. And it is understood from it that the essence of the effective cause does not depend on it, so the part comes out absolutely. 353 The cause comes out, so the refutation by it is not met. And in al-Minhaj there is an additional restriction which he did not transmit: 'and not its existence.' Then there is no need for this understanding, for the part of the cause depends on its existence, so it comes out by it; except that regarding the carried part there is room for reflection. (It is said) in Sharh al-Sharh: but it is problematic for the cause itself, for it applies to it that the effect of the effective cause depends on it (necessarily, because the effect of something depends on the realization of its essence. And what is in some commentaries of al-Minhaj is that it comes out by the last restriction, for the existence of the cause depends on its essence because it is an additional attribute, so it comes out by his saying: 'not its existence.' Its falsity is obvious, for existence, though additional, does not depend on the existing essence, so reflect. And it is repelled by the fact that what is understood from the definition is that it (i.e., what the effect depends on) is distinct from the effective cause, so the cause comes out. Then the opposite is objected: life in eternal knowledge, for it is a condition for the existence of knowledge in Him, exalted, and His attribute of it, and there is no effect of the effective cause in it, for the need for the effective cause is origination according to the majority of theologians, and His knowledge is eternal. This does not apply to those who make the cause of need possibility, as do the verifying theologians, among whom is the definer by this definition. It is said: If this (i.e., origination as the need) were correct, then the attributes of the Necessary Being (may His glory be exalted) (which are additional and eternal) would be independent of the effective cause, even of the essence described by them, since there is no origination, so no need. This would entail either that they are necessarily existent if existence is necessary for them in themselves, thus multiple necessary beings by essence (Allah forbid), or that they are possible and independent of the effective cause if they are possible existence. Then the door of proving Allah (i.e., knowledge of Him) would be closed, for its basis is the need of the possible for the effective cause, and you have permitted its existence without an effective cause. (I say: First, the existence of an attribute is its existence for its subject, as Ibn Sina stated). So the existence of His attributes, exalted, is subsisting by His essence, not by themselves, so the necessity of multiple independent existences does not follow. (But the impossible is that). This expression implies the permissibility of two non-independent necessary beings; no Muslim, nor even a rational person, would dare to utter it, let alone permit it. Moreover, the attributes are certainly eternal. Since the cause of need is origination according to them, there is no need. So either they are possible, then there would be an equal relation to existence and non-existence without a preponderant; or they are necessary, and necessity is incompatible with need in existence, so they are independent of the essence, thus independent, leading to what he admitted is impossible, and also contrary to the assumption that they are attributes. The author's statement can be explained: the attributes' existence is not existence in themselves but for their subjects; they are necessary in relation to the essence, and whatever is necessary by something is necessary by it, not by itself, so no necessity by itself and no independence. The impossible is the second, which entails independence. He expressed 'necessary existence of things independent of the Necessary Being by essence' as an expression of the thing by its necessary consequence. So this is either a rebuttal or a summary refutation, but the source of doubt is not cut off by them, so he appended the second containing the solution. This is the ultimate explanation of his statement. (And I say: Secondly, they are possible and independent of the effective cause, but they are in need of the requirer (muqtada). And the closure of the door of knowing the Maker through signs would only follow if they were independent of the requirer absolutely. And 'effective cause' (muaththir) according to them is more specific, for that which gives existence is called 'muqtada'; if it gives by will and choice it is called 'muaththir'. So the attributes are possible and in need in their existences of the essence described by them, but
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition of 'shart' from al-Minhaj. The author first presents the definition: condition is that upon which the effect of the efficient cause depends, whether rationally or conventionally. This excludes the cause itself and a part of the cause. He then discusses an additional restriction 'not its existence' and evaluates objections. The main objection is about eternal attributes like life being a condition for knowledge in Allah, which seems to require a condition without an efficient cause. The author responds by distinguishing between 'muqtada' (requirer) and 'muaththir' (efficient cause), and by explaining that attributes are possible and need the essence, not an external efficient cause. The discussion integrates rational theology (kalam) with usul definitions, showing how Hanafi usul handles metaphysical objections. The key lesson is that a condition is distinct from the cause and can apply even to eternal things when understood correctly.

Key Terms
المُؤَثِّر (al-mu'aththir): المفيد للوجود بالإرادة والاختيار، وهو أخص من المقتضى
المُقْتَضَى (al-muqtada): ما يفيد الوجود مطلقاً، سواء بإرادة أو بدونها

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because life is a created attribute.

Key Takeaway
The definition of shart is refined by the muqtada/muaththir distinction to include eternal rational conditions, preserving its universality in usul.
